  - Python-Powered Excel C HAPTER 6 Data Manipulation with Pandas Introduction In this chapter, we will focus on data manipulation using the mighty pandas library of Python. It is designed for data analysis, offering functionalities similar to Excel but with greater efficiency and scalability. You will learn to import data and perform operations such as handling missing values, filtering, aggregating, pivoting, or other complex transformations. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of how to clean, transform, and analyze data using pandas, preparing you for more advanced Excel-Python integration with libraries like openpyxl and xlwings. Structure In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics: Introducing the mighty pandas library DataFrames and Series Data import and export Datatypes in pandas Filtering, selecting, and querying data Data cleaning and handling missing values Column calculations and quick statistics Advanced data transformation Grouping and pivoting data Combining data sets Introducing the mighty pandas library Python’s pandas library is designed to make your workflow faster, scalable, automation-friendly, even for messy or large datasets. This library has the broader goal of becoming the most powerful and flexible open-source data analysis or manipulation tool available in any language. Unlike Excel, where copying formulas or updating pivot tables can become tedious and error-prone, pandas workflows are scripted once and reused endlessly , ensuring both consistency and speed. For advanced users relying on Power Query , Power Pivot , or even VBA , pandas offers a unified environment where importing data, merging files, cleaning columns, and summarizing insights can all be done in one place. Even common Excel pain points, like dealing with slow workbooks, broken formulas, or lookup errors across multiple files, are easily addressed in pandas through intuitive, scalable code. Let us start looking into a concise preview of what we can do with pandas , each topic is explained in detail in the following sections. If you are using Anaconda, pandas is already included. You do not need to install it again. If you have created a conda environment for the book (as explained in Chapter 2 , Setting Up Python) , pandas is installed there already. For plain Python users ( python.org ), If pandas is not installed, install it by running the following command in Anaconda Prompt or Terminal: pip&#160;install&#160;pandas==3.0.0 Once you have the library installed, you can import it using alias pd (refer to Chapter 4 , External Packages and Date-time Handling ). If you are unsure whether pandas is already installed, you can try importing it before installing anything. If it runs without error, pandas is already available and no installation is required. import &#160;pandas&#160; as &#160;pd print(pd.__version__) &#160; Expected output : 3.0.0 This library gives you the ability to import data files from various sources and different formats. For now, use read_excel() to read an excel file employees.xlsx containing employee records. Data files are available in Data folder of the GitHub repo. To import data sets from there, you can use os.path.join() method (recommended) or hardcode file path as discussed in Chapter 5 , Automating File Management with Python : import &#160;pandas&#160; as &#160;pd import &#160;os DATA_PATH&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(os.getcwd(),&#160;&apos;Data&apos;) data &#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel(os.path.join(DATA_PATH,&#160; &apos;employees.xlsx&apos;)) print(data ) This will display data in Excel-like table: &#160;&#160;&#160; emp_id &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; name &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Chicago &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; No In Colab or Jupyter, DataFrame will appear as a nicely formatted HTML table. If you are working in Spyder, you can double click on data from Variable Explorer to open an editor which allows you to format and edit data (you can explore but do not save changes, if any). This is a toy data set, so you can print or view all the rows. If your data set is too big to print, you can have a quick glance at it using data.head() (prints the first five rows by default) or data.tail() (prints the last five rows by default): print(data.head()) The expected output is: &#160;&#160; emp_id &#160;&#160;&#160; name &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; job_title &#160;&#160;&#160; dept &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; sal &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; loc &#160; Yes 3 &#160;&#160;&#160; E004 &#160;&#160; David &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; DevOps &#160; Engineer &#160;&#160;&#160; Engg &#160;&#160; 91000 &#160;&#160;&#160; Austin &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; No 4 &#160;&#160;&#160; E005 &#160;&#160; Susan &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Account &#160; Manager &#160;&#160; Sales &#160;&#160; 80000 &#160;&#160;&#160; Boston &#160;&#160;&#160; Yes To print last 2 rows only, you can run data.tail(2) and it will show: &#160; &#160;&#160; emp_id &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; name &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; job_title &#160;&#160;&#160; dept &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; sal &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; loc &#160; is_churn 18 &#160;&#160;&#160; E019 &#160;&#160;&#160; Sophia &#160;&#160; Sales &#160; Executive &#160;&#160; Sales &#160;&#160; 71000 &#160;&#160;&#160; Boston &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Yes 19 &#160;&#160;&#160; E020 &#160;&#160; Michael &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; HR &#160; Manager &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; HR &#160;&#160; 87000 &#160;&#160; Chicago &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; No A simple data.shape command reveals the number of rows and columns (20, 7) and data.columns will fetch you all column names, which is especially useful when you have too many columns. Moreover, quick information about the size of data, column name, column types, missing values, etc., can be fetched by a simple command data.info() , which shows the following output: &lt;class &#160; &apos;pandas.DataFrame&apos;&gt; RangeIndex:&#160;20 &#160;entries,&#160; 0 &#160;to&#160; 19 Data&#160;columns&#160;(total&#160;7 &#160;columns): &#160; #&#160;&#160;&#160;Column&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;Non-Null&#160;Count&#160;&#160;Dtype ---&#160;&#160;------&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;--------------&#160;&#160;----- &#160; 0 &#160;&#160;&#160;emp_id&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 20 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; str &#160;&#160; &#160; 1 &#160;&#160;&#160;name&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 20 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; str &#160;&#160; &#160; 2 &#160;&#160;&#160;job_title&#160;&#160; 20 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; str &#160;&#160; &#160; 3 &#160;&#160;&#160;dept&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 20 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; str &#160;&#160; &#160; 4 &#160;&#160;&#160;sal&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 20 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;int64 &#160; 5 &#160;&#160;&#160;loc&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 20 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; str &#160;&#160; &#160; 6 &#160;&#160;&#160;left&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 20 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; str &#160;&#160; dtypes:&#160;int64(1),&#160;str(6) memory&#160;usage:&#160;1.2 &#160;KB It tells us that data is basically a pandas DataFrame (this is how pandas store tables). 20 entries means that there are twenty rows, indexed as 0 to 19 (as the index starts from 0), and a total of seven columns. For each column, it tells us the index (starting from 0), column name, number of non-null or non-missing values, and datatype of that column. In this data set, there are no missing or null values, as we can see (all 20 values are non-null for each column). The column sal is a numerical column, while the rest are str (string) datatypes. We will discuss more about DataFrames and datatypes in the next section. For now, let us do some quick analysis of this data. The function, data.describe() provides a summary of all numerical columns by default. You can customize it to include or exclude specific columns, such as non -numeric columns, date columns, or columns by name. As you can guess, data[&apos;dept&apos;] will fetch you dept column of the data, allowing you to perform operations on it. If you are curious to see how many unique values there are in this column and names of different departments in the data , you can chain nunique() and unique() methods to this column shown as follows: data[ &apos;dept&apos;].nunique()&#160; #&#160;4 _ Counts unique values data[&apos;dept&apos;].unique()&#160; #&#160;[&apos;Sales&apos;, &apos;Engg&apos;, &apos;HR&apos;, &apos;Mktg&apos;] To get a count of employees in each department, you can use value_counts() function, and with an additional argument, it can also generate percentages without needing to manually write the formula: data[&apos;dept&apos;].value_counts() It shows: dept Engg &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 7 Sales &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 5 HR &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 4 Mktg &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 4 Name:&#160;count,&#160;dtype:&#160;int64 To get the relative frequency (proportion) of each department: data [&apos;dept&apos;].value_counts(normalize&#160;=&#160;True) # Percentage Table The output tells us that most employees, 35%, are from the engineering department: dept Engg &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 0.35 Sales &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 0.25 HR &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 0.20 Mktg &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 0.20 Name:&#160; proportion,&#160;dtype:&#160;float64 The column left indicates if an employee has left the job (yes or no). If you want to get pivot-style count table of how many employees have left from each department, use a simple function: pd.crosstab(data[“dept”],&#160;data[“left”]) To add a column and row of totals, margin argument can be used: pd.crosstab(data[&quot;dept&quot;],&#160;data[&quot;left&quot;],&#160;margins=True) The output is: left &#160;&#160;&#160;No&#160;&#160;Yes&#160;&#160; All dept&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Engg&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;6 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 7 HR&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;2 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 2 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 4 Mktg&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;2 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 2 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 4 Sales&#160;&#160;&#160;1 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 4 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 5 All &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 11 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 9 &#160;&#160;&#160; 20 Also, to show values as a percentage of row, column, or overall total, the normalize argument can be used with or without using the margin argument. You can chain together more methods, such as multiply(100) or round(2) , to display the numbers conveniently: pd.crosstab(data[&quot;dept&quot;],&#160;data[&quot;left&quot;],&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;normalize= &apos;columns&apos; ).round(2)&#160;&#160; #&#160;%&#160;w.r.t.&#160;‘left’ variable The output is: left &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; No &#160;&#160;&#160; Yes dept&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Engg&#160;&#160;&#160;0.55 &#160;&#160; 0.11 HR&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;0.18 &#160;&#160; 0.22 Mktg&#160;&#160;&#160;0.18 &#160;&#160; 0.22 Sales&#160;&#160;0.09 &#160;&#160; 0.44 It shows that 44% of those who left the job were from the Sales department. To find percentages with respect to index or department, we can use normalize=&apos;index&apos; : pd.crosstab(data[&quot;dept&quot;],&#160;data[ &quot;left&quot;],&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;normalize= &apos;index&apos;).multiply(100).round(2) # % by ‘dept’ variable The output is: left &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; No &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Yes dept&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Engg&#160;&#160;&#160;85.71 &#160;&#160; 14.29 HR&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;50.00 &#160;&#160; 50.00 Mktg&#160;&#160;&#160;50.00 &#160;&#160; 50.00 Sales&#160;&#160;20.00 &#160;&#160; 80.00 It shows, for example, that in the sales department, 80% of employees have left. So far, we have created simple pivot tables 1 . If you are looking at department-wise mean salary, you can use groupby() : data.groupby (&apos;dept&apos;)[&apos;sal&apos;].mean().astype(int) The output is: dept Engg&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;94857 HR&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;75500 Mktg&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;77000 Sales&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;75600 Name:&#160;sal,&#160;dtype:&#160;int32 Here, astype(int) has been chained to convert numbers into integers, or you can choose to round them. To calculate multiple statistics, such as mean, minimum, and maximum, for each department, you can use agg along with groupby() . (refer to the code file). If employee names are to be cleaned for lower case and removing white spaces, you can chain together string methods such as str.lower() and str.strip() . Let us look at the first three entries after cleaning using head(3) : data[&quot;name&quot;].str.lower().str.strip().head (3) The output is: 0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;alice 1 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;mark 2 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;linda Name:&#160;name,&#160;dtype:&#160;object You might be wondering about writing .str before using string methods, it is needed as the methods like .lower() , .strip() , or others work on strings, but data[&apos;name&apos;] is not a string itself, it is a column in the dataset. We call it pandas Series. The .str accessor allows us to apply string methods elementwise to each value in the column. pandas is not just for data manipulation, it can plot charts too! By chaining .plot() to your data or table, you can quickly visualize trends without needing extra libraries. You will explore this in detail later, but even now, see how effortlessly it integrates visuals: pd.crosstab(data[&apos;dept&apos;],&#160;data[&apos;left&apos;]).plot(kind= &apos;bar&apos;) # Refer figure 6.1a data.boxplot(column=&apos;sal&apos;,&#160;by=&apos;dept&apos;) # Refer figure 6.1b Chart title, subtitle, grid lines, size, and colour can all be customized easily, for now the charts created by the above-mentioned code are as shown in Figure 6.1 : Figure 6.1 : (a) Grouped bar chart, (b) Side-by-side Box plot In this section, you have also seen method chaining, i.e., calling multiple methods in sequence without creating temporary variables. This streamlines code, making it more efficient and readable. This gives you a glimpse of the wide range of possibilities with pandas . Before diving deep into the library for analytics, let us first formally introduce DataFrames and Series. DataFrames and Series You might have noticed that DataFrames allow you to see the data the way you see it in Excel, letting you explore and analyze it much like in Excel. Dataframes are 2-dimensional arrays, which means that it has rows and columns where each column can be of a different datatype. We have already worked with 2-dimensional data in the form of a list of lists, a dictionary of lists, or a list of dictionaries, etc., and you will agree that pandas DataFrames make working with such data extremely convenient and efficient. You can convert nested data structures into a DataFrame by using pd.DataFrame() : users &#160;=&#160;[[ &apos;Excel&apos;,&#160;10],&#160;[&apos;R&apos;,&#160;15],&#160;[&apos;Python&apos;,&#160;14]] # list of lists df &#160;=&#160;pd.DataFrame( users ,&#160;columns=[&apos;Tool&apos;,&#160;&apos;User_Count&apos;])&#160; # DataFrame print(df) Here, columns argument specifies the labels for the DataFrame’s columns. The output is: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Tool&#160;&#160;User_Count 0&#160;&#160;&#160;Excel&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;10 1&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;R&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;15 2&#160;&#160;Python&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;14 You might sometimes get JSON data from a web API, which is usually a list of dictionaries such as: users &#160;=&#160;[{ &apos;Tool&apos;:&#160;&apos;Excel&apos;,&#160;&apos;User_Count&apos; :&#160;10},&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;{ &apos;Tool&apos;:&#160;&apos;R&apos;,&#160;&apos;User_Count&apos;:&#160;15}, {&apos;Tool&apos;:&#160;&apos;Python&apos;,&#160;&apos;User_Count&apos;:&#160;14}] Or if a certain function results in a dictionary of lists, such as: users &#160;=&#160;{ &apos;Tool&apos;:&#160;[&apos;Excel&apos; ,&#160;&apos;R&apos;,&#160;&apos;Python&apos;], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;User_count&apos;:&#160;[10,&#160;15,&#160;14]} You can easily convert these dictionary-based data structures into a DataFrame: df&#160;=&#160;pd.DataFrame(users) When you have data for different columns in separate lists, you can use list-zip combination as mentioned: tool &#160;=&#160;[ &apos;Excel&apos;,&#160;&apos;R&apos; ,&#160;&apos;Python&apos;] user_count &#160;=&#160;[ 10,&#160;15,&#160;14] df &#160;=&#160;pd.DataFrame(list(zip(tool,&#160;user_count)),&#160;columns= [&apos;Tool&apos;,&#160;&apos;User_Count&apos;]) When you import external data using pandas , it is usually loaded as a DataFrame. Also, you have seen in the previous section that a single column of a DataFrame works as a 1-dimensional homogeneous array, we call it a Series . Refer to Figure 6.2 : Figure 6.2 : pandas Series and DataFrame DataFrames and Series are two core data structures specific to pandas . You can create a pandas Series using any 1-d data structure, such as a list, a tuple, a dictionary, or even using range() : #&#160;From&#160;List s_list &#160;=&#160;pd.Series([ 10,&#160;20,&#160; 30]) #&#160;From&#160;Tuple s_tuple &#160;=&#160;pd.Series(( 10,&#160;20,&#160;30)) #&#160;From&#160;Dictionary&#160;(keys&#160;become&#160;index) s_dict &#160;=&#160;pd.Series({ &apos;0&apos;:&#160;10,&#160;&apos;1&apos;:&#160; 20,&#160;&apos;2&apos;:&#160;30}) #&#160;From&#160;range() s_range &#160;=&#160;pd.Series(range( 10,&#160;40,&#160;10)) Data import and export For analyzing your data, you might need to frequently import data from text, CSV, Excel, or JSON files, among other file types. In Chapter 5 , Automating File Management with Python , you have learnt about reading data from text or a CSV file using open() method with or without using the csv module. Here, you will learn to use pandas to import data from files of different types. The pandas input-output API provides a set of top-level reader functions like pandas.read_excel() that you have seen so far. Refer to the official document at https://pandas.pydata.org/docs/user_guide/io.html to check the file types it supports. In the Data folder, sample files are provided. Try reading those using pandas functions after setting the path: csv_data&#160;=&#160;pd.read_csv(&apos;employees.csv&apos;) json_data&#160;=&#160;pd.read_json(&apos;employees.json&apos;) html_data = pd.read_html(&apos;employees.html&apos;) Note that read_html() always returns a list even if there is only one table, using index [0] extracts it as a DataFrame. Each of these functions has many useful arguments to control how you want to read your data. Let us discuss read_csv() and read_excel() in detail. To read text files with tab, semicolon, or any other separator, you can use the sep argument. Excel might not auto-parse exotic delimiters, such as caret ( ^ ), double colon ( :: ), tilde ( ~ ), or custom strings (such as, @@@ or ### ), which are used when other delimiters conflict or content has standard punctuation inside. Excel users might need to import manually or with Power Query. However, Python’s pd.read_csv() offers an elegant and unified way to read all text-based delimited files, you just need to pass the right sep (separator) argument. It is always recommended to inspect your data once to ensure that it is loaded correctly. Spyder’s Variable Explorer helps with that. Read sample data files yourself to verify: df_sc &#160;=&#160;pd.read_csv( &quot;semicolon_sep.csv&quot;,&#160;sep=&apos;;&apos;); &#160; df_sc.shape &#160;#(20,&#160;7) df_t &#160;=&#160;pd.read_csv( &quot;tab_sep.tsv&quot;,&#160;sep=&quot;\t&quot;);&#160;df_t.shape &#160; #&#160;(20,&#160;7) df_c = pd.read_csv( &quot;caret_sep.txt&quot;, sep=&apos;^&apos;); df_c.shape #(20, 7) You can also choose to read only specific columns of your data using usecols argument and read fewer rows using nrow argument, which is perfect for quickly previewing large files or working with just the needed data. It saves memory and reduces processing time, unlike Excel, which loads everything by default unless you perform extra steps in Power Query or VBA: df_sample &#160;=&#160;pd.read_csv( &apos;employees.csv&apos;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; nrows=10, usecols =[0,1,4]);&#160;df_sample.shape # (10, 3) If your file contains metadata, extra headers, notes, or inconsistent formatting at the top of a file, before the actual data starts, the skiprows argument comes in handy. It allows you to specify line numbers (0-indexed) or the number of lines to skip (int) at the start of the file. With pd.read_csv() , we can also load data directly from a URL, if the link points directly to a raw CSV file (i.e., it ends with .csv and does not require login or redirects): url&#160;= &#160; &apos;https://archive.ics.uci.edu/ml/machine-learning-databases/iris/iris.data&apos; df&#160;= &#160;pd.read_csv(url) We can use the index_col argument in the read_csv() function to set a column as the index column. Alternatively, after loading the data, we can also choose to use a column as the index (row labels) by using the set_index() method and reset it again using the reset_index() method: df = pd.read_csv(&apos;employees.csv&apos;) # Using&#160;&apos;emp_id&apos;&#160;as&#160;the&#160;row-index&#160; df_indexed&#160;= &#160;pd.read_csv(url,&#160;index_col =&apos;emp_id&apos;) #&#160;Alternately,&#160;set&#160;&apos;emp_id&apos;&#160;as&#160;the&#160;new&#160;index&#160;after&#160;reading&#160;the&#160;data df_indexed&#160;= &#160;df.set_index( &apos;emp_id&apos;) df_indexed.head(2) The output shows that emp_id column is now treated as row-labels: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160; name&#160;&#160; job_title&#160;&#160;&#160; dept&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;sal&#160;&#160; loc&#160; left emp_id&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; E001&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;Alice&#160;Sales&#160;Executive&#160;&#160; Sales&#160;&#160;72000 &#160;&#160;&#160;NY&#160;&#160;&#160; No E002&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;Mark&#160;&#160;Software&#160;Engineer&#160;&#160;&#160;Engg&#160;&#160;95000 &#160;&#160;SFO&#160;&#160;&#160; No #&#160;Reset&#160;index&#160;(bring&#160;&apos;emp_id&apos;&#160;back&#160;as&#160;a&#160;column) df_reset&#160;= &#160;df_indexed.reset_index() df_reset.head(2) The output is: &#160;&#160; emp_id&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;name&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;job_title&#160;&#160;&#160;dept&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;sal&#160;&#160;loc&#160; left 0 &#160;&#160;&#160;E001&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;Alice&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;Sales&#160;Executive&#160;&#160;Sales&#160;&#160; 72000 &#160;&#160;&#160;NY&#160;&#160;&#160; No 1 &#160;&#160;&#160;E002&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;Mark&#160;&#160;Software&#160;Engineer&#160;&#160;&#160;Engg&#160;&#160; 95000 &#160;&#160;SFO&#160;&#160;&#160; No Many arguments we have explained for read_csv() also apply to read_excel() , as you can see in Figure 6.3 (Official documentation of read_excel() function): Figure 6.3 : Arguments of the read_excel() function For more options, refer to pandas official documentation (at https://pandas.pydata.org/docs/reference/api/pandas.read_excel.html ). We would recommend that you try out a few arguments as per your specific needs. Now, let us explore some of the specific features of read_excel() : By default, read_excel() function reads first sheet. The sheet_name() argument allows you to read a specific sheet by its name or index, or retrieve multiple sheets at once: # By default, it reads first sheet df_jan&#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel(&apos;finance_data.xlsx&apos;)&#160; #&#160;To read&#160;the first&#160;sheet, using index (0-inedxed) df_jan&#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel(&apos;finance_data.xlsx&apos;,sheet_name=0)&#160; #&#160;To read the sheet&#160;named&#160;&apos;Feb&apos;, by specifying sheet name &#160; df_feb&#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel(&apos;finance_data.xlsx&apos;,sheet_name=&quot;Feb&quot;)&#160; #&#160;To read the&#160;first and second sheets df_jan_feb&#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel(&apos;finance_data.xlsx&apos; ,sheet_name=[0,&#160;1])&#160; #&#160;To read&#160;all&#160;the sheets df_all_sheet&#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel(&apos;finance_data.xlsx&apos;,sheet_name=None)&#160; When you read multiple sheets, it returns a dictionary, whose length equals the number of sheets read, with sheet names as keys and DataFrames as values. print(df_all_sheet.keys())&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;dict_keys([&apos;Jan&apos;,&#160;&apos;Feb&apos;,&#160;&apos;Mar&apos;]) print( type(df_all_sheet[&apos;Jan&apos;]))&#160; #&#160;&lt;class&#160;&apos;pandas.core.frame.DataFrame&apos;&gt; #&#160;To&#160;fetch&#160;data&#160;from&#160;sheet&#160;named&#160;&apos;Jan&apos; df_all_sheet.get(&apos;Jan&apos;) This function also supports reading xls, xlsb, xlsm, and many other file types. We can also read html data, or connect to databases and read tables. To create the sample files, we have used pandas writer functions, such as to_csv() , to_excel() , to_json() , etc. You can also use pd.ExcelWriter() with to_excel() to write data to multiple sheets. We will cover more on this in Chapter 9, Core Excel Read-Write Tools in Python . Datatypes in pandas Apart from regular int , float , bool and str datatype, pandas offer additional datatypes, which makes handling data efficient. For example, columns like Date of Birth , Application Date , or transaction date can be considered as the datetime datatype for smoother manipulation. Prior to pandas 3.0.0 (major release dated January 21, 2026 ), columns with text (string) data such as Employee ID , Employee Name , etc. were also considered as object datatype, which are now inferred as str (string) datatype. So, in latest version, object datatype is assigned only to non- standard columns or mixed datatypes. For example, any column like salary , amount or approved contains numeric values as well as the literal string &apos;missing&apos; (not to be confused with actual missing values like NaN or None ). These columns will be read as object columns by default. We can convert it to a numeric column either while reading the data or after loading. Another key datatype is the category datatype, which can be used for qualitative variables with fixed categories such as gender , age group , or region . In most cases, it helps save memory. To optimize performance and memory usage, we can convert column(s) to more specific datatypes 2 as needed, depending on the nature of the data. You can either define a datatype while reading data into pandas or change it later. Defining a datatype while reading the file If you know the correct datatypes for categorical or datetime columns, you should define the datatype while reading the data. For example, when you print df.dtypes for finance_data.xlsx , it shows many columns as object datatype, which should be stored as datetime , float or category columns. txn_id&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;str txn_date&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;object client&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;str amount&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;object approved&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;object region&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;str dtype:&#160;object You can define an appropriate datatype for one or more columns using the dtype argument while reading the data: df &#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel( &apos;finance_data.xlsx&apos;,&#160;dtype={&apos;region&apos;:&#160; &apos;category&apos;}) print(df.dtypes) The output is: txn_id&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;str txn_date&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;object client&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;str amount&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;object approved&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;object region&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;category dtype:&#160;object It changes the datatype for the region column from str to category . However, while trying to convert the amount column to float, it fails. df &#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel( &apos;finance_data.xlsx&apos;,&#160;dtype={&apos;amount&apos;:&#160;&apos;float64&apos;}) # ValueError Inspecting this column tells us that this column has some values stored as missing in text format. You might encounter errors when specifying the dtype argument if the column contains non-numeric or invalid entries that do not align with the expected type. In such cases, you can import data as-is and later change datatype to suitable ones, or handle missing values while reading the data by using na_values argument such as: df&#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel(&apos;finance_data.xlsx&apos;,&#160;na_values=&apos;missing&apos;) print(df.dtypes) We also had values stored as missing in text format in the txn_date column; all such values are now being treated as missing values. As a result, Python correctly interprets txn_date as a datetime column and amount as a float column. txn_id&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;str txn_date&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;datetime64[us] client&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;str amount&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;float64 approved&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;object region&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;str dtype:&#160;object We recommend you inspect the issue with approved column as well to read it as float. If the date column is read as an object type, you can also use the parse_dates argument in the reader function, which works only if there are no missing or invalid values in that column. Alternatively, you can convert it after reading the file. Changing datatype after reading the file Sometimes, after you have imported your dataset, you may need to adjust datatypes if columns are misinterpreted (e.g., numbers as text, dates as objects). For conversion to numeric, pd.to_numeric() and to datetime, pd.to_datetime() methods 3 can be used with the errors=&apos;coerce&apos; argument to handle invalid or missing values by converting them to NaN . By default, errors=&apos;raise&apos; is used, which is good when you want strict validation and fail-fast behavior on any non-numeric values, while errors=&apos;coerce&apos; is useful when working with messy real-world data where invalid entries should be converted into NaN instead of stopping execution. df [&apos;amount&apos;]&#160;=&#160;pd.to_numeric(df[&apos;amount&apos;],&#160;errors=&apos;coerce&apos;) df[&apos;txn_date&apos;]&#160;=&#160;pd.to_datetime(df[&apos;txn_date&apos;],&#160;errors=&apos;coerce&apos;) For converting column to category datatype, you can use astype() method: df[&apos;region&apos;] = df[&apos;region&apos;].astype(&apos;category&apos;) These functions also allow changing the datatype of multiple columns at once without a loop. Filtering, selecting, and querying data So far, you have used df[&apos;column_name&apos;] to fetch a single column. Similarly, you can quickly fetch multiple columns by supplying a list of column names inside df[] : df&#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel(&apos;finance_data.xlsx&apos;,&#160;na_values=[&apos;missing&apos;,&#160;&apos;-&apos;]) df[[ &apos;client&apos;,&#160;&apos;amount&apos;]]&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;It’s&#160;a&#160;DataFrame Furthermore, pandas offer amazing capabilities to select data you might need for analysis: Selecting data based on datatypes : Sometimes, you need to get all numeric columns, or all date-based columns, etc., for some analysis. You can do that conveniently with the df.select_dtypes() method: #&#160;Select&#160;only&#160;float-type&#160;columns&#160;(returns&#160;full&#160;DataFrame&#160;slice) df.select_dtypes(&apos;float&apos;) #&#160;Get&#160;column&#160;names&#160;of&#160;float-type&#160;columns&#160;only df.select_dtypes( &apos;float&apos;).columns #&#160;Select&#160;all&#160;numeric&#160;columns&#160;(both&#160;int&#160;and&#160;float) df.select_dtypes(include=&apos;number&apos;) #&#160;Select&#160;all&#160;non-numeric&#160;columns&#160;(str, object,&#160;datetime,&#160;category,&#160;etc.) df.select_dtypes(exclude=&apos;number&apos;) # String datatype df.select_dtypes(&apos;str&apos;).columns In older pandas versions (&lt; 3.0.0), text columns were treated as object datatype. So, for backward compatibility, columns with the new string dtype are currently treated as object when you call select_dtypes(&apos;object&apos;) . This behaviour is deprecated and will be removed in a future release. Selecting data with row-labels and column names using df.loc : The preferred way to draw data based on row labels, row index, or conditions and column names is to use df.loc[row index/label/condition, column_name] : df.loc[0,&#160;:]&#160;&#160; #&#160;1st&#160;row,&#160;all&#160;columns df.loc[0 ,&#160;&apos;client&apos;]&#160; #&#160;1st&#160;row,&#160;client&#160;col df.loc[:,&#160;&apos;client&apos;]&#160;&#160; #&#160;all&#160;the&#160;rows,&#160;client&#160;col df.loc[:,&#160;[&apos;client&apos;,&#160;&apos;region&apos;]]&#160; #&#160;all&#160;the&#160;rows,&#160;multiple&#160;columns df.loc[:,&#160;&apos;client&apos; &#160;:&#160; &apos;region&apos;]&#160; #&#160;all&#160;the&#160;rows,&#160;consecutive&#160;columns #&#160;NOTE:&#160;including&#160;&apos;region&apos;&#160;column df.loc[[0,&#160;2],&#160;:]&#160; #&#160;multiple&#160;rows,&#160;all&#160;columns&#160; df.loc[0:2,&#160;:]&#160; #&#160;consecutive&#160;rows (includes row index 2),&#160;all&#160;columns&#160; Note: When using slicing in df.loc, remember that it includes the last element. For example, df.loc[0:2] will include rows 0, 1, and 2, unlike other Python slicing, where the last element is usually excluded. If you set txn_id as the row label (index) in your DataFrame, you can use the same logic, but instead of numeric indices, you would reference the actual txn_id values: #&#160;Setting&#160;&apos;txn_id&apos; as index/row label df_index&#160;=&#160;df.set_index(&apos;txn_id&apos;) df_index.loc[&apos;T001&apos;,&#160;:]&#160; #&#160;row&#160;with&#160;txn_id&#160;T001 df_index.loc[&apos;T002&apos;,&#160;&apos;client&apos;]&#160; #&#160;row&#160;with&#160;txn_id&#160;T001,&#160;client&#160;col To filter data based on some condition(s) : To get data for transactions where amount exceeds 1000, you can mention condition(s) inside square brackets, such as: #&#160;Transactions&#160;with&#160;amount&#160;greater&#160;than&#160;1000 df.loc[df[&apos;amount&apos;]&#160;&gt;&#160;1000, :] #&#160;Amount&#160;greater&#160;than&#160;1000&#160;and&#160;approved&#160;transactions df.loc[(df[&apos;amount&apos;]&#160;&gt;&#160;1000)&#160;&amp;&#160;(df[&apos;approved&apos;]&#160;==&#160;1), :] You can achieve the same results without explicitly using .loc (by simply writing df[condition] ) but using df.loc[condition] is safer and preferred for more complex filtering operations. For selecting data strictly based on row-index and column-index : If your data does not have column headers, or if you are unsure of the names, or simply you prefer numeric indexing instead, you can use df.iloc[row_index, column_index] to select data based on the row and column positions. Here, the same indexing principles apply as you have used earlier with lists, tuples, or strings: df.iloc[2,&#160;1]&#160; #&#160;third&#160;row,&#160;second&#160;column df.iloc[0:3, 0] &#9;&#9; # first, second, third row _ first column Practicing to subset data the way you need will help you make things easier and faster. We would recommend using df.loc whenever possible, unless you specifically need row and column positions, in which case use df.iloc . Deleting rows or columns or both : df.drop() can be used to delete rows, columns, or both based on the parameter axis , axis=0 (default) removes rows, and axis=1 removes columns: #&#160;To drop&#160;columns df.drop([&apos;txn_id&apos;,&#160;&apos;client&apos;],&#160;axis&#160;=&#160;1)&#160; # To drop row(s) df.drop(0) # To drop&#160;row(s) &#160;&amp; column (s) df.drop(index &#160;=&#160;[ 0,&#160;1],&#160;columns&#160;=&#160;[&apos;txn_date&apos;, &apos;client&apos;]) Advanced querying : Another powerful function, df.query() , enables advanced row filtering based on SQL-like string expressions. This makes filtering more dynamic and readable. You can even use Python variables inside queries with @ or apply complex filtering conditions: #&#160;Use&#160;variable&#160;inside&#160;query threshold&#160;=&#160;float(input(&apos;Enter&#160;Min&#160;Amount:&#160;&apos;)) df.query(&quot;amount&#160;&gt;&#160;@threshold&quot;) #&#160;Filter&#160;rows&#160;where&#160;region&#160;is&#160;in&#160;a&#160;list df.query(&quot;region&#160;in&#160;[&apos;East&apos;,&#160;&apos;North&apos;]&quot;) #&#160;Rows&#160;where&#160;amount&#160;is&#160;between&#160;1000&#160;and&#160;1500 df.query(&quot;amount.between(1000,&#160;1500)&quot;) Replicating Excel’s COUNT, COUNTIF, COUNTIFS, SUMIF, SUMIFS : Use the insights gained so far to determine the following: total number of transactions, count of approved transactions, and how many of those were of an amount exceeding 1000, and total amount for approved transactions in the East region using the learnings so far. These use cases with solutions are given in the accompanying code file. More filtering functions: You can also explore df.filter() , when you do not want to hard-code column names. It is useful in extract, transform, and load ( ETL ) pipelines where column names vary slightly across files. You can also use all() to ensure every element meets a condition, and any() to check if at least one element does. Refer to the code files for examples. Data cleaning and handling missing values Missing or incomplete data is a common challenge in any analysis, regardless of the tool. Whether working in Excel or Python, unaddressed missing values can lead to incorrect calculations or errors. In Excel, empty cells or cells with n/a , NA , NaN , #N/A , &lt;NA&gt; , or other similar values often disrupt formulas and may return #N/A or #VALUE! errors. In pandas, empty cells are stored as NaN 4 (stands for Not a Number) for most datatypes and NaT (Not a Time) for date-time columns. You can choose to treat missing values carefully before analyzing the data. Let us start with the same finance_data.xlsx that we had used earlier and load the first sheet in pandas. Inspect the data carefully. You will see that pandas automatically identify blank cells (within the data), n/a , NA , NaN , #N/A , &lt;NA&gt; , or other similar values as missing data and denote them as nan . However, this data has some other problematic cells like text written as missing (cell B5 and D14) and cell entry as – in cell E12, which are kept as is by pandas. You can verify this by using df.info() or viewing the data: df &#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel( &apos;finance_data.xlsx&apos;) Figure 6.4 shows the same data in Excel and how it looks in Spyder after reading using pandas: Figure 6.4 : finance_data in Excel and pandas To tell pandas to treat any specific value (such as missing and - in our example) as a missing value, we can use na_values argument in the reader function: df &#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel( &apos;finance_data.xlsx&apos;,&#160;na_values=[&apos;missing&apos;,&#160;&apos;-&apos;]) df.info() You can see in Figure 6.5 that using na_values ensures these values are converted to nan or NaT , i.e., missing values: Figure 6.5 : Data view and df.info() after ensuring to read missing values appropriately Identifying missing values The method .isna() or .isnull() checks for missing values in a Series or DataFrame and returns True for missing values and False for others. Chaining it with the .sum() method gives the total number of missing values in the column or data, because when you try to apply sum over Booleans, True converts to 1 and False converts to 0 . df.isna().sum() gives the count of missing values in each column. Sometimes, we need to see the count of missing values in each row. In that case, we can use df.isna().sum(axis=1) . Note: The axis parameter determines the direction of operation. axis=0 applies functions across rows to produce sum of each column, while axis=1 applies functions across columns to produce sum of each row. This parameter is commonly used in several pandas functions such as drop() , fillna() , apply() , mean() , etc. Chaining any of these with the .sum() method, once again, gives the overall total. We have a total of 23 missing values in this data (too many for this size of data, but fine for demo purposes): print(df.isna().sum().sum()) &#160; #&#160;23 Likewise, the functions .notna() or .notnull() check for non-missing values and returns True where data is present. Handling missing values To handle missing values in a data set, it is crucial to understand the data, the reason for non-availability of that value, and the business logic to figure out what to fill in there, or if it is needed to treat those missing values as a separate category. For this topic, we will show you different ways to fill missing values, which may or may not make sense practically, as we are playing with a toy data set for now. For now, we would recommend creating a copy of the data so that your original data remains as-is in case you need it later by running this code: df_backup = df.copy()&#160; #&#160;creating a copy of data Here, the amount column has some missing values, all of these are approved transactions. We can fill them all as a fixed value, say, 500 (assuming that is the minimum order allowed). To fill missing values with a constant, we can use fillna() method: df[&apos;amount&apos;].fillna(500)&#160; #&#160;Does&#160;not&#160;change&#160;original&#160;data df[&apos;amount&apos;] #&#160;Still it has missing values This code does not change the original data. If you intend to change the same, you can assign the result back to the column or update the whole DataFrame by supplying a dictionary of column name, replacement value, and using the argument inplace = True : df[&apos;amount&apos;]&#160;=&#160;df[&apos;amount&apos;].fillna(500)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Changes&#160;original&#160;data #&#160;or df.fillna({&apos;amount&apos;:&#160;500},&#160;inplace=True)&#160; #&#160;Changes&#160;original&#160;data Filling missing values using any constant for multiple columns can be done by supplying a dictionary of column name and replacement value in fillna() : df&#160;=&#160;df_backup.copy() &#160; #&#160;Now&#160;again&#160;df&#160;has&#160;missing&#160;values&#160;in&#160;‘amount’&#160; #&#160;filling&#160;missing&#160;values&#160;in&#160;multiple&#160;columns df.fillna({&apos;amount&apos;:&#160;500,&#160;&apos;approved&apos;:&#160;1}) For the transaction date and client name column, maybe you can think of filling in the missing values with the previous valid entry, because in a chronological transaction log, a blank date or client usually means same as the last recorded , rather than a new, different value. We can use ffill method to fill the missing value with the immediate previous value, and bfill method to fill the missing data with the immediate next value, if available. For illustration purposes, we are showing forward fill for txn_date and backward fill for the client column: df[&apos;txn_date&apos;].ffill()&#160; df[&apos;client&apos;].bfill() These both methods depend on existing valid values. If the required previous or next value is not available, the missing entry will stay unfilled, which you can fill with a constant value or use other strategies. You might come across older code using fillna(method=&apos;ffill&apos;) . It worked earlier but is now deprecated. These were the basic ways to fill up missing values. At times, we need to go beyond the basics. One thing we also do in analytics is to explore the data, other tables and relationship between the variables to take a clue on missing values. For example, some other relationship table gives us information that client Alice is from East region or the accounts department gave us the info that any transaction that amounts for over 1500 is not approved. We can combine conditional filtering with fillna() to fill missing values based on such logic: df.loc[df[&apos;client&apos;]&#160;==&apos;Alice&apos;,&#160;&apos;region&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;East&apos; df.loc[df[&apos;amount&apos;]&#160;&gt;&#160;1500,&#160;&apos;approved&apos; ]&#160;=&#160;0 Sometimes, we simply replace the missing value by the mean, median, or category-wise statistics for numerical variables and the most frequent value or category-wise mode for categorical variables. Some examples are: #&#160;Mean&#160;replacement mean_amt&#160;=&#160;df[&apos;amount&apos;].mean() df[&apos;amount&apos;].fillna(mean_amt.round(1)) #&#160;Median&#160;Replacement df[&apos;amount&apos;].fillna(df[&apos;amount&apos;].median()) #&#160;Mode/&#160;Most&#160;Frequent&#160;Value&#160;Replacement most_fre_region&#160;=&#160;df[&apos;region&apos;].value_counts().idxmax() df[&apos;region&apos;].fillna(most_fre_region) We can also use advanced options like interpolation for filling up missing values. #&#160;Replacement&#160;by&#160;Interpolation df[&apos;amount&apos;].interpolate() # Linear Interpolation Data analysts spend a significant portion of their time on data cleaning, with missing value treatment being a major aspect of the process. Library pandas provides powerful and flexible functions for handling missing values, enabling efficient data manipulation beyond Excel’s limitations. Beginners often choose to delete rows or columns with missing data for a quick cleanup. This can be done with dropna() function (refer to more examples in the code file): df.dropna()&#160;&#160; #&#160;deletes&#160;all&#160;the&#160;rows&#160;with&#160;&apos;any&apos;&#160;missing&#160;value df.dropna(how=&apos;all&apos;)&#160; #&#160;delete&#160;only&#160;the&#160;row(s)&#160;with&#160;&apos;all&apos;&#160;missing&#160;values df.dropna(axis&#160;=&#160;1)&#160; #&#160;delete&#160;all&#160;the&#160;columns&#160;with&#160;&apos;any&apos;&#160;missing&#160;value df.dropna(axis=1,&#160;how=&apos;all&apos;)&#160; #&#160;delete&#160;only&#160;the&#160;column(s)&#160;with&#160;&apos;all&apos;&#160;missing&#160;values df&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;No&#160;change&#160;in&#160;original&#160;data You might have noticed that dropna() returns a new object without missing values and does not modify the original DataFrame unless you use inplace=True . Managing duplicates Sometimes, due to system errors, some transactions get logged twice, and we trust the first entry (based on sort order or timestamp) and want to remove the rest. For example, these two rows of the data contain duplicates: 7 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; T007 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaT &#160;&#160; Angela &#160;&#160; 1300.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;1.0 &#160;&#160; South 8 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; T007 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaT &#160;&#160; Angela &#160;&#160; 1300.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;1.0 &#160;&#160; South You can use the drop_duplicates() function to remove duplicate rows from the DataFrame, keeping only the first occurrence of each unique row: df.drop_duplicates() The output is: &#160;&#160;&#160; NaT &#160;&#160;&#160; David &#160;&#160; 1100.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;1.0 &#160;&#160; South 4 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; T005 &#160; 2024-01-20&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN &#160;&#160; 950.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1.0 &#160;&#160;&#160; East 15 &#160;&#160;&#160; T014 &#160; 2024-01-31&#160;&#160;Olivia &#160;&#160; 1000.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;0.0 &#160;&#160; South 16 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaT &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN Row index 8 is dropped. You can also remove duplicates based on specific column(s) using a subset argument. Such as removing duplicate payments or records based on transaction ID, i.e., column txn_id : df.drop_duplicates(subset=[&apos;txn_id&apos;], keep=&apos;first&apos;) # keep=&apos;first&apos; is default If you are doing a data quality audit and want to flag any suspicious duplicate IDs, regardless of which came first, so your team can investigate all instances. To see every row 5 with duplicated txn_id values, you can use df[df[&apos;txn_id&apos;].duplicated(keep=False)] . Column calculations and quick statistics In Excel, applying a formula to a column requires copying or dragging, taking care of cell references, while pandas allows you to perform operations directly on columns without needing to write a loop. This is called vectorized calculation, and this is one of the reasons pandas is fast and powerful. Let us use the same data as before, slightly trimmed for brevity: df &#160; = &#160; df.head() df_backup = df.copy() # Back-up in case we need it df The output is: &#160; &#160; txn_id &#160;&#160;&#160; txn_date &#160; client &#160;&#160; amount &#160;&#160; approved &#160; region 0 &#160;&#160;&#160; T001 &#160; 2024-01-15&#160;&#160;Alice &#160;&#160; 1200.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN 1 &#160;&#160;&#160; T002 &#160; 2024-01-16&#160;&#160;&#160;Mark &#160;&#160;&#160; 900.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 0.0 &#160;&#160; North 2 &#160;&#160;&#160; T003 &#160; 2024-01-18&#160;&#160;Linda &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN 3 &#160;&#160;&#160; T004 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaT &#160;&#160; David &#160;&#160; 1100.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;1.0 &#160;&#160; South 4 &#160;&#160;&#160; T005 &#160; 2024-01-20&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN &#160;&#160; 1500.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN &#160;&#160;&#160; West Assuming a transaction fee of 5% on all amounts, we are willing to create a column named fee that shows this amount. Then, we would prefer to compute the net amount, that is, the difference between amount and fee . Library pandas help us do this neatly with simple code: df[&apos;fee&apos;] &#160; = &#160; df[&apos;amount&apos;] &#160; * &#160; 0.05 &#160; #&#160;NaNs&#160;are&#160;produced&#160;for&#160;missing&#160;values df[&apos;net_amount&apos;] &#160; = &#160; df[&apos;amount&apos;] &#160; - &#160; df[&apos;fee&apos;] df The output is: &#160;&#160; txn_id &#160;&#160;&#160; txn_date &#160; client &#160;&#160; amount &#160;&#160; approved &#160; region &#160;&#160;&#160; fee &#160;&#160; net_amount 0 &#160;&#160;&#160; T001 &#160; 2024-01-15&#160;&#160;Alice &#160;&#160; 1200.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160; 60.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1140.0 1 &#160;&#160;&#160; T002 &#160; 2024-01-16&#160;&#160;&#160;Mark &#160;&#160;&#160; 900.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 0.0 &#160;&#160; North &#160;&#160; 45.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 855.0 2 &#160;&#160;&#160; T003 &#160; 2024-01-18&#160;&#160;Linda &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN 3 &#160;&#160;&#160; T004 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaT &#160;&#160; David &#160;&#160; 1100.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;1.0 &#160;&#160; South &#160;&#160; 55.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1045.0 4 &#160;&#160;&#160; T005 &#160; 2024-01-20&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN &#160;&#160; 1500.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN &#160;&#160;&#160; West &#160;&#160; 75.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1425.0 As the amount value was not available for the third row, we got missing values in our newly computed columns too, as expected but we can handle using fillna() directly in the current code: df = df_back_up # Going back to original data df[&apos;fee&apos;]&#160;=&#160;df[&apos;amount&apos;].fillna(0)&#160;*&#160;0.05&#160;&#160; df[&apos;net_amount&apos;]&#160;=&#160;df[&apos;amount&apos;].fillna(0)&#160;-&#160;df[&apos;fee&apos;] This code creates a fee column by replacing NaN values in amount with 0 temporarily and multiplying the result by 0.05. It does not alter the original amount column. Alternately, we can use mul() or sub() functions, as these offer the fill_value argument to define a particular value if a data point is missing. This is especially helpful when combining two columns when either one has missing values. With fill_value=0 , missing values in either column are treated as 0, so net_amount will always show a number: df &#160; = &#160; df_back_up &#160;&#160;&#160; df.loc[:, &#160; &apos;fee&apos;] &#160; = &#160; df[&apos;amount&apos;].mul(0.05, &#160; fill_value &#160; = &#160; 0) df.loc[:, &#160; &apos;net_amount&apos;] &#160; = &#160; df[&apos;amount&apos;].sub(df[&apos;fee&apos;], &#160; fill_value &#160; = &#160; 0) df The output is: &#160;&#160; txn_id &#160;&#160;&#160; txn_date &#160; client &#160;&#160; amount &#160;&#160; approved &#160; region &#160;&#160;&#160; fee &#160;&#160; net_amount 0 &#160;&#160;&#160; T001 &#160; 2024-01-15&#160;&#160;Alice &#160;&#160; 1200.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160; 60.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1140.0 1 &#160;&#160;&#160; T002 &#160; 2024-01-16&#160;&#160;&#160;Mark &#160;&#160;&#160; 900.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 0.0 &#160;&#160; North &#160;&#160; 45.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 855.0 2 &#160;&#160;&#160; T003 &#160; 2024-01-18&#160;&#160;Linda &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160; 0.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 0.0 3 &#160;&#160;&#160; T004 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaT &#160;&#160; David &#160;&#160; 1100.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;1.0 &#160;&#160; South &#160;&#160; 55.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1045.0 4 &#160;&#160;&#160; T005 &#160; 2024-01-20&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN &#160;&#160; 1500.0&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN &#160;&#160;&#160; West &#160;&#160; 75.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1425.0 We have decided to give Rs. 100 cashbacks only for approved transactions, try creating another column for that. In the previously mentioned examples, we have used subtraction and multiplication on columns, and the calculation of the values is done elementwise . The following table gives you common operators and their corresponding method names: Operator Method + .add() - .sub() * .mul() / .div() // .floordiv() % .mod() ** .pow() == .eq() != .ne() &gt; .gt() &gt;= .ge() &lt; .lt() &lt;= .le() Table 6.1 : Common operators and their corresponding method names For quick computations, you can use operators as you use with numbers, e.g., df[&apos;amount&apos;] += 50 . And when you need more control over missing data or when indexes or column names do not match perfectly, you can use methods such as add() or sub() . pandas also provides vectorized versions of common mathematical functions that can be applied on columns, a few are mentioned as follows: df[&apos;amount&apos;].min() df[&apos;amount&apos;].max() df[&apos;amount&apos; ].mean() df[&apos;amount&apos;].median() df[&apos;amount&apos;].sum() Another powerful function is df.assign() , which lets you create new columns dynamically without modifying the original table, ideal for temporary transformations. It works well in method chains (with df.apply() , df.query() , etc.), improving readability. Note: Method chaining is powerful; however, it can lead to lengthy code. When a line of code becomes too long to fit neatly, Python allows you to break it across multiple lines. You can use a backslash (\) for explicit line continuation, or simply wrap the expression in parentheses (), or brackets []. The latter is generally preferred for readability, as it is cleaner and less errorprone. For example: #&#160;Using&#160;backslash df_full.assign(net_amount&#160;=&#160;df_full[&apos;amount&apos;]&#160;*&#160;0.9)&#160;\ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;.query( &quot;region&#160;==&#160;&apos;East&apos;&quot;)&#160;\ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;.sort_values( &apos;net_amount&apos;,&#160;ascending=True) #&#160;Using&#160;parenthesis (df_full.assign(net_amount&#160;=&#160;df_full[&apos;amount&apos;]&#160;*&#160; 0.9) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;.query( &quot;region&#160;==&#160;&apos;East&apos;&quot;) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;.sort_values( &apos;net_amount&apos;,&#160;ascending=True)) The above code computes a net_amount as 90% of the amount, filters for rows where region == &apos;East&apos; , and returns the rows sorted by net_amount in ascending order. For advanced logic to compute derived columns, you can use apply() , map() , or transform() . The next section discusses these. Advanced data transformation There is a lot you can do with pandas , we will cover all key functions that help in efficiently manipulating or analyzing data. For that, start with importing and inspecting a dummy YouTube channel statistic for Maya, the content creator, we referred to in Chapter 1 , Python for Excel Users . Variable names are self-explanatory. Check the data description for more details: df&#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel(&apos;YT_data.xlsx&apos;,&#160;index_col=&apos;Video&#160;Id&apos;) This data (150 rows and 15 columns) does not have any missing value. You can check for duplicates as well. Column names are quite lengthy and renaming them would be helpful for analysis. df.rename() takes a dictionary to map names: df.rename(columns={ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Video&#160;Title&apos;:&#160;&apos;title&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Playlist&#160;Name&apos;:&#160;&apos;playlist&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Video&#160;Duration&#160;(Mins)&apos;:&#160;&apos;duration_min&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Video_Publish_Date&apos; :&#160;&apos;published&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Watch&#160;Time&#160;(Mins)&apos;:&#160;&apos;watch_min&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Views&apos;:&#160;&apos;views&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Impressions&apos;:&#160;&apos;impressions&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Subscribers&apos; :&#160;&apos;subs_net&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Subscribers&#160;Gained&apos;:&#160;&apos;subs_gained&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Subscribers&#160;Lost&apos;:&#160;&apos;subs_lost&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Average&#160;Percentage&#160;Viewed&apos;:&#160;&apos;avg_view_pct&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Likes&apos; :&#160;&apos;likes&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Comments&apos;:&#160;&apos;comments&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Shares&apos;:&#160;&apos;shares&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;Dislikes&apos;:&#160;&apos;dislikes&apos; },&#160;inplace=True) The column playlist which stores names of playlists, can be converted to category datatype: df[&apos;playlist&apos;]&#160;=&#160;df[&apos;playlist&apos;].astype(&apos;category&apos;) df.info() The output is: &lt;class &#160; &apos;pandas.DataFrame&apos;&gt; Index:&#160;150 &#160;entries,&#160;V_001&#160;to&#160;V_150 Data&#160;columns&#160;(total&#160;15 &#160;columns): &#160; #&#160;&#160;&#160;Column&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;Non-Null&#160;Count&#160;&#160;Dtype&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;
