150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;datetime64[us] &#160; 4 &#160;&#160;&#160;watch_min&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;float64&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160; 5 &#160;&#160;&#160;views&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;int64&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160; 6 &#160;&#160;&#160;impressions&#160;&#160;&#160; 150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;int64&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160; 7 &#160;&#160;&#160;subs_net&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;int64&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160; 8 &#160;&#160;&#160;subs_gained&#160;&#160;&#160; 150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;int64&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160; 9 &#160;&#160;&#160;subs_lost&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;int64&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160; 10 &#160;&#160;avg_view_pct&#160;&#160; 150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;float64&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160; 11 &#160;&#160;likes&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;int64&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160; 12 &#160;&#160;comments&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;int64&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160; 13 &#160;&#160;shares&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;int64&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160; 14 &#160;&#160;dislikes&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 150 &#160;non-null&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;int64&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; dtypes:&#160;category(1),&#160;datetime64[us](1),&#160;float64(3),&#160;int64(9),&#160;str(1) memory&#160;usage:&#160;17.8+&#160;KB Now, column names are convenient to type, and the playlist is reflected as a category datatype. Feel free to explore unique values and the frequency table for this column. You can also check the summary of the data set for numeric columns, all columns, or selected columns using df.describe() . We will move to something practically useful. Applying custom functions to data To apply a custom function to data, pandas offer apply() or map() functions. For example, to create a column video_length based on a logic such that if duration_min is less than 10, label it as Short ; otherwise, label it as Long . There are many ways to do it. Let us do it using both map() and apply() : df[&apos;video&#160;length&apos;]&#160;=&#160;( &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;df[ &apos;duration_min&apos;] &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;. map(lambda &#160;x:&#160; &apos;Short&apos; &#160; if &#160;x&#160;&lt;&#160; 10 &#160; else &#160; &apos;Long&apos;) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;.astype( &apos;category&apos;) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;) # Or Using apply() function df[&apos;video&#160;length&apos;]&#160;=&#160;( &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;df[ &apos;duration_min&apos;] &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;.apply( lambda &#160;x:&#160; &apos;Short&apos; &#160; if &#160;x&#160;&lt;&#160; 10 &#160; else &#160; &apos;Long&apos;) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;.astype( &apos;category&apos;) ) We demonstrated the powerful combination of lambda function, combined with map() or apply() for column-wise operation. This approach is highly effective for data processing. The apply() function can also be used on a DataFrame for applying functions across rows or columns. For instance, suppose we want to create a new column, performance, that categorizes videos (each row) as High , Medium , or Low based on specific criteria involving multiple columns. In this case, defining a function and applying it row-wise is more practical: def&#160;tag_performance(row): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;if&#160; row[&apos;views&apos;]&#160;&gt;&#160;5000 &#160; and &#160; row[&apos;watch_min&apos;]&#160;&gt;&#160;200: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; return &#160; &apos;High&apos; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;elif&#160; row[&apos;views&apos;]&#160;&lt;&#160;1000: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; return &#160; &apos;Low&apos; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; else: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; return &#160; &apos;Medium&apos; df[&apos;performance&apos;]&#160;=&#160;df.apply(tag_performance,&#160;axis=1)&#160;&#160; To use apply() on a DataFrame, we need to specify axis = 1 to indicate that the function should be applied to each row rather than each column. This is important because the tag_performance() function expects a row. In short, to transform values in a single column or a Pandas Series, map() is often a better choice than apply() . For complex row-wise transformations involving multiple columns, apply() can be used. Sorting and ordering the columns If in any table, you need to sort, reorder, shuffle, or arrange in custom order for improving search, ranking items, or for sampling, all of these can be done with simple code. For example, to sort videos by views (highest to lowest), similar to Excel’s Sort Largest to Smallest feature: #&#160;Sort&#160;rows &#160; by &#160; &apos;views&apos; &#160; in &#160;descending&#160; order df.sort_values(by=&apos;views&apos;,&#160;ascending=False) You can fetch title and views columns for information about videos with the highest and lowest views. For further diagnostics, you can either print the first and last few values using head() and tail() or save the newly created data with a different name. Next, suppose you are willing to see prioritized videos with playlist names arranged alphabetically (A-Z) and then arranged in descending order of watch time, this is basically sorting using two columns and can be achieved by using a list of column names in df.sort_values() method: #&#160;Sort&#160;by&#160;playlist&#160;(A-Z),&#160;then&#160;by&#160;watch&#160;time&#160;(descending) playlist_watchTime&#160;=&#160;(df.sort_values(by=[&apos;playlist&apos;,&#160;&apos;watch_min&apos;],&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; ascending=[True,&#160;False]) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[[ &apos;title&apos;,&#160;&apos;playlist&apos;,&#160;&apos;watch_min&apos;]]) The first few rows of playlist_watchTime dataset are as given in Figure 6.6 : Figure 6.6 : Glimpse of playlist_watchTime data set This data set does not have missing values, but if you have missing values in some column, you can control the placement of missing values to be at the start or end using the same powerful sort_value() function. Cumulative sum 6 or a running total of views can be computed easily with df[&apos;views&apos;].cumsum() . Also, df.sample() allows you to take random samples of data or randomly shuffle the data, and much more, very useful in data science. Grouping and pivoting data In Excel, COUNTIF , SUMIF , and AVERAGEIF provide quick conditional summaries while PivotTables and PowerQuery enable interactive groupings, aggregations, and data reshaping for deeper analysis. pandas offers similar yet more advanced capabilities: groupby() , agg() , transform() , and filter() for summarizing, transforming, and filtering groups (like performing advanced COUNTIF , SUMIF , and AVERAGEIF , etc.). pivot_table() and pivot() for pivoting and reshaping data (like PivotTables, but with more control). melt() , stack() , unstack() for reshaping datasets between wide and long formats (an alternative to PivotTables and PowerQuery). Newer versions of Excel, especially Excel 365, have introduced dynamic arrays, FILTER , UNIQUE , LET , and even Python integration , but pandas remains unmatched in flexibility for complex data manipulations. Just like Excel, pandas provides multiple ways to achieve similar tasks, the key is to choose the most readable and practical option for your needs. Summarizing, transforming, and filtering groups Using the same YT_data , let us find some key insights using groupy() , agg() , and transform() . The groupby() function in pandas is incredibly powerful. It allows you to compute multiple summaries across one or more columns, such as total views and watch time per playlist. You can also generate multiple summaries of a single column (e.g., sum, mean, and max of views) or apply different aggregations to different columns (e.g., sum of views and max of watch time). All of this can be done efficiently without the need for a Pivot Table. Total views per playlist: df.groupby(&apos;playlist&apos; )[&apos;views&apos;].sum() The output is: playlist Career&#160;Advice&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;21106 Data&#160;Storytelling&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;33599 Excel&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;349088 Linear&#160;Algebra&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;64940 Python&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;239396 R&#160;Programming&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;29944 Science&#160;Experiments&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;32514 Statistics&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;44081 Name:&#160;views,&#160;dtype:&#160;int64 The output is a pandas Series, you can use df.groupby(&apos;playlist&apos;)[[&apos;views&apos;]].sum() to get a DataFrame. Total views and watch time by playlist: df.groupby(&apos;playlist&apos;)[[&apos;views&apos;,&#160;&apos;watch_min&apos;]].sum() The output is: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;views&#160;&#160;watch_min playlist&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Career&#160;Advice&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 21106 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 607.22 Data&#160;Storytelling&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;33599 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 754.97 Excel&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;349088 &#160;&#160;&#160; 16064.33 Linear&#160;Algebra&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;64940 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 3352.33 Python&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;239396 &#160;&#160;&#160; 12988.73 R&#160;Programming&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 29944 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1226.64 Science&#160;Experiments&#160;&#160;&#160;32514 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1509.77 Statistics&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;44081 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 2604.28 Multiple aggregations on a column: In Excel, applying multiple aggregations requires multiple formulas, manual adjustments, or switching to Pivot Tables or Power Query, while such tasks are as cool as a breeze in Python. You can use agg() to perform multiple aggregations, such as computing different statistics on same column or across multiple columns: df.groupby(&apos;playlist&apos;)[&apos;views&apos;].agg([&apos;sum&apos; ,&#160;&apos;mean&apos;,&#160;&apos;max&apos;]) The output is: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160; sum &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;mean&#160;&#160;&#160; max playlist&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Career&#160;Advice&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;21106 &#160;&#160; 7035.333333 &#160;&#160; 8915 Data&#160;Storytelling&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;33599 &#160;&#160; 8399.750000 &#160;&#160; 9489 Excel&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;349088 &#160;&#160; 5454.500000 &#160;&#160; 9942 Linear&#160;Algebra&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;64940 &#160;&#160; 5411.666667 &#160;&#160; 7949 Python&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;239396 &#160;&#160; 5093.531915 &#160;&#160; 9837 R&#160;Programming&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;29944 &#160;&#160; 5988.800000 &#160;&#160; 8517 Science&#160;Experiments&#160;&#160;&#160;32514 &#160;&#160; 4644.857143 &#160;&#160; 9365 Statistics&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;44081 &#160;&#160; 5510.125000 &#160;&#160; 7690 To apply multiple aggregations across different columns: df.groupby(&apos;playlist&apos;).agg({&apos;views&apos;: &#160; &apos;sum&apos;, &#160; &apos;watch_min&apos;: &#160; &apos;mean&apos;}) And to give meaningful names to resultant columns. The output is: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;total_views&#160;&#160;avg_watch_min playlist&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Career&#160;Advice&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;21106 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 202.406667 Data&#160;Storytelling&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;33599 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 188.742500 Excel&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;349088 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 251.005156 Linear&#160;Algebra&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;64940 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 279.360833 Python&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;239396 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 276.355957 R &#160;Programming&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 29944 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 245.328000 Science&#160;Experiments&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;32514 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 215.681429 Statistics&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;44081 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 325.535000 You can also perform aggregation with custom functions, which makes it super convenient to perform data tasks on the fly. One such example: #&#160;Custom&#160;aggregation&#160;with&#160;lambda&#160;with&#160;clean&#160;custom&#160;names&#160; (df &#160;.groupby( &apos;playlist&apos;)[&apos;views&apos;] &#160;.agg( &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;min_views= &apos;min&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;max_views= &apos;max&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;range_views= lambda &#160;x:&#160;x. max ()&#160;-&#160;x.min()) .reset_index()) The output is: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160; playlist &#160;&#160; min_views &#160;&#160; max_views &#160;&#160; range_views 0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Career &#160; Advice &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 4425&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;8915&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;4490 1 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Data &#160; Storytelling &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 7438&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;9489&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;2051 2 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Excel &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 633 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 9942&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;9309 3 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Linear &#160; Algebra &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 755 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 7949&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;7194 4 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Python &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 509 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 9837&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;9328 5 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; R &#160; Programming &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1424&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;8517&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;7093 6 &#160;&#160; Science &#160; Experiments &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1270&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;9365&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;8095 7 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Statistics &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 3824&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;7690&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;3866 You can also use df.agg() to apply aggregation functions on the entire DataFrame (no grouping), or on a single column directly. You can use df.groupby(col) without specifying an aggregation immediately. This creates groups, which you can explore or iterate over later. This chapter’s code files have many practical, ready-to-use examples, so you do not need to Google common patterns every time! To broadcast group-level stats back to all rows using transform() : If you need to add group-level stats, such as playlist-average views attached to each row 7 , you can use transform() instead of agg() : df[&apos;playlist_avg_views&apos;]&#160;=&#160;df.groupby(&apos;playlist&apos;)[&apos;views&apos;].transform(&apos;mean&apos;) df[[&apos;playlist&apos;,&#160;&apos;views&apos;,&#160;&apos;playlist_avg_views&apos;]].tail() The output is: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160;playlist&#160;&#160;views&#160;&#160;playlist_avg_views Video&#160;Id&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; V_146&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;Python&#160;&#160;&#160;5030&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;5093.531915 V_147&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;R&#160;Programming&#160;&#160;&#160;7381&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;5988.800000 V_148&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;R&#160;Programming&#160;&#160;&#160;7449&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;5988.800000 V_149&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;Statistics&#160;&#160;&#160;6741&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;5510.125000 V_150&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;R&#160;Programming&#160;&#160;&#160;8517&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;5988.800000 Yet another powerful function in pandas is filter() that allows us to refine data dynamically. It can retain or exclude entire groups based on conditions, such as keeping playlists where total views exceed a set threshold. It enables quick selection of rows or columns by matching specific words, like extracting columns containing min in their name. With regex-based pattern matching, it provides precise filtering, making complex data extraction effortless, ideal for tasks that would otherwise require tedious manual selection in Excel. Refer code file for impactful examples. Pivoting and reshaping data Excel users use PivotTables frequently to summarize and explore the data by dragging fields into Rows , Columns , Values , and sometimes using Slicers or connecting multiple tables with the Data Model . Let us see how this can be done in pandas: Simple Pivot Table : To fetch playlist-wise total views, you can use df.pivot_table(values=..., index=..., aggfunc=...) , where values are like Excel&apos;s Values , index represents Rows , and aggfunc is the Value Field Settings : df.pivot_table(values=&apos;views&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; index=&apos;playlist&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;aggfunc= &apos;sum&apos;, margins = True, # To add a totals margins_name = &apos;Grand Total&apos;) As you can see in Figure 6.7 , the results are the same in Python and Excel: Figure 6.7 : Pivot table in Python (left) vs. Excel (right) • &#9; Multi-dimensional Pivot Table : To get playlist vs. published month with the sum of views, you can create a column for published_month in the data and use it as columns , while using &apos;playlist&apos; as index and &apos;views&apos; as values in df.pivot_table() Some combinations may not have any values. In Python, these appear as NaN (similar to Excel’s blank cells). We can replace them with a custom value such as 0 using the fill_value argument: df.pivot_table(values=&apos;views&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; index=&apos;playlist&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;columns= &apos;published_month&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;aggfunc= &apos;sum&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;fill_value= 0)&#160; •&#9; Multiple aggregations : To get the sum and mean of views per playlist, you can pass a list of functions in aggfunc argument: df.pivot_table(values=&apos;views&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; index=&apos;playlist&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;aggfunc=[ &apos;sum&apos;,&#160; &apos;mean&apos;]) It is like adding the same field twice to Values (one Sum, one Average) in Excel. Just like aggfunc , you can provide multiple values for other arguments, such as values , index , or columns in df.pivot_table() . You can also apply different aggregation functions to different columns, such as: df.pivot_table(values=[&apos;views&apos;,&#160;&apos;subs_net&apos;], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; index=&apos;playlist&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;aggfunc={ &apos;subs_net&apos;:&apos;sum&apos;,&#160;&apos;views&apos;:&apos;mean&apos;}) Apart from pivot_table() for structured summary, you have already seen the use of groupbby() for flexible grouping and crosstab() for frequency analysis. You can also use query() , loc() , assign() , etc., with pivot_table() for dynamic filtering, selecting specific data, and adding computed columns to enable dynamic and efficient analysis 8 . Also, for combining multiple tables in aggregation, you can use pandas merging methods as discussed next. In addition, you can use melt() , stack() , and unstack() to reshape your data as you need. For complex, multi-step data transformations, you can explore df.pipe() . It lets you chain together custom functions cleanly, making long processing pipelines easier to read. Combining data sets Excel (even Excel 365) lacks a clean, scalable way to combine datasets. Functions such as VLOOKUP , INDEX-MATCH , or the new dynamic array formulas like FILTER are used to merge data from different tables. However, these tend to be cumbersome and error prone as the volume or complexity of the data grows. Additionally, Excel’s built-in functions struggle with multi-key join and advanced merging logic, making it difficult to handle more sophisticated join conditions often required in SQL. While Power Query supports standard joins, Python allows for more complex merging conditions, such as fuzzy matching, multi-key joins, and conditional joins that go beyond standard inner, left, right, and outer joins. Even experienced Power Query users can benefit from using pandas’ concat , join , and merge , especially for large data, automation, complex workflows, and scalability beyond Excel’s limitations. To replicate code examples, load different sheets of demo_sales.xlsx data with appropriate names and view those to understand how these are related: customers &#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel( &apos;demo_sales.xlsx&apos; ,&#160;sheet_name=&apos;Customers&apos;) orders &#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel( &apos;demo_sales.xlsx&apos;,&#160;sheet_name=&apos;Orders&apos;) products &#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel( &apos;demo_sales.xlsx&apos;,&#160;sheet_name=&apos;Products&apos;) sales_jan &#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel( &apos;demo_sales.xlsx&apos;,&#160;sheet_name=&apos;Sales_Jan&apos; ) sales_feb &#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel( &apos;demo_sales.xlsx&apos;,&#160;sheet_name=&apos;Sales_Feb&apos;) Concatenating Sometimes, you may need to add rows to an existing table. It is like copying rows from one worksheet and pasting them into another. For example, adding sales for the months Jan and Feb as shown in the following figure: Figure 6.8 : sales_jan and sales_feb data You can do it using the concat() function, which joins DataFrames along a particular axis. By default, it stacks data row-wise. The argument ignore_index=True resets the index for the new combined DataFrame: sales_jan_feb&#160;=&#160;pd.concat([sales_jan,&#160;sales_feb],&#160;ignore_index&#160;=&#160;True ) print(sales_jan_feb)&#160; The output is: &#160; &#160;OrderID&#160;ProductID&#160;&#160;Quantity&#160;&#160;Amount 0 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1001&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;P01&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 120 1 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1002&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;P02&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 2 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 250 2 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1003&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;P01&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 175 3 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1004&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;P03&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 3 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 300 4 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1005&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;P02&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 1 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 80 Also, axis = 1 can be used to stack data column-wise, if needed . pd.concat([data1,&#160;data2,&#160;data3,&#160;...]) lets us stack multiple datasets, enabling us to read multiple Excel files and combine them into a single table for analysis. Joining and merging like SQL joins When you want to retrieve data from two or more tables based on logical relationships between the tables, you can perform different types of joins on tables. SQL users might be familiar with these joins. We will join data about orders and customers using CustomerID column from both tables, as follows: Figure 6.9 : Orders and customers DataFrames Let us look at different types of joins: Inner join : It keeps only the matching (common) rows from both datasets. For the given data sets, inner join will keep data for customers with ID C001, C002, and C003: merged_inner&#160;= &#160;pd.merge(orders,&#160;customers,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; on=&apos;CustomerID&apos;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;how =&apos;inner&apos;) print(merged_inner) #&#160;Only&#160;Orders&#160;where&#160;CustomerID&#160;exists&#160;in&#160;both&#160;tables &#160; The output is: &#160;&#160;OrderID&#160;CustomerID&#160;&#160;OrderDate&#160;&#160;Amount&#160;CustomerName&#160;Region 0 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1001&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;C001&#160; 2024-01-05 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 120 &#160;&#160;Alice&#160;Smith&#160;&#160;North 1 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1002&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;C002&#160; 2024-01-06 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 250 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;Bob&#160;Jones&#160;&#160;South 2 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1004&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;C001&#160; 2024-01-08 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 300 &#160;&#160;Alice&#160;Smith&#160;&#160;North 3 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1005&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;C003&#160; 2024-01-09 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 80 &#160;&#160;Carol&#160;White&#160;&#160;&#160;East Left join : It keeps all rows from the first (left) dataset and adds matching data from the second, filling gaps with NaNs if no match exists. This might be useful to analyze sales with customers’ data to see all orders, while adding customers’ details, where available: merged_left&#160;=&#160;pd.merge(orders,&#160;customers,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; on= &apos;CustomerID&apos;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;how= &apos;left&apos;) print(merged_left) &#160; # all&#160;Orders, adding customer details if available The output is: &#160;&#160;OrderID&#160;CustomerID&#160;&#160;OrderDate&#160;&#160;Amount&#160;CustomerName&#160;Region 0 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1001&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;C001&#160; 2024-01-05 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 120 &#160;&#160;Alice&#160;Smith&#160;&#160;North 1 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1002&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;C002&#160; 2024-01-06 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 250 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;Bob&#160;Jones&#160;&#160;South 2 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1003&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;C005&#160; 2024-01-07 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 175 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;NaN 3 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1004&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;C001&#160; 2024-01-08 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 300 &#160;&#160;Alice&#160;Smith&#160;&#160;North 4 &#160;&#160;&#160;O1005&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;C003&#160; 2024-01-09 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; 80 &#160;&#160;Carol&#160;White&#160;&#160;&#160;East Right join : It is the opposite of left join, keeping all rows from the second (right) data and adding matching data from the first. It can help identify customers who have not placed any orders yet: merged_right &#160; = &#160; pd.merge(orders, &#160; customers, &#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; on=&apos;CustomerID&apos;, &#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; how=&apos;right&apos;) merged_right # all customers, adding order details if available The output is: Out[35]: &#160; &#160;&#160; OrderID &#160; CustomerID &#160;&#160; OrderDate &#160;&#160; Amount &#160; CustomerName &#160; Region 0 &#160;&#160;&#160; O1001 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; C001 &#160; 2024-01-05&#160;&#160;&#160;120.0 &#160;&#160; Alice &#160; Smith &#160;&#160; North 1 &#160;&#160;&#160; O1004 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; C001 &#160; 2024-01-08&#160;&#160;&#160;300.0 &#160;&#160; Alice &#160; Smith &#160;&#160; North 2 &#160;&#160;&#160; O1002 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; C002 &#160; 2024-01-06&#160;&#160;&#160;250.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Bob &#160; Jones &#160;&#160; South 3 &#160;&#160;&#160; O1005 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; C003 &#160; 2024-01-09&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;80.0 &#160;&#160; Carol &#160; White &#160;&#160;&#160; East 4 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; C004 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaT &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160; David &#160; Brown &#160;&#160;&#160; West Outer (full) join : It keeps all rows from both datasets, filling blanks where matches do not exist. This can be useful if you are auditing mismatches between lists: merged_outer &#160; = &#160; pd.merge(orders, &#160; customers, &#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; on=&apos;CustomerID&apos;, &#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; how=&apos;outer&apos;) merged_outer # Combining all orders and all customers The output is: Out[37]: &#160; &#160;&#160; OrderID &#160; CustomerID &#160;&#160; OrderDate &#160;&#160; Amount &#160; CustomerName &#160; Region 0 &#160;&#160;&#160; O1001 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; C001 &#160; 2024-01-05&#160;&#160;&#160;120.0 &#160;&#160; Alice &#160; Smith &#160;&#160; North 1 &#160;&#160;&#160; O1004 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; C001 &#160; 2024-01-08&#160;&#160;&#160;300.0 &#160;&#160; Alice &#160; Smith &#160;&#160; North 2 &#160;&#160;&#160; O1002 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; C002 &#160; 2024-01-06&#160;&#160;&#160;250.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; Bob &#160; Jones &#160;&#160; South 3 &#160;&#160;&#160; O1005 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; C003 &#160; 2024-01-09&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;80.0 &#160;&#160; Carol &#160; White &#160;&#160;&#160; East 4 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; C004 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaT &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160; David &#160; Brown &#160;&#160;&#160; West 5 &#160;&#160;&#160; O1003 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; C005 &#160; 2024-01-07&#160;&#160;&#160;175.0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; NaN Cross join : It combines every row from the first dataset with every row from the second, creating all possible pairs (used less often). So, if you want to pair every order with every customer, generating all possible combinations, you can use cross join. pd.merge(orders,&#160;customers,&#160;&#160;how=&apos;cross&apos;) Although rare but might be useful for theoretical scenarios like mapping potential product recommendations for each customer. Also, pd.merge() accepts column names if multiple columns serve as keys in the datasets. Similarly, you can use df.merge() , a chaining style function 9 , which makes it easier to chain multiple data sets, from left to right, such as df1.merge(df2).merge(df3) There are many other ways to combine data sets. You can refer to the pandas official guide ( https://pandas.pydata.org/docs/user_guide/merging.html ) for the same. In short, df.join() merges DataFrames based on their index, while pd.merge() joins them using specific columns, giving more flexibility for complex merges. Conclusion This chapter provided a strong foundation in using pandas to clean, filter, pivot, transform, and summarize datasets efficiently. For further depth, the official pandas documentation and tutorials (at https://pandas.pydata.org/docs/getting_started/overview.html ) offer comprehensive insights. In the next chapter, we will build on this knowledge by introducing additional libraries and tools that simplify and optimize data analysis and bring advanced insights to your datasets with minimal effort. Which three customers brought in the highest total sales? What is the average order value ( AOV ) per customer? (Hint: total sales divided by the number of orders). For each region, what is the best-selling sub-category? Part 5: Shipping efficiency (advanced) Calculate the average delivery time (days between Order Date and Ship Date). Which region has the fastest average shipping time? Congratulations on successfully completing an Exploratory Data Analysis ( EDA ), a fundamental skill essential for every data analyst to master. Does Excel struggle? e.&#9; How would Python’s Pandas library solve these problems more efficiently? 1 pd.crosstab() is not a full pivot table, it quickly summarizes categorical variables (like Excel’s COUNTIFS), but for full pivoting with custom aggregations, we will discuss pivot_table() in detail later in the chapter. 2 Other Pandas specific data types include time delta, Interval, Sparse, and BooleanArray, etc. however these are rarely needed for standard analysis. 3 Most of dataframe methods like these create a modified copy (means return a new object) instead of changing the original DataFrame. To apply changes permanently, you must assign the result back to df, like df = df.method() . 4 In pandas, NaN (from NumPy) represents missing numerical values, while nan may appear due to display formatting but it refers to the same type. 5 The keep argument (‘first’, ‘last’, False) in duplicated() marks duplicates while keeping the original DataFrame unchanged. keep=first or keep=last marks all duplicates except for the first or last occurrence while keep = False marks all duplicates. In drop_duplicates() , this argument works similarly but removes duplicates instead of marking them. keep=first or keep=last drop duplicates except for the first or last occurrence while keep = False drops all duplicates. 6 Similarly, .cumprod() computes cumulative product, useful in compounding or growth calculations 7 agg() reduces groups to one row per group, transform() preserves row count.   - Python-Powered Excel C HAPTER 3 Python Fundamentals Introduction Learning a language always starts with learning the alphabet, small words, basic grammar, and sentence formation before becoming fluent. The same is the case with Python. Learning this programming language starts with numbers, letters, and operators. In this chapter, we will set the stage for your Python journey by exploring the core concepts and building blocks of the language. Aimed at Excel users with little to no coding background, this chapter will not only make you comfortable with Python but also prepare you for more advanced topics in subsequent chapters. By the end of this chapter, you will have the confidence to write your own functions, handling errors gracefully, and using list and dictionary comprehension to write cleaner and more efficient Python code. Additional exercises and usecases are provided in the accompanying code files, and readers are encouraged to practice each topic thoroughly before proceeding to the next, as not all examples are included in the chapters to maintain conciseness. Structure This chapter will cover the following topics: From Excel formulas to Python scripts Working directory Variables in Python Datatypes in Python Typecasting in Python Operators in Python Deep dive on strings Python data structures Control flow for conditions and repeating actions Functions in Python Exception handling in Python Getting help in Python From Excel formulas to Python scripts Excel users have cells, formulas, and logic in their minds, which makes it easier to learn Python. This is because the same logic can be written in Python with super-simple syntax with more power, flexibility, and control. As Excel formulas work on cell references, in Python, you work with variables, allowing for dynamic calculation. In Excel, functions like =SUM(A1:A10) perform operations on ranges, while Python achieves the same with sum(my_list) , offering greater flexibility. Conditional logic, used as =IF(A1&gt;10, &quot;High&quot;, &quot;&quot;) , translates effortlessly into Python’s if x &gt; 10: print(&quot;High&quot;) , making it equally user-friendly. Another example, merging two data sets in Excel is no fun, but Python can do it so gracefully by data1.merge(data2) . Moreover, Python offers great flexibility in defining custom functions for specific tasks as compared to Visual Basic for Applications ( VBA ) or a lambda function (in Excel 365) if you have used those. While Excel’s tables and ranges store data in a grid, Python has many different containers to store data as per the need. Even error handling is analogous. You are familiar with errors like #VALUE! or # DIV/0! , while Python raises ValueError , or ZeroDivisionError , both requiring debugging. By observing the resemblances, you will find Python approachable as well as an exciting, powerful extension of your existing skills. Python as a calculator Y ou might have used Excel to perform quick calculations like =A1+B1 or =A1*10 . You will find Python just as intuitive, if not more flexible. In Excel, calculations are tied to cell references (e.g., A1 and B1), where the formula updates automatically when the referenced cells change. In Python, we achieve the same results using variables instead of cells. For example, assigning a = 10 and b = 2 allows us to perform a+b or a*10 . We can print these values by using the in-built print() function, as you have seen in Chapter 2 , Setting Up Python. Unlike Excel, where operations are confined to a grid, Python calculations work in a free-flowing environment, making it easy to define and reuse values dynamically. By removing the constraints of fixed cell locations, Python enables more complex computations, pushing the envelope of number crunching in Excel. Here is how Python handles basic arithmetic: #&#160;Assigning&#160;values&#160;to&#160;variables a&#160;=&#160;10;&#160;b&#160;=&#160;3 #&#160;Performing&#160;operations print (a&#160;+&#160;b)&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Addition&#160;(10&#160;+&#160;3&#160;=&#160;13) print(a&#160;-&#160;b)&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Subtraction&#160;(10&#160;-&#160;3&#160;=&#160;7) print(a&#160;*&#160;b)&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Multiplication&#160;(10&#160;*&#160;3&#160;=&#160;30) print(a&#160;/&#160;b)&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Division&#160;(10&#160;/&#160;3&#160;=&#160;3.3333333333333335) print(a&#160;**&#160;b)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Exponentiation&#160;(10^3&#160;=&#160;1000) print(a&#160;//&#160;b)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Floor&#160;Division&#160;(Quotient&#160;part&#160;of&#160;the&#160;division&#160;10/&#160;3&#160;=&#160;3) print(a&#160;%&#160;b)&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Modulus&#160;(remainder&#160;of&#160;10&#160;/&#160;3&#160;=&#160;1) Except for the exponentiation or power operator, quotient, and remainder operator, all other mathematical operators are the same as in Excel. Before we discuss more about datatypes and operators in Python, we will discuss variables. There is one more thing, you need to keep your Python files organized before you start doing more coding . Let us look at something useful about using a dedicated folder for all your Python scripts. Working directory So far, you might have already created some Python files in Spyder that save to a default folder 1 ( C:\Users\YourUsername for windows users) an d when you try to open, Spyder looks for the file in the same folder. This folder is known as the working directory 2 . You can verify this path by looking at the File Explorer-like bar at the top right of the Spyder Interface. It shows the same folder. You might want to change it. We would recommend creating a folder with a suitable name, like ‘Excel_Python’ , for this book and setting it as your permanent working directory. There are multiple ways to change the working directory in Spyder: Click the folder icon located in the top-right section of the main Spyder window, as shown in Figure 3.1 and select a new directory. This option is quick, however, you will need to set the working directory in every Spyder session. Try pressing Ctr+ . to restart the kernel and you will see it is back to the default folder. Figure 3.1 : Setting working directory in Spyder Go to Tools | Preferences | Working directory | Startup (At startup, the working directory is:) | The following directory (browse here and choose). Click on Apply and then OK . It will set your desired folder as permanent working directory. You can change it later for your other projects. Figure 3.2 : Setting permanent working directory in Spyder Many times, you would need to write code for getting or setting working directory and it can be done with os module quickly. After importing the module with import os , running os.getcwd() displays the path of current working directory. Similarly, os.chdir() allows you to change working directory to a given path. import&#160;os os.getcwd()&#160;&#160; #&#160;Get&#160;current&#160;directory os.chdir(&quot;D:/Path/That/You&#160;Choose&quot;)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Change&#160;working directory Note that we have used forward slashes (/) in the folder path as this code is written on a Windows machine. In Windows, folder paths show up using backslashes (\) while on Mac or Linux it is forward slash (/). Since backslash (\) is also an escape character in Python (e.g., \n, \t), we usually need to replace each backslash (\) with double backslashes (\\) or a forward slash (/) to avoid warnings, errors, or unintended characters. This can be done through the find and replace feature of the IDE we are using. Alternately, we can use raw strings (r&quot;...&quot;). The r prefix tells Python to treat the string literally, without interpreting backslashes as escape sequences. You will learn more about strings and file paths later. For now, you can refer to the code file for examples. Now that the working directory is set, you are all set to dive into Python fundamentals. Variables in Python In Excel, cells hold some value, and formulas reference those cells for calculations. In Python, variables fit the bill as they store data that can be used and manipulated throughout a script. A variable in Python is a name given to a value, making it easy to reference (like cell reference in Excel) and reuse. In Python, you can create a variable and assign a value to it using the equals sign (=) as you have done earlier with score = 68 . Here, the variable score is like a labelled box that stores the value of 68 . Note: In Python, you do not need to specify the type of data a variable holds (like a number or text). In other words, Python is dynamically typed. You should try to give meaningful names to your variables, like total_sales or product_price , instead of v1 and v2 . Also, there are some naming rules you need to consider: Variable name can include letters, numbers, or underscores, but cannot start with a number. It cannot contain space, or any special character such as %, or $ (e.g., names like marks % or total sales are not allowed). For names containing more than one word, you can use underscore (_), or capitals such as total_sales , MarksPerc . Unlike VBA, Python is case-sensitive ( Revenue and revenue are different in Python). Reserved or keywords such as if , next , for , while , etc. cannot be used as variable names, somewhat similar to Excel, where certain names are restricted or advised to be avoided due to built-in functions or features. (e.g., try to name a sheet as History and it will show an error). Here are some examples for defining the variables: age&#160;=&#160;40 print(age) # 40 #&#160;Invalid&#160;NAMES&#160;(You&apos;ll&#160;get&#160;syntax&#160;error&#160;in&#160;these&#160;examples):&#160; total&#160;sales&#160;=&#160;1200 &#160;&#160; #&#160;Spaces&#160;are&#160;not&#160;allowed&#160; discount%&#160;=&#160;10 &#160;&#160; #&#160;special&#160;characters&#160;(like&#160;%)&#160;are&#160;not&#160;allowed&#160; 1batch&#160;=&#160;500 &#160;&#160; #&#160;Variable&#160;names&#160;cannot&#160;start&#160;with&#160;a&#160;number #&#160;Valid&#160;NAMES:&#160;(For&#160;the&#160;examples&#160;mentioned&#160;above) total_sales&#160;=&#160;1200 &#160; #&#160;Underscores&#160;not &#160;spaces(snake_case,&#160;recommended)&#160;&#160; print (total_sales)&#160;&#160; #&#160;1200&#160;&#160; discount_perc&#160;=&#160;10 &#160;&#160; #&#160;Use&#160;descriptive&#160;names&#160;without&#160;special&#160;characters&#160;&#160; print(discount_perc) batch1&#160;=&#160;500 &#160;&#160; #&#160;Numbers&#160;can&#160;be&#160;used,&#160;but&#160;not&#160;at&#160;the&#160;start&#160;&#160; print(batch1) #&#160;Different&#160;naming&#160;conventions&#160;&#160; average_price&#160;=&#160;550 &#160; #&#160;snake_case&#160;(Recommended for variable names)&#160; AveragePrice&#160;=&#160;550 &#160;&#160; #&#160;PascalCase&#160;&#160;&#160; averagePrice&#160;=&#160;550 &#160;&#160; #&#160;camelCase&#160; #&#160;Changing&#160;data&#160;type&#160;of&#160;a&#160;variable&#160;(allowed&#160;in&#160;Python)&#160;&#160; x&#160;=&#160;10 &#160;&#160; #&#160;x&#160;is&#160;an&#160;integer&#160;&#160; print(x)&#160;&#160; #&#160;10&#160;&#160; x&#160;=&#160;&apos;ten&apos; &#160;&#160; #&#160;Now&#160;x&#160;is&#160;a&#160;string/text&#160;&#160; print(x)&#160;&#160; #&#160;ten&#160;(Python &#160;allows&#160;dynamic&#160;typing)&#160;&#160; At this point, it is recommended for you to type and run all the code yourself and try out more examples to get a better understanding of the topic. For now, you can use Camel Case ( numberOfGraduates ), Pascal Case ( NumberOfGraduates ), or Snake Case ( number_of_graduates ) for variable names. When you grow more comfortable with Python, you can refer to Python’s official style guide (PEP 8) https://peps.python.org/pep-0008/#naming-conventions or Google’s style guide https://google.github.io/styleguide/pyguide.html for naming conventions and coding standards. In Spyder, variables on the last line display their value automatically without needing a print() command. The same happens in Jupyter or Colab notebooks: x&#160;=&#160;10 x #&#160;automatically&#160;prints&#160;the&#160;value&#160;of&#160;x You can use a semicolon ( ; ) to write multiple statements on the same line, like score &#160;=&#160;68;&#160;score&#160;+&#160;2 Also, Python supports multiple assignments, allowing you to assign values to multiple variables in a single line, e.g., a,&#160;b&#160;=&#160;5,&#160;25 Datatypes in Python In Excel, when you enter data, it can be of different datatypes, such as general, number, currency, short and long dates, text, etc. Broadly, you can store data as numbers (integer, fraction, percentage, scientific, etc.), text, or dates. In Python, broadly, data can be of three types: numbers (integer, floats, or complex), strings (similar to text), and Booleans. In Python, date and time are handled differently using an external module that we will discuss separately in Chapter 4 , External Packages and Date-time Handling. Let us understand by creating some variables and checking their datatypes: Integer (int) : In Python, if you type x = 5 , it is treated as an integer ( int ) by default. You can check its datatype by printing type(x) . Python’s type() function is similar but more powerful than Excel’s TYPE() function. Whole numbers (positive, negative or zero), are referred as integers (int) in Python. More examples: quantity&#160;=&#160;10;&#160;type(quantity)&#160;&#160; #int score&#160;=&#160;-5;&#160;type(score)&#160;&#160; #int Key insight: In Excel, handling large numbers can be a real challenge. For example, entering a value like 123456789123456789123456789123456789 in Excel results in significant loss of precision after 15 digits. While Python’s int type grows dynamically, handling numbers of any size without extra configuration. big_number&#160;=&#160;123456789123456789123456789123456789 print(big_number)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Works&#160;seamlessly in Python Note: Excel internally stores all numbers as floating-point values, even if they appear as integers. Unlike Python, which distinguishes between int and float, Excel treats all numbers as floats. Floats : Now, try running 10/5 in your Python IDE, and you will get 2.0 , which is basically float (real numbers in math) datatype. Numbers with decimal point are treated as floats in Python, for example, 3.14, -0.001 . x&#160;=&#160;10/5 ;&#160;x&#160; #&#160;2.0 print(type(x)) #&#160;float price&#160;=&#160;999.99;&#160;print(type(price)) #&#160;float Python stores floats like Excel (up to a 15-digit limit) based on IEEE 754 standards. However, higher precision for floating-point numbers can be achieved by using decimal module in Python. It is very rare that you might need it, though. Note: Excel often rounds off or formats float numbers silently, whereas Python exposes the raw floating-point behavior (which is a good thing for transparency and control). Unlike Excel, Python does not hide tiny floating-point inaccuracies. For example, 0.1 + 0.2 may give 0.30000000000000004 instead of 0.3. This is normal behavior in most programming languages and ensures full precision. For applications requiring exact decimal precision, such as financial calculations, Python provides the built-in decimal module. Boolean (True or False) : Just like Excel has TRUE and FALSE , Python uses Boolean ( bool ) values to represent logical conditions (we will discuss those shortly): in_stock&#160;=&#160;True;&#160;print(type(in_stock)) #&#160;True 10 &#160;&gt;&#160; 20 &#160; #&#160;False Strings : In Excel, text entries like Product A are stored as General or Text format. In Python, text is represented as a string ( str ) and must be enclosed in single or double quotes. Inside quotation marks, even if you write any number, that will be treated as a string : product_name&#160;=&#160;&quot;Laptop&quot; ;&#160;product_name&#160; #&apos;Laptop&apos; type(product_name)&#160; #&#160;str score&#160;=&#160;&apos;Twenty&apos;;&#160;score&#160; #&#160;&apos;Twenty&apos; type(score)&#160; #&#160;str In Python, working with multi-line strings or strings that contain variables is much simpler as compared to VBA: msg&#160;=&#160;&apos;&apos;&apos;Hi,&#160;I&#160;am&#160;Dr&#160;Nisha.&#160;I&#160;welcome&#160;you&#160;all&#160;to&#160;learn&#160;Python&#160;for&#160;Excel. Learning&#160;Python &#160;is&#160;easy&#160;and&#160;using&#160;it&#160;with&#160;Excel&#160;is&#160;pure-fun! Keep&#160;learning!&apos;&apos;&apos; print(msg)&#160; Output : Hi,&#160;I&#160;am&#160;Dr&#160;Nisha.&#160;I&#160;welcome&#160;you&#160;all &#160;to&#160;learn&#160;Python&#160; for &#160;Excel. Learning&#160;Python&#160;is &#160;easy&#160; and &#160;using&#160;it&#160; with &#160;Excel&#160; is &#160;pure-fun! Keep&#160;learning! We will discuss more about strings in the section Deep dive on string of this chapter. Dates : Excel stores dates as serial numbers (e.g., 1-Jan-2025 is stored as 45658 ). While in Python, dates are handled properly using the datetime module, which allows for date and time manipulation. Refer to Chapter 4 , External Packages and Date-time Handling , for details on this. from &#160;datetime&#160; import &#160;date order_date&#160;=&#160;date(2025 ,&#160;1,&#160;1)&#160; #&#160;date() function similar&#160;to&#160;Excel’s DATE() print(order_date)&#160; #&#160;2025-01-01 print(&apos;current_date:&#160;&apos;,&#160;date.today()) #&#160;Similar&#160;to&#160;Excel’s&#160;TODAY() #&#160;prints&#160;current&#160;date&#160;in&#160;yyyy-mm-dd&#160;format NoneType in Python : In Excel , an empty cell is simply blank , but its behavior depends on how it is used. Some formulas treat it as 0 , others as an empty string ( &quot;&quot; ). In Python , the equivalent concept is None , which represents the absence of a value. Unlike Excel&apos;s blanks, None is a distinct datatype ( NoneType ) and does not behave like 0 or an empty string in calculations. When you import Excel, text, or CSV data into Python, blank cells or #N/A values are treated differently depending on the library you use. For example, as None (No value or empty), NaN (Not a Number or missing), or empty strings. You will learn all of this in detail later, just know that a few things work differently in Python compared to Excel, especially when it comes to math operations, so a little caution goes a long way. Python also supports complex datatype and fraction datatype from fraction module you can learn more about these types at: (Python’s Official Document here https://docs.python.org/3/library/stdtypes.html ). Typecasting in Python In Excel, datatypes are inferred automatically. For example, entering 100 , stores it as a number, while “100” is treated as text. Excel can also interpret numbers with / as dates (this is sometimes annoying). In Excel, you can convert datatypes manually or using some functions like VALUE() or TEXT() . In Python, changing datatype is much more convenient and we call it typecasting . Sometimes Python automatically changes data types during operations (implicit typecasting). To deliberately change types (which is known as explicit typecasting), we use the following built-in functions: int() : Converts to an integer float() : Converts to a float/decimal number str() : Converts to a string (text) bool() : Converts to a boolean ( True or False ) Converting to numbers You can convert text to int or float if the text contains a valid numeric value. While VALUE() function in Excel can create issues due to hidden characters, formatting inconsistencies, or encoding issues, Python’s int() and float() work seamlessly: sales&#160;=&#160;&quot;500&quot; #&#160;Stored&#160;as&#160;a&#160;string&#160;(text) type(sales) #&#160;str sales_num&#160;=&#160;int(sales) #&#160;Convert&#160;to&#160;an integer type(sales_num) #&#160;int print(sales_num&#160;*&#160;2)&#160;&#160; #&#160;1000&#160;(works&#160;like&#160;a&#160;number&#160;now) age&#160;=&#160;&quot;&#160;&#160;30&#160;&#160;&quot; type(age) #&#160;str int(age)&#160; #&#160;30 float(age)&#160; #&#160;30.0 You can also convert Boolean to numbers (int or float). It allows you to seamlessly use Boolean values in mathematical operations, logical expressions, and even indexing without extra conversions. In Python , True converts to 1 , and False converts to 0 : passed&#160;=&#160;True;&#160;int(passed)&#160; #&#160;1 present&#160;=&#160;False;&#160;int (present)&#160; #&#160;0 Converting to Bools In Excel, TRUE and FALSE are treated as Boolean values , but if stored as text ( &quot;TRUE&quot; ), you need extra steps to convert them, such as IF(A1=&quot;TRUE&quot;, TRUE, FALSE) . In Python, bool() helps in such conversions: status_text&#160;=&#160;&quot;True&quot; &#160;;&#160; bool(status_text)&#160;&#160; #&#160;True&#160;(converted to boolean) bool(&quot;False&quot;)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;True as it converts any non-empty string to True bool(&quot;&quot;)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;False&#160;as&#160;only&#160;empty&#160;string&#160;is&#160;converted to False Warning: bool(&quot;False&quot;) still returns True because any non-empty string is considered True in Python. To properly convert &quot;True&quot; or &quot;False&quot; text values, you can use. status_bool&#160;=&#160;status_text.lower()&#160;==&#160;&quot;true&quot; In Excel, any nonzero number is treated as TRUE . Python follows the same logic but with a simpler implementation: bool(10)&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;True bool(0)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;False You will later see that Python can even convert arrays like list, set, tuple, dictionary, etc., to Boolean. Everything, except empty array converts to True . Similarly, str() can be used to convert values into text for display, logging, or file output. Now that you are familiar with Python datatypes and conversions, let us explore how to use various operators to manipulate them. Operators in Python In Excel, formulas use operators to perform calculations, compare values, or apply logic. You have already seen arithmetic operators (Refer to the section From Excel formulas to Python scripts in this chapter) like +, -, *, and / in Python, which work similarly to Excel. Let us discuss other common operators and how they work in Python. Comparison or relationship operators Just as Excel allows comparisons like =A1&gt;B1 , Python uses comparison operators to check relationships between values, returning True or False . Most comparison operators work the same way in Excel and Python, except for equal to ( = ) and not equal to ( &lt;&gt; ) in Excel: price1&#160;=&#160;500;&#160;price2&#160;=&#160;700 &#160;&#160; print(price1&#160;&gt;&#160;price2)&#160;&#160; #&#160;False&#160;(500&#160;is&#160;not&#160;greater&#160;than&#160;700) print(price1&#160;==&#160;500)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;True&#160;(equal&#160;to&#160;500,&#160;similar&#160;to&#160;A1=500&#160;in&#160;Excel) print(price1&#160;!=&#160;price2)&#160; #&#160;True&#160;(similar&#160;to&#160;A1&lt;&gt;B1&#160;in&#160;Excel) Logical operators Excel’s =AND(A1&gt;20, B1&lt;500 ) checks whether both conditions are true simultaneously and =OR(A1&gt;20, B1&lt;500) checks whether at least one condition is true. In Python, we use logical operators ( and , or , not ) in the same way. Excel uses AND() , OR() , and NOT() as functions, while Python uses and , or , and not as keywords without parentheses: stock&#160;=&#160;50;&#160;price&#160;=&#160;200 &#160;&#160; print(stock&#160;&gt;&#160; 20 &#160; and &#160;price&#160;&lt;&#160; 500)&#160;&#160; #&#160;True&#160;(Both&#160;conditions&#160;met) print(stock&#160;&gt;&#160;100 &#160; or &#160;price&#160;&lt;&#160; 500)&#160;&#160; #&#160;True&#160;(At&#160;least&#160;one&#160;condition&#160;met) print(not &#160;(price&#160;&gt;&#160; 500))&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;True&#160;(Reverses&#160;the&#160;condition) Key insight: In Python, logical operators use shortcircuit evaluation, meaning they stop checking as soon as the result is determined. This is beneficial because it avoids unnecessary calculations (and even prevents errors, like dividing by zero), unlike Excel, which always evaluates every condition. False &#160; and &#160; 1/0 &#160; #&#160;False True &#160; or &#160; 1/ 0 &#160; #&#160;True In Excel, =AND(FALSE,1/0) and =OR(TRUE, 1/0) results in #DIV/0! error. Membership operators In Excel, you might check if a value exists in a column using COUNTIF(A:A, &quot;Laptop&quot;) &gt; 0 . In Python, we use in and not in to check if a value exists in a list: products&#160;=&#160;[&quot;Laptop&quot;,&#160;&quot;Phone&quot;,&#160;&quot;Tablet&quot;]&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Laptop&quot; &#160; in &#160;products)&#160;&#160; #&#160;True&#160;(Found&#160;in&#160;the&#160;list) print(&quot;Camera&quot; &#160; not &#160; in &#160;products)&#160;&#160; #&#160;True&#160;(Not&#160;in&#160;the&#160;list) By understanding these operators, Excel users can easily translate their spreadsheet logic into Python scripts. Table 3.1 lists Python operators and their Excel equivalents where they differ: Operator categor y Operator name Python example Excel equivalent Description or comment Arithmetic Integer division a // b =QUOTIENT(A1, B1) Excel has a function, not an operator Modulo (Remainder) a % b =MOD(A1, B1) Exponentiation a ** b =A1 ^ B1 Raises a to the power of b Comparison Equal to a == b =A1 = B1 Checks if values are equal Not equal to a != b =A1 &lt;&gt; B1 Checks if values are not equal Logical AND a and b (supports short circuit evaluation) AND(A1, B1) Excel has a function while Python has an operator OR a or b (supports short circuit evaluation) OR(A1, B1) NOT not a NOT(A1) Membership In a in apple No direct Excel equivalent Checks if item exists in a sequence (text or range) Not in a not in apple No direct Excel equivalent Checks if item does not exist in the sequence Assignment Assignment x = 10 x = 10 (in VBA only) Assigns value Compound assignment x += 5 No direct Excel equivalent Add and assign in one step Identity Is a is b Is (in VBA only) Checks if two variables point to the same object Is not a is not b IsNot (in VBA only) Checks if two variables point to the same object Logical or bitwise equivalence Eqv No direct equivalent needed Expr1 Eqv Expr2 (VBA Only) Rarely used in practice Table 3.1 : Python’s operators vs. Excel’s operators Deep dive on strings While discussing datatypes, we have introduced Python’s strings as text datatype, for example, &apos;Excel&apos; , or &apos;123&apos; . However, strings in Python can also be considered as a sequence of characters, so Excel can be treated as a collection of 5 characters or strings: E , x , c , e , and l . Other basic datatypes that we discussed so far (numbers or bools) represent a single value, hence are called scalers , while strings can also be treated as a collection of values, hence are also called aggregate datatype or data structure . Python’s core datatypes are shown in Figure 3.3 . We will discuss other aggregate datatypes in the next section. Figure 3.3 : Python datatypes Working with text data in Excel often feels limiting. With Excel’s text functions like LEFT(), RIGHT() , MID() , and TEXT() , handling complex text manipulation such as splitting full names, cleaning inconsistent data, extracting patterns, or combining text across multiple cells quickly turns unwieldy. Even newer additions in Excel 2021/365 like TEXTSPLIT() , TEXTBEFORE() , TEXTAFTER() , and TEXTJOIN() often fall short when tasks become more complex or large-scale. For advanced text extraction, cleaning up, bulk operations, or conditional string replacement, Excel users switch to VBA. However, the syntax becomes too lengthy and hard to maintain, particularly when you need the functionality across multiple workbooks. Even Power Query, while powerful, has a learning curve and is not always intuitive for quick one-off tasks. Performance issues also arise with very large text datasets, and there is a stringent cell character limit of 32,767 characters, of which only 1,024 characters are visible directly in the cell grid. This limitation reflects Excel’s design as a spreadsheet application, optimized for structured data and calculations rather than large-scale text processing. By contrast, Python does not impose a practical limit on string length beyond the available system memory. And Python’s string handling solves these pain points efficiently. With concise, readable code, you can easily clean, transform, and analyze text data, whether it is standardizing product names, parsing emails, or reformatting dates. Python makes what feels like a workaround in Excel into a smooth, flexible solution, saving time and reducing errors, especially when working with large datasets. This positions Python suitable for handling extensive text datasets, bulk operations, and advanced manipulations such as regular expressions, conditional replacements, and natural language processing. We have discussed that a Python string is simply text data, enclosed in single quotes ( &apos; &apos; ), double quotes ( &quot; &quot; ). When you need to include quotes within the text itself, you can use different types of quotes to avoid confusion: book &#160;=&#160; &quot;Python-Powered Excel&quot;;&#160;print(book) book &#160;=&#160; &apos;Python-Powered Excel&apos;;&#160; print(book) s1&#160;=&apos;&quot;Python-Powered&#160;Excel&quot;&#160;is&#160;my favorite&#160;book.&apos;;&#160;print(s1) s2&#160;=&#160;&quot;The&#160;book&#160;&apos;Python-Powered&#160;Excel&apos;&#160;is practical yet simple.&quot;;&#160;print(s2) To conserve chapter length, many outputs are not shown; run the code stepwise and observe the console for full results. Additionally, you can use escape sequences to include special characters or quotes within your strings. An escape sequence is a backslash ( \ ) followed by the character you want to include. For instance, to include a double quote inside a double-quoted string or a single quote inside a single-quoted string, you can use \&quot; or \&apos; respectively: s3&#160;=&#160;&quot;He&#160;said,&#160;\&quot;Python-Powered&#160;Excel\&quot;&#160;is&#160;a&#160;great&#160;book.&quot;;&#160; print(s3)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; s4&#160;=&#160;&apos;It\&apos;s&#160;a&#160;wonderful&#160;book&#160;on Excel-Python &#160;integration.&apos; ;&#160;print(s4) The output is: He&#160;said,&#160;&quot;Python-Powered&#160;Excel&quot;&#160;is&#160;a&#160;great&#160;book. It&apos;s&#160;a&#160;wonderful&#160;book&#160;on&#160;Excel-Python&#160;integration. Escape sequences can also be used to include special characters like new lines or tabs within strings. E.g., \n for a new line and \t for a tab: using_tab&#160;=&#160;&apos;tool:\tPython&apos; using_new_line&#160;=&#160;&apos;tools:\nPython\nExcel&apos; You should print to see the output. In the next example, we are showing both \t and \n : info&#160;=&#160;&apos;Book:\tPython-Powered&#160;Excel\nAuthor:\tDr&#160;Nisha&#160;Arora\nPublisher:\tBPB&#160;Publications&apos; print(info) The output is: Book:&#160;Python-Powered&#160;Excel Author:&#160;Dr&#160;Nisha&#160;Arora Publisher:&#160;BPB&#160;Publications This way, you can include special characters and quotes within your strings without any issues. Essential string operations The len() function returns the total length of a string, including all characters and spaces. For example, len(&apos;Nisha&#160;Arora&apos;) will return 11 , as it counts every character, including the space between &quot;Nisha&quot; and &quot;Arora&quot; . Changing case in Python is similar to Excel, as shown in the following code: text&#160;=&#160;&quot;Hello,&#160;Excel&#160;Users!&quot; print(text.upper())&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;HELLO,&#160;EXCEL&#160;USERS! print(text.title())&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Hello,&#160;Excel&#160;Users! print(text.lower())&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;hello,&#160;excel&#160;users! print(text.capitalize())&#160; #&#160;Hello,&#160;excel&#160;users! Removing extra spaces in Python is easier, even for leading or trailing spaces: text&#160;&#160;=&#160;&quot;&#160;&#160;Learning&#160;Python &#160;is&#160;Fun!&#160;&#160; &quot; print(text.strip())&#160; #&#160;Removes&#160;both&#160;leading&#160;and&#160;trailing&#160;spaces text.lstrip()&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Removes&#160;only&#160;leading&#160;(left)&#160;spaces text.rstrip()&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Removes&#160;only&#160;trailing&#160;(right)&#160;spaces Replacing text, finding the position of a character in the given text are like Excel functions (Refer to the code file for examples). Python directly supports counting occurrences of a character using count() . For example: email&#160;=&#160;&quot;user@example.com&quot; print(email.count(&quot;@&quot;))&#160;&#160; #&#160;1 print(email.find(&quot;@&quot;))&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;4&#160;(index _ More on this soon!) Splitting strings based on any separator is simpler, as shown in the following code: text&#160;=&#160;&apos;Python-Powered&#160;Excel&apos; print(text.split())&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;[&apos;Python-Powered&apos;,&#160;&apos;Excel&apos;] print(text.split(sep=&apos;-&apos;))&#160; #&#160;[&apos;Python&apos;,&#160;&apos;Powered&#160;Excel&apos;] text&#160;=&#160;&apos;Nisha&#160;Arora&apos; text.split()&#160; #&#160;Output&#160;is&#160;a&#160;list,&#160;can&#160;be&#160;further&#160;sliced. [Refer Next Topic] text.split()[0]&#160; #&#160;Nisha In Excel 2021 or 365, you can use TEXTSPLIT() for the same, but older versions need a combination of LEFT() , LEN() , MID() , and FIND() , etc. The startswith() and endswith() methods 3 in Python, are great for validating strings. Excel 365 introduced similar functions TEXTBEFORE , and TEXTAFTER , however, older versions of Excel need a workaround for these tasks: filename&#160;=&#160;&quot;report.xlsx&quot; print(filename.endswith(&quot;.xlsx&quot;))&#160;&#160; #&#160;True print(filename.startswith(&quot;report&quot;))&#160; #&#160;True String concatenation in Python is more versatile and efficient, compared to Excel, with easier integration of variables and complex expressions: folder&#160;=&#160; &quot;C:/Documents&quot; filename&#160;=&#160;&quot;report.xlsx&quot; path&#160;=&#160;folder&#160;+&#160;&quot;/&quot; &#160;+&#160;filename&#160; print(path)&#160;&#160; #&#160;C:/Documents/report.xlsx While concatenation of strings can be done using the + operator, for more dynamic and readable text, especially when mixing variables, numbers, or calculations, Python offers formatted strings ( f-strings ). These are like having Excel’s TEXT() and concatenation combined in one smooth step. Inserting a variable or performing calculations inside the string is super easy: cl&#160;=&#160;140 ;&#160;imp&#160;=&#160;800 print(f&quot;Clicks:&#160;{cl},&#160;Impressions:&#160;{imp}&quot;)&#160; #&#160;Clicks:&#160;140,&#160;Impressions:&#160;800 print(f&quot;Click&#160;Through&#160;Rate&#160;(CTR):&#160;{cl/imp:.2%}&quot;) #&#160;Click&#160;Through&#160;Rate&#160;(CTR):&#160;17.50% You can even use function calls inside the string. Another much-needed functionality, Python offers to remove prefix or suffix quickly: name&#160;=&#160;&quot;Ms&#160;Nisha&#160;Arora,&#160;PhD&quot; print(name.removeprefix(&quot;Ms&#160;&quot;))&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Nisha&#160;Arora,&#160;PhD print(name.removesuffix(&quot;,&#160;PhD&quot;))&#160; #&#160;Ms&#160;Nisha&#160;Arora #&#160;Great&#160;thing&#160;is&#160;you&#160;can&#160;chain&#160;together&#160;string&#160;methods cleaned&#160;=&#160;name.removeprefix(&quot;Ms&#160;&quot;).removesuffix(&quot;,&#160;PhD&quot; ) print(cleaned)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;&#160;Nisha&#160;Arora Indexing and slicing strings Another amazing feature of Python’s strings is that you can directly access individual characters and substrings using indexing. In Python, indexing refers to accessing an element of a sequence (like a string or list) using its position. Python counts positions from left to right, starting at 0 (Refer to Figure 3.4 ), unlike VBA, where the first character is indexed as 1 : Figure 3.4 : Indexing in Python Python also supports negative index to count from right to left, starting from -1 . So, the last element of the sequence is indexed -1 . To fetch any character from a string, you need to use square brackets: text&#160;=&#160;&apos;Python&apos; text[0]&#160; #&#160;first&#160;character text[3]&#160; #&#160;index&#160;3,&#160;i.e.,&#160;4th&#160;character text[-1]&#160; #&#160;last&#160;character text[-2]&#160; #&#160;index&#160;2&#160;from&#160;the&#160;end,&#160;second&#160;last&#160;character If you need to extract a substring from a string, it is called slicing, and it can be done using a range of indices. Python goes beyond simple indexing. You can grab slices (ranges) of text easily. Basic syntax is string[start:stop] where: Start : The position where the slice begins (inclusive). Stop : The position where the slice ends (exclusive). string[a:b] will slice string starting at index a , and extracting until index (b-1) : name&#160;=&#160;&quot;Microsoft&#160;Excel&quot; print(name[0:9 ])&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Microsoft&#160;(characters&#160;indexed from&#160;0&#160;to&#160;8) print(name[:9])&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;same&#160;as&#160;before&#160;as&#160;it&#160;starts&#160;from&#160;index&#160;‘0’,&#160;if&#160;we&#160;don’t&#160;mention&#160;explicitly print(name[10:])&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Excel&#160;(from&#160;index&#160;10&#160;to&#160;end) print(name[-5:])&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Excel&#160;(from&#160;5th&#160;last&#160;or&#160;index&#160;-5&#160;till&#160;end) You can even skip characters by slicing with step string[start:stop:step] as shown: print(name[0:9:2])&#160; #&#160;Mcoot&#160;-&gt;&#160;every&#160;alternate&#160;chracter&#160;from&#160;index&#160;0&#160;to&#160;8 print(name[::2])&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;McootEcl&#160;-&gt;&#160;every&#160;alternate&#160;character print(name[::-1])&#160;&#160; #&#160;lecxE&#160;tfosorciM&#160;-&gt;&#160;negative step reverses the string In Python, strings are immutable, meaning you cannot change the original string directly. You might have noticed that methods like strip() , replace() , and removeprefix() , etc., do not modify the string itself. Instead, they create and return a new string with the changes applied. If you want to keep the updated version, you must assign it to a variable: text&#160;=&#160;&quot;I&#160;love&#160;Python&quot; new_text&#160;=&#160;text.replace(&apos;Python&apos;,&#160;&apos;Excel&apos;) print(new_text)&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;I&#160;love&#160;Excel print(text)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;I&#160;love&#160;Python Here, text remains unchanged unless you reassign it. With just a few simple string methods (and with the knowledge of control flow that you will learn soon), Python empowers you to handle text far beyond what Excel formulas and VBA can do for data cleaning and file management. Mastering strings is a key step to boost your Excel skills with Python. At this moment, we recommend practicing string methods before reading further. More examples and use-cases are mentioned in the code file. Python data structures Strings are powerful, but when you need to store data for multiple people or items like IDs, names, or records, you need something more. Just like you use rows, columns, and tables in Excel, Python uses data structures like lists, tuples, sets, dictionaries, and even strings to organize collections of values. We call them data structures or aggregate datatypes. Let us understand these aggregate datatypes in detail. Lists In Excel, you store multiple values, such as the prices of ten products, in a column range like A1:A10. Other options are to create an array in VBA or use Excel’s (2021 or 365) dynamic array functionality for better sorting, filtering, etc. In Python, you can store multiple values in a list (among other options). A list in Python is: Flexible : You can include any type of data in it, even a list itself. Mutable : Modifiable, means it allows you to add, remove, or replace elements. Indexable : You can fetch elements by indexing or slicing. Ordered : Elements stay in the same sequence as they were added. You can create a list by putting different elements (any datatype) separated by commas inside square bracket: products&#160;=&#160;[&apos;mobile&apos;,&#160;&apos;laptop&apos;,&#160;&apos;tablet&apos; ,&#160;&apos;mobile&apos;];&#160;products&#160; #&#160;[&apos;mobile&apos;,&#160;&apos;laptop&apos;,&#160;&apos;tablet&apos;,&#160;&apos;mobile&apos;] stock&#160;=&#160;[120,&#160;38,&#160;55];&#160;stock&#160; #[120,&#160;38,&#160;55] demo&#160;=&#160;[&apos;Nisha&apos;,&#160;40,&#160;True];&#160;demo&#160; #&#160;[&apos;Nisha&apos;,&#160;40,&#160;True] In the preceding examples, you must have noticed that a list can include mixed datatypes, and duplicates are also allowed.
