The following examples show other properties of a list: #&#160;Define&#160;a&#160;list&#160;of&#160;prices&#160;for&#160;4&#160;products prices&#160;=&#160;[19.99,&#160;29.95,&#160;9.99,&#160;14.49,&#160;44.99] #&#160;Add&#160;a&#160;new&#160;price&#160;to&#160;the&#160;list prices.append(34.99);&#160;prices&#160; #&#160;[19.99,&#160;29.95,&#160;9.99,&#160;14.49,&#160;44.99,&#160;34.99] #&#160;Remove&#160;a&#160;price&#160;from&#160;the&#160;list prices.remove( 14.49);&#160;prices&#160; #&#160;[19.99,&#160;29.95,&#160;9.99,&#160;44.99,&#160;34.99] #&#160;Change&#160;the&#160;price&#160;of&#160;the&#160;first&#160;product prices[0]&#160;=&#160;21.99;&#160;prices&#160; #&#160;[21.99,&#160;29.95,&#160;9.99,&#160;44.99,&#160;34.99] #&#160;Sort&#160;the&#160;list&#160;in&#160;ascending/descending&#160;order sorted(prices)&#160; #&#160;[9.99,&#160;21.99,&#160;29.95,&#160;34.99,&#160;44.99] sorted(prices,&#160;reverse=True)&#160; #&#160;[44.99,&#160;34.99,&#160;29.95,&#160;21.99,&#160;9.99] In above-mentioned code, we have created a simple list of numbers and performed basic operations to add a new element using append() method or remove the existing element using remove() method. This is allowed as lists are mutable. We then indexed the list by using square bracket sub-setting (indexing and slicing work the same way it works for strings and tuples). We have also updated the price of the first product, because such operations are feasible with mutable data structures. As a list is an ordered entity, we can sort it (ascending or descending for a list of numbers or alphabetical order for a list of texts) too. You can join two lists by simply using a + sign, which creates a brand new list or extend() method, which extends the original list. For more detailed explanation and examples, refer to code file in the GitHub repo: #&#160;Merging&#160;monthly&#160;sales&#160;data jan_sales&#160;=&#160;[12000,&#160;15000] feb_sales&#160;=&#160;[18000,&#160;16000] mar_sales&#160;=&#160;[ 20000,&#160;18000] q1_sales&#160;=&#160;jan_sales&#160;+&#160;feb_sales&#160;+&#160;mar_sales q1_sales&#160; #&#160;[12000,&#160;15000,&#160;18000,&#160;16000,&#160;20000,&#160;18000] #&#160;Appending&#160;New&#160;Hires&#160;to&#160;an&#160;Existing&#160;Employee&#160;List employees&#160;=&#160;[&apos;Amit&apos;,&#160;&apos;Priya&apos;,&#160;&apos;John&apos;] new_joinees&#160;=&#160;[&apos;Sara&apos;,&#160;&apos;Leo&apos;] employees.extend(new_joinees) print(employees)&#160; #&#160;[&apos;Amit&apos;,&#160;&apos;Priya&apos;,&#160;&apos;John&apos;,&#160;&apos;Sara&apos;,&#160;&apos;Leo&apos;] Lists also allow you to create a nested data structure for tabular data, as given in the following figure: Figure 3.5 : Sample data In Excel, you use tables, range, or a dynamic array to store 2-dim data like this. Let us create such data in Python using a nested list (list or lists): #&#160;A&#160;list&#160;of&#160;lists&#160;representing&#160;a&#160;table&#160;of&#160;product&#160;details product_details&#160;=&#160;[ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ &quot;Product&#160;A&quot;,&#160;19.99,&#160;10], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ &quot;Product&#160;B&quot;,&#160;29.95,&#160; 5], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ &quot;Product&#160;C&quot;,&#160;9.99,&#160;15] ] #&#160;Accessing&#160;elements&#160;in&#160;the&#160;nested&#160;list print(product_details[0])&#160;&#160; #&#160;[&apos;Product A&apos;, 19.99, 10]&#160;_ Product A details print(product_details[1][ 1])&#160;&#160; #&#160;29.95 _ Price&#160;of&#160;Product&#160;B #&#160;Modifying&#160;an&#160;element&#160;in&#160;the&#160;nested&#160;list product_details[2][2]&#160;=&#160;20 &#160;&#160; #&#160;Change&#160;the&#160;stock&#160;of&#160;Product&#160;C&#160;to&#160;20 #&#160;Adding&#160;a&#160;new&#160;product&#160;to&#160;the&#160;nested&#160;list product_details.append([&quot;Product&#160;D&quot;,&#160;14.49,&#160; 8]) If you are comfortable with Excel’s dynamic arrays (Excel 2021 or 365), you will find Python lists even more powerful and flexible. Tuples A tuple is like a list but is immutable , which means once created, it cannot be modified . Tuples are useful to protect the data from edits. For example, coordinates, fixed settings, or functions that expect constant values. You can create a tuple directly, putting elements, and separating values by commas, or you can use parentheses for the same. Indexing is the same as you have done in strings or lists, and there are fewer methods available for tuples, as you can simply not add, remove, or change any element once the tuple is created: #&#160;Creating&#160;a&#160;tuple&#160;by&#160;using&#160;()&#160;constructor coordinates&#160;=&#160;(10,&#160;20);&#160;coordinates&#160;&#160; #&#160;(10,&#160;20) #&#160;You&#160;can&#160;also&#160;create&#160;a&#160;tuple&#160;directly&#160;(sep&#160;values&#160;by&#160;a&#160;comma) coordinates&#160;=&#160;10,&#160;20&#160;;&#160;coordinates&#160;&#160; #&#160;(10,&#160;20) #&#160;Or&#160;you&#160;can&#160;convert&#160;a&#160;list&#160;to&#160;tuple coordinates&#160;=&#160;tuple([10,20]);&#160;coordinates&#160;&#160; #&#160;(10,&#160;20)&#160; #&#160;As&#160;tuple&#160;is&#160;immutable,&#160;you&#160;can&apos;t&#160;add/remove&#160;or&#160;change&#160;elements #&#160;However,&#160;you&#160;indexing/slicing&#160;is&#160;allowed&#160;as&#160;it&#160;is&#160;ordered&#160;and&#160;indexable coordinates[0]&#160; #&#160;10 coordinates[1]&#160; #&#160;20 #&#160;append(),&#160;remove(),&#160;pop(),&#160;insert&#160;or&#160;other&#160;similar&#160;methods&#160;are&#160;not&#160;available&#160;for&#160;tuples You can also create nested tuples (refer to the code file for more examples). Dictionaries In Excel, you store structured data in rows and columns, where each column has a header, and data is organized in rows. You use lookup functions, such as VLOOKUP , INDEX , and MATCH , or XLOOKUP (Excel 2021/365) to retrieve specific data points based on criteria. In Python, you can achieve similar functionality using dictionaries 4 ( dict ). Dictionaries store data as key-value pairs, which is analogous to how columns and rows are structured in Excel. You can access values corresponding to specific keys via indexing: #&#160;Create&#160;a&#160;dictionary&#160;by&#160;enclosing&#160;a&#160;comma-separated&#160;key-value&#160;pairs&#160;in&#160;{}. Use&#160;‘:’ to&#160;separate&#160;each&#160;key&#160;from&#160;its&#160;associated&#160;value. employees&#160;=&#160;{&quot;E101&quot;:&#160;&quot;Prateek&quot;,&#160;&quot;E102&quot;:&#160;&quot;Pragya&quot;} employees #&#160;LOOKUP&#160;Employee&#160;Name&#160;by&#160;Employee&#160;Id employees.get(&quot;E101&quot;)&#160; # It&#160;safely&#160;handles&#160;missing&#160;keys&#160;(You&#160;can&#160;print&#160;a&#160;msg&#160;as&#160;you&#160;prefer) employees.get(&quot;E103&quot;,&#160;&quot;Not&#160;Found&quot;) Additionally, you can easily merge two or more dictionaries with Python: #&#160;Merging&#160;two&#160;dictionaries&#160;(Much&#160;easier&#160;than&#160;Excel) overwrites, if key repeats &#160; &#9; products_prices&#160;=&#160;{ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Apple&quot;:&#160;120, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Banana&quot;:&#160;60 } new_prices&#160;=&#160;{ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Mango&quot;:&#160;280, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Banana&quot;:&#160;100 } updated&#160;=&#160;products_prices&#160;|&#160;new_prices;&#160;updated # Python 3.9+ #&#160;{&apos;Apple&apos;:&#160;120,&#160;&apos;Banana&apos;:&#160;100,&#160;&apos;Mango&apos;:&#160;280} # |= updates the dictionary in place by merging another dictionary products_prices |= new_prices; products_prices #&#160;{&apos;Apple&apos;:&#160;120,&#160;&apos;Banana&apos;:&#160;100,&#160;&apos;Mango&apos;:&#160;280} You can also use the unpacking operator for merging dictionaries: updated&#160;=&#160;{**products_prices,&#160;**new_prices} print(updated)&#160; #&#160;{&apos;Apple&apos;:&#160;120,&#160;&apos;Banana&apos;:&#160;100,&#160;&apos;Mango&apos;:&#160;280} The above-mentioned methods create new merged dictionary, while update method modifies the original dictionary in place. Note: Dictionaries require unique keys. When merging dictionaries, duplicate keys are resolved by last-write-wins, i.e., the value from the rightmost dictionary overwrites earlier ones . Sets A set in Python is an unordered collection of unique values. This is like Excel’s UNIQUE() function , which removes duplicate values from a range: #&#160;You&#160;can&#160;create&#160;a&#160;set&#160;by&#160;using&#160;curly&#160;braces&#160;{}&#160;or&#160;set()&#160;function. numbers&#160;=&#160;[1 ,&#160;2,&#160;2,&#160;3,&#160;3,&#160;3] unique_numbers&#160;=&#160;set(numbers) print(unique_numbers)&#160;&#160; #&#160;{1,&#160;2,&#160;3} product_id&#160;=&#160;{&apos;g1001&apos;,&#160; &apos;g1002&apos;,&#160;&apos;g1002&apos;} product_id&#160; #&#160;{&apos;g1001&apos;,&#160;&apos;g1002&apos;}&#160;#&#160;Keeps&#160;unique&#160;element&#160;only Sets are particularly useful for set-theoretic operations such as union, intersection, and difference. In Excel, achieving these operations requires some workarounds. Warning: Sets are unordered in Python, meaning they do not maintain the original order of values. If the order matters, use a list or a NumPy array (to be discussed later in the book) instead. Before moving to the next topic, we recommend practicing Python data structures, different methods, and indexing. By understanding Python’s data structures in terms of Excel’s familiar features, such as dynamic arrays, tables, lookup, etc., learning Python becomes much easier and more intuitive: Python Excel equivalent Description/Use case with simple Python code List Vertical or horizontal range, array Ordered collection of values (can be mixed types) such as [Apple, Banana, 50, 30] Tuple Constant range (Locked for edits) Immutable list (useful for fixed data) such as (Jan, Feb, Mar) Dictionary Named range or lookup table Key-value pairs, e.g., {Apple: 120, Banana: 60} Set Not directly available Unordered collection with unique elements such as {A, B, C} String Cell with text Work as a collection of values, much more powerful e.g., Hello .upper().strip() gives the output HELLO None Blank cell or #N/A Represents missing or no value, e.g., x = None DataFrame (pandas) 5 Table or structured range Table with labeled columns and rows (like Excel table) e.g., pd.DataFrame(products) Table 3.2 : A quick summary of Python data structures Beyond the core datatypes, readers can explore advanced structures such as OrderedDict, frozenset, namedtuple, dataclass, and composite patterns like lists of dictionaries or dictionaries of lists, as these broaden practical understanding. Control flow for conditions and repeating actions You must have used IF() , IFS() , and conditional formatting in Excel to perform logic-based calculations and highlight specific data. You might even use loops and conditions in VBA to automate repetitive tasks. Python takes this idea further with clear, readable structures for decision-making and repetition, what we call control flow . Control flow allows your code to react to perform specific tasks based on different conditions, and repeat actions, making your Python script smarter and more dynamic. The three main control flow tools in Python are shown in Figure 3.6 : Figure 3.6 : Control flow in Python Conditional statements in Python To apply logic to your data, such as do something when sales meet the target or inventory reaches a minimum level, you can use Python&apos;s if , elif , and else keywords. The details are as follows: if statement : Python will check if the condition is true. If so, it runs statement1 . Ensure you add a colon after the condition and indent the next line (your IDE will help). The pseudo-code is: if&#160;condition: &#160;&#160;statement1 Always use consistent spaces (usually 4) to indent code blocks and never mix spaces and tabs. Incorrect indentation will cause syntax errors, but your IDE usually helps you automatically indenting the code. sales&#160;=&#160;1200 # Try changing values of this variable &amp; running the code again if &#160;sales&#160;&gt;&#160;1000: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Eligible&#160;for&#160;bonus&quot;) It is good practice to test the code with different input values to ensure the correctness of the logic. You might notice for lower sales values (upto 1000), no message is printed. This is because we did not include the else block in the code. Let us see it in action. if-else statement : The if-else statement allows you to execute one block of code if a condition is true and another block if it is false. The pseudo-code is: if&#160;condition: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;statement1 else: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;statement2 This example prints Profit when revenue is greater than 1000, otherwise it prints Loss : revenue&#160;=&#160;950 # Try changing values of this variable &amp; notice the output if &#160;revenue&#160;&gt;&#160;1000: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Profit&quot;) else: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Loss&quot;) if-elif-else statement : The if-elif-else statement lets you handle multiple conditions in a readable and efficient way, unlike Excel&apos;s nested IF() functions. You can add multiple conditions using elif without making it hard on the eyes. The pseudo-code is: if&#160;condition1: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; statement1 elif&#160;condition2: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; statement2 else: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; statement3 For example, to measure the performance of salesmen based on the number of sales they make, you can use multiple elif blocks as needed: # Example: Sale performance analysis sales&#160;=&#160;1500 if &#160;sales&#160;&gt;&#160;2000: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;performance&#160;=&#160; &quot;Excellent&quot; elif &#160;sales&#160;&gt;&#160;1500: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;performance&#160;=&#160; &quot;Good&quot; elif &#160;sales&#160;&gt;&#160;1000: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;performance&#160;=&#160; &quot;Average&quot; else: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;performance&#160;=&#160; &quot;Needs&#160;Improvement&quot; Nested if-else statements : Nested if-else statements are used when you need to make multiple layers of decisions. While if-elif-else is often sufficient, there are scenarios where nested conditions provide better control and precision, especially when you need to check sub-conditions within a primary condition. Here is the pseudo-code : if&#160;condition1: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if&#160;condition2: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; statement1 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; else: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; statement2 else: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; statement3 For example, for user authentication, if you need to check both username and password to authenticate a user, you can write nested conditions like this: username&#160;=&#160;&quot;admin&quot;; password&#160;=&#160;&quot;password123&quot; if &#160;username&#160;==&#160; &quot;admin&quot;: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;password&#160;==&#160; &quot;password123&quot;: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Access&#160;granted.&quot;) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; else: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Incorrect&#160;password.&quot;) else: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Unknown&#160;user.&quot;) You can also use shorthand notation, that is one-liner code for simple conditionals, for example: sales&#160;=&#160;1200 if&#160;sales&#160;&gt;&#160;1000:&#160;print(&quot;Eligible&#160;for&#160;bonus&quot;)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Eligible&#160;for&#160;bonus revenue&#160;=&#160;950 &quot;Profit&quot; &#160;if&#160;revenue&#160;&gt;&#160;1000&#160; else &#160; &quot;Loss&quot; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;&apos;Loss&apos; Note: While shorthand notation is neat, avoid overusing it for complex conditions. It can hamper the readability, just like a messy nested IF formula in Excel. Iterative and transfer statements in Python In Excel, repetitive tasks are often done by dragging formulas, using helper columns or using VBA. Excel 365’s BYROW() and BYCOL() functions with LAMBDA() bring loop-like behavior, but Python offers a simpler and more powerful way to handle such tasks. In Python, there are two types of loops: for loops while loops Use a for loop when you know how many times you want to repeat something, like looping n times, looping through rows of data or a list of products. Use a while loop when you want to repeat an action until a condition is met , like checking stock levels until they are all sold out. Examples are a great way to grasp a concept, so let us begin with a few simple ones. The for loop You are familiar with Python’s core data structures (or aggregate datatypes), such as string, list, tuple, dictionary, or a set that is used to hold multiple data points. Using the for loop you can perform a task for each data point of an iterable or collection. An iterable is anything in Python you can go through one item at a time, like aggregate datatypes, or even a range (we have a range() function that produces a sequence of numbers), just like dragging down a formula over a range of cells in Excel. There are many other iterables in Python that you will come across while working with Python. The syntax is similar to conditional statements with a slight change, as you can see: for &#160;variable&#160;in&#160; iterable: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Do&#160;something&#160;with&#160;variable If you have a list of scores and you want to give a bonus score (+2) to each participant, you can basically write a for loop (as you want to repeat this operation for each score) like this: #&#160;Adding&#160;Bonus&#160;score&#160;(+2)&#160;for&#160;each&#160;participant score_list&#160;=&#160;[23,&#160;39,&#160;46,&#160;38,&#160;27] print(&apos;After&#160;adding&#160;Bonus,&#160;Scores are:&#160;\n&apos; ) # Just adding context for &#160;score&#160;in&#160;score_list:&#160; &#9; # Iterating for every element of the list &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(score&#160;+&#160;2)&#9;&#9; # performing +2 every iteration For each number in score_list (we are referring to those as score , but you can call them anything you wish), it prints the sum of that number and 2. You might have understood what it does just by reading the code. Code indentation is something unique to Python that reduces cognitive load by avoiding guesswork, as we can simply see it. In the following example, the product list has the names of fruits, but in different cases. By iterating through each product, we are converting each item’s name to lowercase: #&#160;Printing&#160;each&#160;product&#160;name&#160;in&#160;lower&#160;case&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; products&#160;=&#160;[ “Apple”,&#160;“banana”,&#160;“MANGO”,&#160;“ORANGE”] print(&apos;After&#160;text&#160;cleaning,&#160;product&#160;names:&#160;\n&apos;) for &#160;item&#160;in&#160;products: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(item.lower())&#160;&#160; From a product price dictionary, if you want to find products that are priced above 100, you can loop with condition. The code is written by indenting the code block as per logic: #&#160;Print&#160;products&#160;priced&#160;above&#160;100&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; prices&#160;=&#160;{ &quot;Apple&quot;:&#160;120,&#160;&quot;Banana&quot;:&#160;60,&#160;&quot;Mango&quot;:&#160;280,&#160;&quot;Orange&quot;:&#160;145} for&#160;product,&#160;price&#160;in&#160;prices.items(): # first iterate through the dictionary &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;if&#160;price&#160;&gt;&#160;100: &#9;&#9;&#9; # Then check condition &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;print(product,&#160; &quot;is&#160;premium&#160;product&quot;) # print if condition is True It will print results for all products for which the price is greater than 100 ( Apple, Mango, and Orange ). If there is a requirement to only print the first product that meets the requirement and stop after the first match is found, this can simply be done by using the break statement. In Python, break , continue , and pass are known as transfer statements, as these keywords transfer the flow within the loop or code block: #&#160;Find&#160;the&#160;first&#160;product&#160;over&#160;₹100 for &#160;product,&#160;price&#160;in&#160;prices.items(): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;price&#160;&gt;&#160; 100: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print( &quot;Found:&quot;,&#160;product) # Found:&#160;Apple (1 st product with price&gt;100 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; break &#160;&#160; #&#160;Stops&#160;after&#160;first&#160;match, basically loop ends The break statement allows the loop to exit immediately, while continue statement skips the current iteration and moves to the next: #&#160;Print&#160;only&#160;items&#160;under&#160;₹150 for &#160;product,&#160;price&#160; in &#160;prices.items(): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;price&#160;&gt;&#160; 150: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; continue &#160;&#160; #&#160;Skip&#160;expensive&#160;items (priced &gt; 150) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(product,&#160;&quot;is&#160;affordable&quot;) The statement pass does nothing and is used as a placeholder when a statement is syntactically required. For example, when we are not sure yet what to write inside a loop or function, but cannot leave it blank, as Python will throw an error, we use pass as a proxy: for &#160; product, &#160; price&#160;in&#160;prices.items(): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160; price &#160; &gt; &#160; 150: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; pass &#160;&#160; #&#160;Not&#160;Sure&#160;what&#160;to&#160;do&#160;yet, will add code later Similarly, you can also loop through nested lists (or other similar iterables). One such example is: #&#160;Looping&#160;through&#160;rows&#160;of&#160;a&#160;table&#160;(nested&#160;list)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; product_data&#160;=&#160;[ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ “Apple”,&#160; 120], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ “Banana”,&#160;60], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ “Mango”,&#160;280] ] for &#160;row&#160; in &#160;product_data: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;name,&#160;price&#160;=&#160;row &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print( f&quot;{name}:&#160;₹{price}&quot;)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Like&#160;reading&#160;rows&#160;from&#160;Excel In all the preceding examples, we have iterated over different data structures. Sometimes, we need to repeat some operation a fixed number of times (say five times). We can do this using the in-built iterable range(start, stop, step) . The range() 6 function generates a sequence of numbers , commonly used for looping a specific number of times. By default, it creates a sequence of numbers starting from 0 up to one less than the specified stop value. For example, range(5) constructs a range object containing numbers 0 to 4 , but it does not display numbers directly , you can convert it to a list to see the values: range(5)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;range(0,&#160;5) list(range(5))&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;[0,&#160;1,&#160;2,&#160;3,&#160;4] #&#160;You&#160;can&#160;also&#160;use&#160;it&#160;with&#160;start&#160;and&#160;step: list(range (start=1,&#160;stop=10,&#160;step=2))&#160;&#160; #&#160;[1,&#160;3,&#160;5,&#160;7,&#160;9] Using range() in loops is as intuitive as using a list: #&#160;Send&#160;3&#160;reminder&#160;messages for &#160;i&#160; in &#160; range(1,&#160; 4): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;Reminder&#160;{i}:&#160;Please&#160;submit&#160;your&#160;report.&quot;) You can also refer to code files to explore examples related to other powerful functions, such as enumerate() and zip() . We will now discuss the while loop. We use it when we do not know in advance how many times an operation is to be repeated. The while loop The while loop allows repeated until a condition is no longer True . The pseudo-syntax is: while &#160;condition: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;code&#160;to&#160;run&#160;repeatedly The code keeps running if the condition is always True , which can basically become an infinite loop , good enough to crash your machine or freeze your program. Always ensure the loop condition, in due course, terminates, or use a break statement to stop running the loop. If by mistake, you have run an infinite loop, you can manually interrupt the code by using the stop button in Spyder interface (Console) or hitting the Esc key in Colab. Some use-cases of while loop are: #&#160;Wait&#160;for&#160;Correct&#160;Password_&#160;Try&#160;giving&#160;different&#160;inputs&#160;to&#160;the&#160;prompt password&#160;=&#160;&quot;&quot; while &#160;password&#160;!=&#160; &quot;open123&quot;: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;password&#160;=&#160; input(&quot;Enter&#160;password:&#160;&quot;) print(&quot;Access&#160;granted!&quot;) # This will run when condition is met &amp; loop ends #&#160;Countdown&#160;Timer count&#160;=&#160;5 while &#160;count&#160;&gt;&#160; 0: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Countdown:&quot;,&#160;count) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;count&#160;-=&#160; 1 print(&quot;Go!&quot;) List and dictionary comprehension You have learned to use loops to perform an operation repeatedly. Many times, you can achieve the same by using comprehensions too, often in just one line of code. Once you master loops and conditional statements, you can learn about list and dictionary comprehension. For now, this topic is not mandatory, but it will help you make your code shorter, faster, and easier to read. Feel free to skip and move to the next one if you find it overwhelming. To create a list of squares of numbers from 1 to 5, we can start with an empty list and then iteratively append the square of each number within the specified range: squares&#160;=&#160;[] for &#160;x&#160;in&#160; range(1,&#160;6): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;squares. append(x**2) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(squares)&#160;&#160;#&#160;Output:&#160;[1,&#160;4,&#160;9 ,&#160;16,&#160;25]&#160;&#160; This can easily be done using list comprehension in one line. Let us understand how it works. To create a list using list comprehension, the pseudo-code is: [expression&#160;for &#160;item&#160; in &#160;iterable] You already understand iterable (for example, aggregate datatype or range() ), the line for &#160;item&#160; in &#160;iterable basically is to iterate over each element of that iterable (as you do in a for loop). And expression means the statement which says what to do with each item (e.g., item + 2 , or item.upper() , etc.). Using list comprehension for our example to create a list of squares of numbers from 1 to 5: [x**2 &#160; for &#160;x&#160; in &#160; range(1,&#160;6)]&#160; #&#160;[1,&#160;4,&#160;9,&#160;16,&#160;25] You might have noticed that using list comprehension is efficient and concise. When we are to introduce conditions in loops, we can achieve the same by slightly changing the code to accommodate the if condition: [expression&#160;for &#160;item&#160; in &#160;iterable&#160; if &#160;condition] Here again: item : The current value from the iterable. iterable : The original list, range or any iterable. condition : A filter only includes items that meet this. For example, to create a list of squares of even numbers (numbers that are divisible by 2) from 1 to 10, we can write a loop with the condition: squares_even&#160;=&#160;[] for &#160;x&#160;in&#160; range(1,&#160;11): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;x% 2 &#160;==&#160; 0: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;squares_even. append (x**2) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(squares_even)&#160; #&#160;[4,&#160;16,&#160;36,&#160;64,&#160;100] However, this can be done in one line by using list comprehension as shown here: [x**2 &#160; for &#160;x&#160; in &#160; range(1,&#160;11 )&#160;if &#160;x&#160;%&#160; 2 &#160;==&#160; 0]&#160; #&#160;[4,&#160;16,&#160;36,&#160;64,&#160;100] Just like list comprehension, dictionary comprehension helps create a dictionary (key-value pairs) in a concise one-liner : {key_expr:&#160;value_expr&#160;for &#160;item&#160; in &#160;iterable&#160; if &#160;condition} You might have guessed that here, key_expr indicates what to use as the key, and value_expr indicates what to use as the value. squares&#160;=&#160;{x:&#160;x**2 &#160; for &#160;x&#160; in &#160; range(1,&#160;6)} print(squares)&#160; #&#160;{1:&#160;1,&#160;2:&#160;4,&#160;3:&#160;9,&#160;4:&#160;16,&#160;5:&#160;25} To turn a list of products and prices into a dictionary: products&#160;=&#160;[&quot;Apple&quot;,&#160;&quot;Banana&quot;,&#160;&quot;Mango&quot;] prices&#160;=&#160;[120,&#160;60,&#160;280] product_price&#160;=&#160;{p:&#160;pr &#160; for &#160;p,&#160; pr &#160; in &#160;zip(products,&#160;prices)} print (product_price) # &#160;{&apos;Apple&apos;:&#160;120,&#160;&apos;Banana&apos;:&#160;60,&#160;&apos;Mango&apos;:&#160;280} Functions in Python Suppose you have written some code to clean your text data (maybe assume a list of strings for now) using string methods (convert to lower case, remove white spaces, split by a separator, etc.) in a Python script. Later, you came across another text data (another such list), where you need to do the same cleaning process. You would copy-paste the code with the changed name of the data or list, and you are done. Now imagine if you need to do the same process multiple times in the same script, or maybe in different scripts for different data values. You would need something better than copy-pasting. Python reusability comes to rescue and allows you to wrap your logic in a bundle called function and re-use anywhere in your script, even in other scripts too. Python functions offer more advanced reusability, readability, debugging, and maintainability compared to Excel 365’s LAMBDA functions. The functions that we create are called user-defined functions, and we are already familiar with built-in functions such as type(), len(), range() , etc. You can run ?function_name to get a sneak peek of what that function can do, the parameters it takes, and how to use it. Creating simple functions and docstring Using def keyword, you can create a function that takes some input (or no input) and performs a task as needed. The pseudo-syntax is as follows, you can notice the use of colon and indentation as we have used in control structure: def &#160; function_name( input): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Code&#160;block&#160;(what&#160;the&#160;function&#160;does) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; return &#160;result&#160;&#160; #&#160;Optional (with a default of None) While writing or creating a function, we should give it a meaningful name so that we can use this name to call the function whenever needed. Let us start by creating a simple welcome function (with no input parameters) that prints a welcome message. Even though this function is not a useful one, but if we want to add a documentation of what it does, we can add a quick explanation (called docstring ) in triple code inside the body of the function, as shown in the following code: def&#160;welcome(): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;&quot;&quot;&quot;This function &#160; prints &#160; a &#160; welcome &#160; msg&quot;&quot;&quot; &#160; #&#160;←&#160;docstring here, It’s Optional &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Hello, &#160; welcome &#160; to &#160; Python!&quot;) Adding a docstring to your function allows you to use ?function_name to get a quick document of the same. So, you can add it when you think it might be useful. #&#160;A&#160;quick&#160;way&#160;to&#160;inspect/get&#160;help&#160;on&#160;a&#160;function ?welcome &#160; To use this function, you can call it by its name followed by parenthesis: # Calling the function (The Parenthesis is needed, even if there’s no input) welcome() &#160; # Hello, welcome to Python Functions with one or more input parameter(s) In the previous example, the function does not take any input parameter (or argument) 7 and simply prints a message. You can write functions that take input(s) and perform some operations on the same: #&#160;Function&#160;with&#160;one&#160;or&#160;more&#160;inputs def &#160; add_10(a): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(a&#160;+&#160;10 )&#160; #&#160;prints&#160;input value&#160;+&#160;10 add_10(20)&#160; #&#160;30 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; def &#160; add_num(a,&#160;b): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; return &#160;a&#160;+&#160;b result&#160;=&#160;add_num(5,&#160; 15);&#160;print(result) &#160; #&#160;20 The print and return statements You might have noticed the use of print and return in different Python functions. Understanding the difference between the two helps clarify how functions work. In fact, it is not print vs. return as many Python beginners think. Every Python function gives back something. It returns a specific value, if you use return statement or it returns None , if no return statement is provided. So, return is always there, visible or invisible. You can use print() function anytime to show something on the screen, irrespective of whether you have used the return statement or not. For example, the function add_10() does not have a return statement, so it returns None , and because it has a print statement, it prints the result every time you call the function with a valid input. You can not store and use the value from add_10() function: x&#160;=&#160;add_10(20);&#160;print(x)&#160; #&#160;None x+50&#160; #&#160;Error While add_num() function has an explicit return statement, which allows the function to return the sum, that can be saved in a variable (e.g., result variable here) to be used anywhere in the script. You should use print statement when you only want to show something temporarily or debug what is happening inside your function, e.g., to show (not to save) total revenue generated, you can create show_total function which prints price*qty but does not return anything: def &#160; show_total(price,&#160;qty): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;total_rev&#160;=&#160;price&#160;*&#160;qty &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Total Revenue&#160;is:&quot;,&#160;total_rev)&#160; #&#160;Only&#160;shows&#160;it, doesn’t save it show_total(50, 2) # It just prints the value If you try to save the result, and print, it displays None : result = show_total(50, 2) # prints again as the function is called print(result) # None -&gt;there’s nothing stored_ func has no return statement If you want to store, reuse, or pass the result elsewhere in your program, you need to use a return statement like this: def &#160; get_total(price,&#160;qty): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; return &#160;price&#160;*&#160;qty&#160;&#160; #&#160;Can&#160;use&#160;the&#160;result&#160;later as it’s saved Here, the get_total function returns price*qty , and it can be saved in a variable, let us say, total , which can be used in the script later. total&#160;=&#160;get_total(50 ,&#160;2) print(&quot;Total revenue is:&quot;,&#160;total)&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Reusable Keyword and positional arguments So far, you have written functions to take some input parameters, and while calling the function, you need to supply exactly those many parameters, such as the preceding two functions need exactly two parameters (for price and qty ), no more, no less. Also, note that the order of parameters is important. When you supply two inputs, 50 and 2, Python treats the first value, 50, as the first parameter, price , and the second value, 2, as the second one, qty . While using functions with multiple inputs (imagine a function with more than 10 inputs), the user might not recall or be aware of the order of arguments. In that case, the user can call the function by supplying inputs in any order, but by using the names of the arguments: def &#160;balance(income,&#160;expense): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; return &#160;income&#160;-&#160;expense print(balance(5000,&#160;3500))&#160;&#160; #&#160;1500&#160;(Profit&#160;or&#160;balance) print(balance(3500,&#160;5000))&#160;&#160; #&#160;-1500&#160;(Loss) balance(expense&#160;=&#160;3500,&#160;income&#160;=&#160;5000)&#160; #&#160;1500 _ you can write in any order You must have noticed that if you specify name of the parameters while calling the function, you can write arguments in any order (known as keyword arguments), and while calling function without mentioning the names of the arguments, you must pass the arguments in the order of the parameters, and these are called positional arguments. Positional and keyword arguments are basically names given to arguments that are defined by their position or key(name). A function call may include both positional and keyword arguments simultaneously, with the important rule that keyword arguments must always follow positional arguments. balance(5000,&#160;expense=3500)&#160; #&#160;1500 balance(expense=3500,&#160;5000)&#160;&#160;#&#160;SyntaxError Warning: If you use positional arguments after keyword arguments, Python throws an error, ‘SyntaxError: positional argument follows keyword argument’. Function with default value of parameter(s) You can also set a default value for the parameters like this: def &#160; greet(name=&quot;friend&quot;): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;Hello,&#160;{name}!&quot;) greet()&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Hello,&#160;friend!&#160;-&gt;&#160;Uses&#160;default greet(&quot;Nisha&quot;)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Hello,&#160;Nisha!&#160;-&gt;&#160;Uses&#160;input While defining the function, you must write parameter(s) with default value(s), after parameter(s) without default(s). If you do otherwise, you will get ‘SyntaxError: parameter without a default follows parameter with a default’ : def &#160; balance(expense, income=100): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; return &#160;income&#160;–&#160;expense # Works fine def &#160; balance(income=100 ,&#160;expense): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; return &#160;income&#160;–&#160;expense # SyntaxError In short, Python requires that parameters with default values come after those without, and keyword arguments must come after positional arguments. Functions with an arbitrary number of input(s) Remember, add_num function to add two numbers. Now, if you have a task to add many numbers from an iterable (but you do not know how many) and you want to write a function that works for any number of input parameters, you can use *args . Let us look at an example, followed by reading the details: def &#160; total_bill (*items): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; return &#160; sum(items) print(total_bill())&#160; #&#160;0 Works with no input print(total_bill(100,&#160;200,&#160;50))&#160; # 350 Works with 3 inputs print(total_bill(100,&#160;200,&#160;50, 120))&#160; # 470 Also works with 4 inputs Using an asterisk ( * ) before the parameter name allows the function to work with an arbitrary number of arguments, i.e., you can call the function with any number of input parameters or even with no input. This example may not be particularly useful, but it provides a general idea of writing a function with an arbitrary number of inputs. Likewise, passing multiple named arguments can be done using **kwargs , i.e., putting ** before the argument name: def &#160; product_info (**details): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; for &#160;key,&#160;value&#160; in &#160;details.items(): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; pr int(f&quot;{key}:&#160;{value}&quot;) product_info(name=&quot;Laptop&quot;,&#160;price= 50000) product_info(name=&quot;Laptop&quot;,&#160;price=50000,&#160;stock=10) In these examples, *args (short for Argument) and **kwargs (abbreviated for Key-Word Argument) are the names used by convention. You can use any other name as well, the asterisk operators * and ** do the job of unpacking arguments and keyword arguments. The powerful Lambda functions If you have used Excel 365’s LAMBDA function to write small formulas on the fly, and you are thinking of that functionality in Python. We have got you covered. Let us show you one-liner lambda functions that make our code elegant. Lambda functions are particularly useful when combined with filter, map, reduce, zip, or list comprehension. To write a LAMBDA function in Excel 365 that adds 10 to a number, you run =LAMBDA(x,&#160;x&#160;+&#160;10)(5) and it returns 15. Or you can use Name Manager to save it and use it anywhere in the same worksheet or workbook . However, its scope does not extend beyond it. In Python, you can save a lambda function simply by assigning it to a variable and use it in the same script. You can also share functions in other scripts (you will learn it later). add_10&#160;=&#160;lambda &#160;x:&#160;x&#160;+&#160; 10 print(add_10( 5))&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;15 Lambda functions are also called anonymous functions because they can be defined inline without assigning a name. For example: (discount&#160;=&#160;lambda &#160;price:&#160;price&#160;*&#160; 0.9)(100) &#160;&#160; #&#160;90.0 If you want to perform some operation (like the preceding one) on each element of the list, you can either write a loop, or use list comprehension, or maybe use a list-map in combination with lambda function: prices&#160;=&#160;[100,&#160;250,&#160; 450, 390, 120] discounted_prices&#160;=&#160;list(map(lambda &#160;p:&#160;p&#160;*&#160; 0.9,&#160;prices)) print(discounted_prices)&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;[90.0,&#160;225.0,&#160;405.0, 351.0, 108.0] This is like applying a formula to the entire column in Excel without needing to drag cells. Other powerful use-cases are shared in the code file. E.g., Lambda functions are convenient for filtering data: list(filter(lambda&#160;p:&#160;p&gt; 300,&#160;prices))&#160; #&#160;[450,&#160;390] Optional Tech Insight: In Python, everything is an object. This includes the data you use and even the functions you write. That is why you will often see things like data.method() (e.g., name.upper() or list.append() ). Behind the scenes, Python organizes these objects using classes. These concepts are taught in object-oriented programming (OOP). Exception handling in Python So far, you might have written a lot of code and probably also solved some of the practice problems. We are sure you might have encountered errors . Yes, let us admit, it is unavoidable to get errors when we write code or do math. Even an experienced coder or a math professor gets errors all the time, therefore it might be a little challenging for a beginner. Let us make this simple for you. Understanding your errors will help you debug your code easily, and catching (that is the term we use for handling the errors that we might encounter beforehand) the errors will allow your script to run smoothly. In Excel, we often come across different types of errors, and we use functions like IFERROR() , ISNA() , ISERR() , ISERROR() , or nested IF() functions to manage those. In Python, broadly there are two types of errors: syntax error and exception error. As you must have met both of those, we recommend you figure out what causes a syntax error vs. other type of errors. When you forget to put a colon in a loop, use wrong indentation, or parenthesis mismatch, you get a syntax error . This type of error occurs when the parser detects an incorrect statement. Many times, your syntax is correct, but still your code runs into an error, for example, you are dividing a number by zero, trying to convert invalid text to a number, printing the variable that does not exist, or fetching the n th element of a sequence that has less than n elements. In such examples, your syntax might be correct, but you are trying to do something that is not feasible. You will get errors such as ZeroDivisionError , ValueError , NameError , IndexError , respectively. We refer to such errors as exception errors . There is one more type of error, you can observe, logical error , which is hard to debug. When the program runs but produces incorrect or unexpected results due to flaws in the program&apos;s logic, user need to check and correct the logic, e.g., adding values which should be multiplied. In Python, exceptions can be caught and handled using try-except or extended forms like try-except-else-finally . You can also raise errors with assert or by defining custom exceptions. For now, let us focus on using try-except for error handling with these examples: x1&#160;=&#160;100;&#160;x2&#160;=&#160;20 try: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(x1/x2)&#160;&#160; except: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&apos;Something&#160;went&#160;wrong!&apos;)&#160; In the current example, when x1 = 100 and x2 = 20 , it prints x1/x2 (i.e., 5.0 ). But if you change x2 to 0 , this code will not break, it will simply print a message &apos;Something&#160;went&#160;wrong!&apos; (you can and you should write a meaningful message here). Code break is the term we use to refer to any interruption in the flow of code, caused by errors or manual stops. While performing a division operation, you might already be suspecting ZeroDivisionError , in that case, you can handle that specific error as: # To catch ZeroDivisionError try: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;result&#160;=&#160; 100/0 &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(result) except &#160;ZeroDivisionError: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Oops!&#160;Can&apos;t&#160;divide&#160;by&#160;zero.&quot;)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Safe&#160;fallback While fetching an element from a list, you might get IndexError if you use the incorrect index, so you can catch that with a useful message: try: &#160;&#160; print(data[3]) except &#160;IndexError: &#160;&#160; print(&quot;You&apos;ve&#160;supplied&#160;wrong&#160;index&quot;) Also, you got NameError: name data is not defined . Actually, you forgot to create a list named data in the first place. It happens, sometimes, you run into errors that you could not think of in advance. To safeguard your code for some cases, you can add an additional except block like this: try: &#160;&#160; print(data[3]) except &#160;IndexError: &#160;&#160; print(&quot;You&apos;ve&#160;supplied&#160;wrong&#160;index&quot;) except: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&apos;Something&#160;else&#160;went&#160;wrong!&apos;) Now, if Python does not find the list data in the environment, you will simply print Something else went wrong! and will not show any error. You can surely catch multiple errors with additional except block(s): data&#160;=&#160;[0,&#160;1,&#160;2] del &#160;data&#160; #&#160;Uncomment/comment&#160;this&#160;line&#160;for&#160;different&#160;results try: &#160;&#160; print(data[3]) except &#160;NameError: &#160;&#160; print(&apos;There&#160;is&#160;no&#160;such&#160;list&#160;in&#160;your&#160;enviornment&apos;) except &#160;IndexError: &#160;&#160; print(&quot;You&apos;ve&#160;supplied&#160;wrong&#160;index&quot;)&#160;&#160; Learning to handle errors elegantly will empower you to write code that does not break. You can write your loops with try-except for cleaner and maintainable code that is easier to debug: #&#160;Converting&#160;text&#160;to&#160;numbers data&#160;=&#160;[&quot;100&quot;,&#160;&quot;250&quot;,&#160;&quot;abc&quot;,&#160;&quot;400&quot;] for &#160;value&#160; in &#160;data: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; try: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;num&#160;=&#160; int(value)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Try&#160;converting&#160;to&#160;integer &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Converted:&quot;,&#160;num) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; except &#160;ValueError: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;Skipped&#160;invalid&#160;value:&#160;{value}&quot;) There are a few more examples in the code file that you might want to practice because even in the world of AI, you still need to practice a lot if you are willing to master a subject. AI can assist you, but you do not want to be totally dependent on it for writing simple code. This chapter will help you get comfortable with the various fundamentals and aid you in quickly grasping advanced topics in subsequent chapters. We would recommend that you attempt practice exercises (available with solutions in the GitHub repo) yourself without looking at the solutions. For that, sometimes, you might need additional help. Let us share different ways to get help in Python. Getting help in Python As a beginner, you would often need help to understand a new function, its arguments, or to figure out the logic to get the task done. Python offers multiple ways to get quick help: help() : To get detailed docstrings such as help(len) or help(str.upper) dir() : See all available methods such as dir(str) type(obj) : Know what you are working with, such as type(5) ?function_name or function_name? : For quick help, such as str? or str.upper? or ?str or ?str.upper If you are using an IDE (like Spyder or others), hover tips and auto-complete are your friends, just like Excel&apos;s tooltips. Apart from these quick and easy options, you can always refer docs.python.org , which is an official Python documentation to understand anything about Python in depth, straight from the source. Another great assets are Stack Overflow, and other Stack Exchange communities where you can ask or search for answers related to Python, Excel, or anything related to programming, data analysis, and automation. Chances are, someone has already faced a similar problem and found a solution. Conclusion In this chapter, we created a solid foundation for your Python journey. Starting from the very basic datatypes and variables, we have covered all key topics like control flow, functions, and exception handling. If you are a complete beginner, we recommend you spend enough time on these fundamentals and solve practice problems before moving to the next topic. The time invested here is like sharpening your tool, it makes everything that follows easier and more effective. In the next chapter, we will show you how to use external packages in your script for more advanced work you might want to do. Also, we will discuss ways to work with date and time. A dedicated chapter on date and time will help you understand the operations that you might need frequently. Try it yourself To deepen your understanding of the concepts presented in this chapter, it is recommended that you undertake the following exercises: For a sample email-id such as &quot;user@example.com&quot;, extract the domain name. Repeat the exercise for each email in the given list [&quot;alice@example.com&quot;, &quot;bob@company.org&quot;, &quot;eve@test.net&quot;]. Do this using different approaches, such as with a loop, list comprehension or lambda function with list-map. To rename a file name &quot;file 1.txt&quot; to &quot;file_1.txt&quot;, use the string method. Now, use this method and iterate over a list of such names by using a loop and by using list comprehension too. For security reasons, you want to mask your passwords. Write code to do the same for password &quot;SuperSecret123&quot; by hiding all characters except the last 2. The output should be ************23 You run a shop and want to give a 10% discount on all prices given in the following price list. Compute discounted prices for each product. You can simply print those or save them in a list. prices&#160;=&#160;[100,&#160;250,&#160;450,&#160;89,&#160;120] From the following dictionary, extract only those products that are priced above ₹ 100: product_prices&#160;=&#160;{&apos;Apple&apos;:&#160;120,&#160;&apos;Banana&apos;:&#160;60,&#160;&apos;Mango&apos;:&#160;280} Write a function that accepts any number of inputs and returns their average. You have a list of file names: files &#160;=&#160;[ &quot;report.csv&quot; ,&#160;&quot;data.xlsx&quot;,&#160;&quot;summary.docx&quot;,&#160;&quot;sales.csv&quot;] Collect the names of only the CSV files in a new list. Such practice will help you with file management tasks that you will explore later in this book. You have imported a list of file names from an Excel sheet. Each name has the prefix Report_. Prepare the file names for display by removing the prefix and showing only the unique part. files &#160;=&#160;[ &quot;Report_Sales.xlsx&quot;,&#160;&quot;Report_Inventory.xlsx&quot;,&#160;&quot;Report_Expenses.xlsx&quot;] Your output should be: Sales.xlsx Inventory.xlsx Expenses.xlsx You are cleaning a customer database and want to flag entries where a certain field (like phone number, customer ID or password) is filled with repeated characters (all characters same such as 000000, aaaaaa, or 11111). This may indicate bad data. Write a code to detect such cases. You have a list of LinkedIn profile URLs as given here: urls&#160;=&#160;[ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;https://www.linkedin.com/in/nishaarora/&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;https://www.linkedin.com/in/johndoe/&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;https://www.linkedin.com/in/pragyasharma/&apos; ] You need to: Extract the profile name from each URL. Ensure no extra / at the end. Return a clean list of profile names. Your output should be [&apos;nishaarora&apos;,&#160;&apos;johndoe&apos;,&#160;&apos;pragyasharma&apos;] Customer Purchase Summary Report: For a raw list of transactions. transactions &#160;=&#160;[ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;{ &quot;customer&quot;:&#160;&quot;Alice&quot;,&#160;&quot;product&quot;:&#160;&quot;Laptop&quot;,&#160;&quot;amount&quot;:&#160;1200}, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;{ &quot;customer&quot;:&#160;&quot;Bob&quot;,&#160;&quot;product&quot;:&#160; &quot;Mouse&quot;,&#160;&quot;amount&quot;:&#160;25}, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;{ &quot;customer&quot;:&#160;&quot;Alice&quot;,&#160;&quot;product&quot;:&#160;&quot;Headphones&quot;,&#160;&quot;amount&quot;:&#160;150}, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;{ &quot;customer&quot;:&#160;&quot;Bob&quot;,&#160;&quot;product&quot;:&#160;&quot;Keyboard&quot;,&#160;&quot;amount&quot;:&#160;75}, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;{ &quot;customer&quot;:&#160;&quot;Charlie&quot;,&#160;&quot;product&quot;:&#160;&quot;Monitor&quot; ,&#160;&quot;amount&quot;:&#160;300}, ] Calculate the total amount spent by each customer. Display the result in a clean format. Stock Portfolio Tracker: For your current stock holdings, given as follows: portfolio&#160;=&#160;{ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;AAPL&quot;:&#160;{&quot;shares&quot;:&#160;10,&#160;&quot;price&quot;:&#160;165.3}, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;GOOGL&quot;:&#160;{&quot;shares&quot; :&#160;5,&#160;&quot;price&quot;:&#160;2800.5}, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;TSLA&quot;:&#160;{&quot;shares&quot;:&#160;8,&#160;&quot;price&quot;:&#160;190.1} } Calculate the current portfolio value using these steps: Multiply shares × price for each stock. Calculate the total value of the portfolio. Optionally, print the individual stock values too. Concept check The following thought-based exercises are designed to help you reflect on common Excel-based tasks and explore how similar challenges can be addressed using Python: Explain how Python strings are a game-changer for Excel users. Can you think of some case examples where using Python string methods is smoother than Excel text functions? You are storing monthly sales targets for a team that does not change. Would you use a list or a tuple? Why? You want to extract all even numbers from a list of 100 integers. How would you do that, and what concepts would you need for the task that are discussed in this chapter? Note down your everyday lookup tasks: searching prices, matching IDs, pulling names from lists, etc. Try to reimagine how you would solve them in Python using dictionaries. If it starts to feel limiting or repetitive, that is your cue; you are ready for something more powerful: Python’s pandas. We will cover the same in detail later in the book. Have you ever worked on a task in Excel where you drag a formula across a column and the formula pulls input from different cells, maybe even from other sheets or files? Now think: Have you accidentally changed one of the input cells, and suddenly your whole formula breaks? Did you spend time figuring out what went wrong? Can Python help in such situations? If yes, how do you think it might reduce these errors or make your workflow more reliable? Excel functions are mostly positional, meaning you must enter values in a specific order. Python gives you flexibility with keyword arguments and default values. How could this help in making your code more readable and forgiving? For now, just read the description about read.csv() method from the pandas library mentioned here https://pandas.pydata.org/docs/reference/api/pandas.read_csv.html and figure out which arguments have default values. (It is okay if you do not understand the function yet, as that is to be covered later in the book.) Have you ever seen errors like #DIV/0!, #N/A, or #VALUE! in Excel and had no clue why they appeared? Think of times you wished Excel explained why a cell went wrong. How would catching errors help? 1 For Mac and Linux users, the default working directory may be /Users/YourUsername/ and /home/YourUsername/, respectively. 2 In Colab, the default working directory is /content/, and in Jupyter, the default working directory is the folder where Jupyter was launched.

  - Python-Powered Excel C HAPTER 8 Unlocking Excel with openpyxl Introduction You have gained proficiency with the powerful libraries like pandas and others for exploring, analyzing, and exporting data to various file formats. Now, it is time to learn how to format and style Excel files directly in Python, without requiring Microsoft Excel to be installed. Most businesses expect reports to be delivered by Excel. After analyzing data using Python, you need to export results (summary, pivot tables and charts) exporting results to Excel for business users. In this chapter, we will discuss practical ways to automate, scale, and enhance Excel-heavy processes using openpyxl. This chapter begins by discussing the limitations of pandas for Excel file handling, followed by learning openpyxl library for loading or creating Excel files and sheets, populating data, computing, formatting cells, and writing it back to Excel. Everything can be done programmatically without needing Excel installed on your machine. We will also cover multiple use cases for your automation needs. By the end of this chapter, you will be equipped with skills to automate Excel tasks with confidence and demonstrate efficiency in your work and impress your boss, if any. Structure In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics: •&#9; Moving past pandas for Excel work •&#9; Getting started with openpyxl •&#9; Creating and managing workbooks •&#9; Reading data from Excel •&#9; Writing data to Excel •&#9; Advanced sheet operations • &#9; Data validation and conditional formatting •&#9; Creating charts and exporting images Working with tables and dynamic named ranges Multi-workbook automation use cases Excel templates Best practices and limitations Moving past pandas for Excel work pandas and other libraries work as powerful tools for data analysis workflows to read, write, manipulate, summarize, and plot data with ease. You might not have noticed that while performing Excel I/O operations, pandas relies on backend engines like openpyxl , xlsxwriter , or xlrd. These are separate reader and writer packages in Python. Python’s openpyxl library provides fine-grained control over Excel files, allowing us to read, write, format cells, apply styles, manage charts, define formulas, named ranges, and automate workbook-level operations. It is particularly well-suited for generating professional reports, automating repetitive tasks, and customizing workbooks in ways that pandas alone cannot. While pandas is excellent for data processing and quick exports, openpyxl complements it by enabling rich formatting, interactivity, and report generation features essential for Excel professionals. Used together, pandas and openpyxl offer a powerful combination for end-to-end Excel-based automation to cover both analytical and presentation needs effectively. Getting started with openpyxl openpyxl &#160;was initially based on&#160;and inspired by PHPExcel. It is a powerful Python library to read and write Excel files of various formats, such as xlsx, xlsm, xltx, and xltm files. Actually, it does much more than that. It enables you to create and format sheets, modify values, formulae, styles, insert charts and images, apply custom formatting, and much more, even if you do not have Excel installed on your machine. Such programmatic control allows users to boost productivity by automating repetitive tasks such as generating reports, auditing spreadsheets, building Excel templates, and version control, etc. Even if you are familiar with Visual Basic for Applications ( VBA ), Power Query, or Power Pivot, openpyxl offers a smarter choice for automation because it provides greater flexibility, seamless integration with Python’s vast ecosystem, and cross-platform support, all while allowing you to handle Excel files without being tied to Excel itself. It is an open-source solution that can streamline complex workflows and provide more powerful customization than Excel&apos;s built-in tools. Before diving into openpyxl , it is essential to understand its key terms. These correspond to familiar Excel concepts but are handled programmatically in Python. As an Excel user, you are familiar with the hierarchy of workbooks, worksheets, and individual cells, and openpyxl uses the same names. It refers to the currently selected worksheet in a workbook as the active sheet and a group of cells (e.g., A1:D10) that can be manipulated as a single unit as the range. Rows and columns become iterable Python sequences. Creating and managing workbooks Let us create an Excel workbook, add some data to it, and save it programmatically: from&#160;openpyxl&#160;import &#160; Workbook wb &#160; = &#160;Workbook() # Creating a new Excel Workbook ws&#160;=&#160;wb.active # Accessing active sheet (first sheet here*) ws.title&#160;=&#160;&apos;products&apos; # Giving this sheet a title ws[&apos;A1&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;product_id&apos; # Assigning some values to cells ws[ &apos;B1&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;product_name&apos; ws[&apos;C1&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;product_category&apos; ws[&apos;D1&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;product_price&apos; wb.save(&apos;business_data.xlsx&apos;) # saving as xlsx file This simple code allowed us to create a new Excel file, shown in Figure 8.1 . Select the active sheet, which means the first sheet here, name it as ‘products’ , and write some data to cells A1 up to D1 without even opening the Excel application. Figure 8.1 : Excel workbook created by openpyxl To access a cell in a worksheet, you can use either A1-style notation, like ws[&apos;A1&apos;] , or the index-based approach with the .cell() method, for example, ws.cell(row=1, column=1) retrieves cell A1 . Both methods let you read from or write to specific cells, depending on your needs. Note: When you work with Excel files using openpyxl , all changes happen in your computer’s memory. To see those changes in Excel, you must save the file using wb.save() first. Only then are the updates written to the actual file. This lets you make lots of edits before saving the final version. Let us make more edits to this file. When we modify a file and save it using the same name and location, it replaces or overwrites the existing version, permanently updating its contents. After reasonable edits, save the file, open it from the location and check for the updates. wb.create_sheet(title=&quot;customers&quot; )&#160; #&#160;Create&#160;a&#160;second&#160;sheet&#160;by&#160;title ws2&#160;=&#160;wb[&apos;customers&apos;]&#160; #&#160;Accessing&#160;the&#160;sheet&#160;by&#160;it&apos;s&#160;name ws2[&apos;A1&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;customer_id&apos; &#160; #&#160;Adding&#160;data ws2[&apos;B1&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;region&apos; &#160; wb.create_sheet(&apos;delivery&apos; ) #&#160;Creating&#160;next&#160;sheet&#160; ws3&#160;=&#160;wb.worksheets[2]&#160; #&#160;Accessing&#160;3rd&#160;sheet&#160;by&#160;it&apos;s&#160;index&#160;(index&#160;starts&#160;from&#160;0) ws3.cell(1,&#160;1,&#160;&apos;order_id&apos;)&#160; #&#160;Value&#160;assignment&#160;to&#160;cells&#160;at&#160;location&#160;(row,&#160;col) ws3.cell(1,&#160;2 ,&#160;&apos;order_date&apos;)&#160; ws3.cell(1,&#160;3,&#160;&apos;proposed_delivery_date&apos;) ws3.cell(1,&#160;4,&#160;&apos;actual_delivery_date&apos;) ws4&#160;=&#160;wb.create_sheet(&apos;Analytics_Report&apos;)&#160; #&#160;Creating&#160;another &#160;sheet&#160; ws4.sheet_properties.tabColor&#160;=&#160;&apos;1072BA&apos; &#160; #&#160;setting the tab color of this sheet #&#160;Again,&#160;trying&#160;to&#160;create&#160;sheet&#160;with&#160;the&#160;same&#160;name,&#160;the&#160;name&#160;will&#160;get&#160;a&#160;suffix&#160;1 ws5&#160;=&#160;wb.create_sheet(&apos;Analytics_Report&apos;) #&#160;Deleting&#160;sheet&#160;named&#160;&apos;Analytics_Report1&apos; wb.remove(wb[&apos;Analytics_Report1&apos;]) wb.worksheets&#160; #&#160;list&#160;of&#160;all&#160;sheet’s&#160;name s #&#160;Insert&#160;a&#160;sheet&#160;named&#160;&apos;marketing&apos;&#160;at&#160;index&#160;3,&#160;i.e.,&#160;4th&#160;sheet wb.create_sheet(&apos;marketing&apos;,&#160;3) #&#160;Move&#160;the&#160;sheet&#160;&apos;products&apos;&#160;to&#160;the&#160;last&#160;position wb.move_sheet(wb[&apos;products&apos;],&#160;offset=len(wb.worksheets)&#160;-&#160;1) wb.save(&apos;business_data.xlsx&apos;) The output Excel file is shown in Figure 8.2 . As you can see, you can create or insert sheets using wb.create_sheet() , remove any sheet using wb.remove() , move or copy a sheet using wb.move_worksheet() or wb.copy_worksheet() . Similarly, ws.insert_rows() inserts rows at a given position, and ws.insert_cols() does the same for a column. Figure 8.2 : Excel workbook with multiple sheets created by openpyxl Reading data from Excel Let us import mydata.xlsx and print the names of all the sheets: from&#160;openpyxl&#160;import&#160;load_workbook file&#160;=&#160;&quot;mydata.xlsx&quot; wb&#160;=&#160;load_workbook(file) wb.sheetnames&#160; #&#160;All&#160;sheets&#160;names If you need to read or extract data from massive Excel files without delays, to run fast and lightweight data analysis or reporting scripts without worrying about formatting, charts, and formulas, you can load the workbook in read-only mode: wb&#160;=&#160;load_workbook(file, read_only=True) # Do some stuff wb.close() File opened in read_only mode must be explicitly closed with the close() method. If your file contains Excel formulas, using data_only=False shows the actual formula, if any, for example, =SUM(B2:B10) as a string, while using data_only=True gets us the calculated values. Note: When you create a new workbook with openpyxl , it automatically includes one sheet by default, called the active sheet. However, when loading an existing file with load_workbook(), active sheet refers to the sheet that was last selected in Excel, not necessarily the first one. You can fetch active sheet by running wb.active However, to ensure code stability and avoid potential errors, it is recommended to access worksheets explicitly by name or index such as wb[&apos;SheetName&apos;] or wb.worksheets[index] rather than relying on active sheet. Also, be aware that sheets are &#160;zero-indexed , for example, the first sheet is at index&#160;0. Accessing cell ranges As mentioned earlier, you can fetch the first sheet by running wb.worksheets[0] and access a specific cell using standard Excel letter and number notation (e.g., ws[&apos;A1&apos;]) or using .cell() method with row and column indices (e.g., ws.cell(1, 1) for cell A1 ). To access a range of cells, you can use slicing, such as ws[&apos;A1&apos;:&apos;E1&apos;] . Rows or columns can be obtained similarly: ws = wb.worksheets[0] colC = ws[&apos;C&apos;] # Columns are indexed by letters col_range &#160;=&#160;ws[ &apos;B:D&apos;] # All the rows for columns B up till D row5 &#160;=&#160;ws[ 5] # Rows are indexed by numbers row_range &#160;=&#160;ws[ 5:10] # All the columns for rows 5 to 10 To get row or column boundaries, ws.max_row and ws.max_column can be used. Warning about indexing nuance in openpyxl : In openpyxl , indexing behaves differently depending on what you are accessing: Rows are indexed by numbers, columns by letters. Hence, ws[5] returns a row, not a column, and ws[‘C’] returns column C. To fetch a column by its header, you need a workaround. Worksheet cells use Excel-style 1-based indexing, such as ws[1] returns row 1. Workbook sheets follow Python-style 0-based indexing because wb.worksheets is a (Python’s) list of all worksheets in your workbook. So, wb.worksheets[0] returns the first sheet, and wb.worksheets[1] returns the second. Iterators like iter_rows() and iter_cols() return generators that yield tuples of cells. You can access individual cells within these tuples using 0-based indexing. Reading structured data To read the content of a cell .value() can be applied on a cell, and to get a generator that gives all sheet values row by row, ws.values() can be used. Let us see this in action. ws[&apos;A1&apos;].value&#160; #&#160;Accessing&#160;Data&#160;from&#160;a&#160;single&#160;cell ws_data&#160;=&#160;ws.values # generator&#160;object&#160;containing&#160;worksheet&#160;values #&#160;Preview&#160;data&#160;by&#160;printing&#160;each&#160;row&#160; for &#160;row&#160; in &#160;ws_data: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(row)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Each&#160;row&#160;is&#160;a&#160;tuple of cell values, 1st row has column names You can also use next() to extract subsequent values from a generator object, each call resumes the generator&apos;s execution and retrieves the next yielded value. If the generator finishes its sequence and is exhausted,&#160; next() &#160; raises a&#160; StopIteration &#160;exception. ws_data&#160;=&#160;ws.values #&#160;Reset&#160;the&#160;generator next(ws_data)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Extract&#160;one&#160;row&#160;at a time If needed, you can save this data in a pandas DataFrame (do not forget to reset the generator): data&#160;=&#160;pd.DataFrame(ws_data,&#160;columns&#160;=&#160;next(ws_data)) If you want to read values only from a single column or row, you can iterate through that data as fetching a single row or column results in a tuple: for&#160;cell&#160;in&#160;ws[&apos;C&apos;]:&#9;# print coordinates and values from Column C &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print (cell.coordinate,&#160;cell.value) for cell in ws[5]:&#9;# print coordinates and values from Row 5 print(cell.coordinate, cell.value) # Alternately using list comprehension [(cell.coordinate,&#160;cell.value)&#160;for &#160; cell &#160; in &#160;ws[ 5]]&#160;&#160; #&#160;Row&#160;5 However, while reading data from a range such as ‘A1:E1’ , you need to index into the returned tuple correctly, since slices such as ws[&apos;A1&apos;:&apos;E1&apos;] return a 2D tuple even for a single row: for &#160;cell&#160; in &#160;ws[ &apos;A1&apos;:&apos;E1&apos;][0]: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(cell.value) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; for &#160;row&#160; in &#160;ws[ &apos;B2:D4&apos;]:&#160;&#160; #&#160;2D&#160;tuple&#160;of&#160;cells &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; for &#160;cell&#160; in &#160;row: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(cell.coordinate,&#160;&quot;=&quot;,&#160;cell.value) It is better to use ws.iter_rows() or ws.iter_cols() to avoid the extra indexing required with slicing syntax. Additionally, reading data from dynamic ranges becomes convenient with these functions. Fetching data of all rows except headers from columns ‘B:D’ : for &#160; row &#160; in &#160;ws.iter_rows(min_row =2,&#160;max_row=max_row,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;min_col =2,&#160;max_col=4,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;values_only =True): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;print( row ) Here, values_only=True returns the cell’s raw values, while values_only=False (default), the method yields Cell objects. To print the same values column-wise: for &#160;col&#160; in &#160;ws.iter_cols(min_row= 2,&#160;max_row= max_row,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;min_col= 2,&#160;max_col=4,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;values_only= True): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(col) Writing data to Excel We will discuss smart ways to add data to this sheet instead of manually writing a value in each cell. Basically, we can use data structures like lists, dictionaries, and even Pandas DataFrames to efficiently populate data to an Excel sheet via openpyxl . Earlier, we had created an Excel file with a sheet named ‘products’ that has column headers assigned to cells in the first row. Let us do the same by writing these headers from a Python list and save this file as products_data.xlsx : from &#160;openpyxl&#160; import &#160;Workbook wb&#160;=&#160;Workbook() ws&#160;=&#160;wb.active ws.title&#160;=&#160;&apos;products&apos; headers&#160;=&#160;[&apos;product_id&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_name&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_category&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_price&apos;] # Header list ws.append(headers) wb.save(&apos;products_data.xlsx&apos;) Alternatively, we can iterate through the header list and assign those to the cells dynamically: #&#160;Populate&#160;headers&#160;dynamically for &#160;col_num,&#160; header&#160;in&#160;enumerate (headers,&#160;start=1): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;cell&#160;=&#160;ws.cell(row= 1,&#160;column=col_num) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;cell. value &#160;=&#160;header This approach gives us more flexibility to apply formatting styles (that we will discuss in the next section, Advanced sheet operations, Formatting and Styles ), and it also works well when we want to put headers in specific cells, not necessarily the first row. Writing data from a list of lists Now that headers are in place, we can add more data from a nested list using ws.append() with minimal code: data &#160;=&#160;[ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 101 ,&#160;&apos;Wireless&#160;Mouse&apos;,&#160;&apos;Electronics&apos;,&#160;25.99], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 102,&#160;&apos;Yoga&#160;Mat&apos;,&#160;&apos;Fitness&apos;,&#160;15.50], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 103,&#160;&apos;Notebook&apos; ,&#160;&apos;Stationery&apos;,&#160;3.99] ] for &#160;row&#160; in &#160; data: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;ws.append(row) Append method always appends at the end of the current data (the next empty row) starting from column A onward. Either we can save the file again and launch it to verify the changes, or we can write a function that prints all of the spreadsheet values by just calling it: def&#160;print_rows(): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;for&#160;row&#160;in&#160;ws. iter_rows (values_only=True): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(row) Calling this function print_rows() shows us the data created so far: (&apos;product_id&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_name&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_category&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_price&apos;) (101,&#160;&apos;Wireless&#160;Mouse&apos;,&#160;&apos;Electronics&apos;,&#160;25.99) (102,&#160; &apos;Yoga&#160;Mat&apos;,&#160;&apos;Fitness&apos;,&#160;15.5) (103,&#160;&apos;Notebook&apos;,&#160;&apos;Stationery&apos;,&#160;3.99) If you need more control and flexibility, you can access cells and then write the value as we have done in the following code. This is useful especially when you need to customize formatting, styling, data validation, or track exact cell positions. Let us add more rows by accessing the cells: data&#160;=&#160;[ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 104,&#160;&apos;Pen&apos;,&#160;&apos;Stationery&apos;,&#160;10], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 105,&#160;&apos;USB&#160;Cable&apos;,&#160;&apos;Electronics&apos;,&#160;150] ] for &#160;r_idx,&#160; row &#160; in &#160;enumerate(data,&#160; start= ws.max_row + 1 ): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; for &#160;c_idx,&#160;val&#160; in &#160;enumerate( row,&#160;start=1): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;ws.cell( row=r_idx,&#160;column=c_idx,&#160;value=val) Running print_rows() verifies that the data is added: (&apos;product_id&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_name&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_category&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_price&apos;) (101,&#160;&apos;Wireless&#160;Mouse&apos;,&#160;&apos;Electronics&apos;,&#160;25.99) (102,&#160;&apos;Yoga&#160;Mat&apos;,&#160;&apos;Fitness&apos; ,&#160;15.5) (103,&#160;&apos;Notebook&apos;,&#160;&apos;Stationery&apos;,&#160;3.99) (104,&#160;&apos;Pen&apos;,&#160;&apos;Stationery&apos;,&#160;10) (105,&#160;&apos;USB&#160;Cable&apos;,&#160;&apos;Electronics&apos;,&#160;150) You can also write data using iter_rows() or iter_cols() by leveraging it with zip() . Writing data from a list of dictionaries Sometimes, you get data from JSON or API responses in the form of a dictionary. You can append the same by iterating through dictionary items as done in the following code: products&#160;=&#160;[ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;{ &apos;id&apos;:&#160;106,&#160;&apos;name&apos;:&#160;&apos;Chair&apos;,&#160;&apos;category&apos;:&#160; &apos;Furniture&apos;,&#160;&apos;price&apos;:&#160;45.50}, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;{ &apos;id&apos;:&#160;107,&#160;&apos;name&apos;:&#160;&apos;Water&#160;Bottle&apos;,&#160;&apos;category&apos;:&#160;&apos;Fitness&apos;,&#160;&apos;price&apos; :&#160;10.00} ] for &#160;product&#160;in&#160;products: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;ws.append([ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;product[ &apos;id&apos;], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;product[ &apos;name&apos;], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;product[ &apos;category&apos;], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;product[ &apos;price&apos;] &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;]) You can run print_rows() again to read the rows from the data. Writing data to a column You can even write data in a column by looping over a list: ws[‘E1’]&#160;=&#160;‘Units&#160;Sold’ quantity&#160;=&#160;[5,&#160;8,&#160;15,&#160;30, 6, 10, 20] for &#160;i,&#160; status &#160; in &#160;enumerate(quantity&#160;,&#160;start= 2): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;ws[f &apos;E{i}&apos;]&#160;=&#160; status So far, we have added enough data to work with Excel formulas. Now, let us see the Excel file generated after saving it in openpyxl : Figure 8.3 : Excel file with data populated from Python You can also create a column that is derived from existing columns. For example, to compute revenue ( price * units_sold ) using given columns, you can compute the values in Python and write the result to an Excel cell, shown as follows: ws[&apos;F1&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;Revenue&apos; for &#160;i&#160; in &#160; range(2,&#160;ws.max_row&#160;+&#160;1): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;price&#160;=&#160;ws[ f&apos;D{i}&apos;].value &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;units_sold&#160;=&#160;ws[ f&apos;E{i}&apos;].value &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;ws[ f&apos;F{i} &apos;]&#160;=&#160;price*units_sold Here, we have used f-strings to insert variable values so they can change dynamically in a loop. This will create a column named revenue that stores values (not formulas) calculated from the price and units_sold columns, as shown in Figure 8.4 . However, as an Excel user, you would prefer to write Excel formulas from openpyxl , so that the result auto-updates if there is any change in the data. We are discussing this in the next section.
