Figure 8.4 : Revenue as static calculated values To write a huge amount of data efficiently, you can use write-only mode which optimizes memory usage by not keeping everything in memory. It can export unlimited amount of data (even more than Excel can handle actually), while keeping memory usage under 10Mb . A write-only workbook can only be saved once, you write data row-by-row sequentially, and it gets flushed to disk as you go. Advanced sheet operations Apart from creation and insertion of data, openpyxl also offers advanced features such as adding Excel formulas, data validation, conditional formatting, adding charts, images, and styling the sheet, to name a few. Let us explore some of these capabilities. Excel formulas and calculations You can write an Excel formula using openpyxl , and Excel itself will evaluate the formula and show the correct result in that column (refer to Figure 8.5 ). Creating Excel formula to compute revenue ( price * units_sold ) in column F can be done conveniently as follows: for &#160;i&#160; in &#160; range(2,&#160;ws.max_row&#160;+&#160;1): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;price&#160;=&#160; f&apos;D{i}&apos; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;units_sold&#160;=&#160; f&apos;E{i} &apos; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;ws[ f&apos;F{i}&apos;]&#160;=&#160;f&apos;={price}*{units_sold}&apos; wb.save(&apos;writing_excel_formula.xlsx&apos;) You need to start a formula with = and write it the same way you do in Excel: Figure 8.5 : Revenue using Excel formula syntax Note that openpyxl does not evaluate the formula, so printing rows will not show you the resultant values, but Excel will evaluate and display the correct value, as shown in the following code. If you want to compute total revenue, you can use Excel’s SUM() function : ws[&apos;H1&apos;]&#160;= &#160; &apos;Total&#160;Revenue&apos; ws[&apos;H2&apos;]&#160;= &#160; &apos;=SUM(F2:F7)&apos; This will show total revenue (computed in Excel) in the H2 cell. However, we prefer not to hardcode a data range. This can be done by assigning ws.max_row to a new variable and using the same in the code: last_row&#160;= &#160;ws.max_row ws[f&apos;E{last_row + 1}&apos;] = &apos;Total Revenue&apos; ws[f&apos;F{last_row + 1}&apos;] = f&apos;=SUM(F2:F{last_row})&apos; sum_formula = f&apos;=SUM(F2:F{last_row})&apos; Similarly, we have also computed the mean revenue by using Excel’s AVERAGE() formula. The resultant Excel file is shown as follows: Figure 8.6 : More Excel formulas written from openpyxl To check available Excel formulas supported by openpyxl , you can run: from &#160;openpyxl. utils &#160; import &#160; FORMULAE FORMULAE If openpyxl does not recognize a formula (like XLOOKUP, UNIQUE, SEQUENCE, TEXTJOIN , etc.), it might show #NAME error in Excel. You can check if a formula is supported or not by using name in FORMULAE . For example, when you run &apos;TEXTJOIN&apos;&#160;in&#160;FORMULAE , it outputs False , indicating that it is not supported by openpyxl : ws[&apos;I1&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;TEXTJOIN()&#160;Function&apos; ws[&apos;I2&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;=TEXTJOIN(&quot;:&#160;&quot;,&#160;TRUE,&#160;B2:C2)&apos; &#160;&#160; #&#160;Shows&#160;#NAME&#160;error&#160;in&#160;Excel Trying to use such functions does not throw any error in Python, but Excel fails to evaluate those. We can simply prefix 1 such functions with _xlfn and they will work (refer to Figure 8.7 ): ws[&apos;J1&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;using&#160;_xlfn&#160;prefix&apos; ws[&apos;J2&apos;]&#160;=&#160;&apos;=_xlfn.TEXTJOIN(&quot;:&#160;&quot;,&#160;TRUE,&#160;B2:C2)&apos; &#160;&#160; #&#160;Now&#160;works In Figure 8.7 , to show you the output effectively, we have accessed column_dimensions attribute of the worksheet and set the hidden property to True for hiding some columns: Figure 8.7 : Using _xlfn prefix for unsupported Excel functions openpyxl also supports setting array formulas with openpyxl.formula utility . Let us use one such formula in our sheet: from &#160;openpyxl. worksheet.formula &#160; import &#160; ArrayFormula ws[‘H4’] = “Transposed Data” ws[‘H5’].value &#160;=&#160; ArrayFormula(‘H5:I7’,&#160;‘=TRANSPOSE(H1:J2)’) You can see in the following figure (output Excel file), the data from range H1:J2 is transformed: Figure 8.8 : Writing an array formula using openpyxl Formatting and styles While working with data in Excel, you use many options to make it convenient for you, such as freeze pan, merge cells, wrap text, filter, change row or column dimensions, etc. These can be done smoothly in openpyxl as follows: ws.freeze_panes&#160;=&#160;&apos;B2&apos; # Freeze top row and first column ws.freeze_panes = None # Remove freeze panes ws.auto_filter.ref&#160;=&#160;ws.dimensions&#9;# Applying filter ws.auto_filter.ref&#160;=&#160;None # Removing the filter ws.insert_rows(1, 2) # Inserting 2 blank rows on the top ws[&apos;A1&apos;] = &apos;Sample Data&apos; ws.row_dimensions[1].height = 20 # increasing row height #&#160;Merge cells ws.merge_cells( &apos;A1:F1&apos;) # Merging cells # Cell Alignment and Wrap-Text from &#160;openpyxl.styles&#160; import &#160;Alignment ws[&apos;A1&apos;].alignment&#160;=&#160;Alignment(horizontal=&apos;center&apos;,&#160;vertical=&apos;center&apos;) ws[&apos;H3&apos;]&#160;=&#160; &apos;OpenPyXl&#160;is&#160;an&#160;awesome&#160;library&#160;that&#160;helps&#160;me&#160;automate&#160;Excel&#160;workflow.&apos; ws[&apos;H3&apos;].alignment&#160;=&#160;Alignment(wrap_text=True) # Enabling text wrapping ws.column_dimensions[&apos;H&apos;].width&#160;=&#160;15 # Setting the width of column wb.save(“data_formatting.xlsx”) You can also style your data by setting font, font size, font color, fill color etc. as you do in Excel: #&#160;Styling individual cells from &#160; openpyxl .styles&#160;import &#160;Font,&#160;PatternFill,&#160;Border,&#160;Side ws[&apos;A1&apos;].font&#160;=&#160;Font(size=16,&#160;bold=True,&#160;color=&apos;FF1F497D&apos;) ws[&apos;H3&apos;].font&#160;=&#160;Font(italic=True,&#160;name&#160;=&#160; &apos;Agency&#160;FB&apos;) wb.save(“data_formatting.xlsx”) As expected, the output file shown in the following figure shows font (font type, size, color, bold, or italic) and the effect of alignments (wrap text, merge and center): Figure 8.9 : Cell dimensions, alignment, and font settings in openpyxl You can style data at the cell-level or by iterating through a row or a column. However, it helps to use a custom-named style for applying consistent, reusable formatting across multiple cells with a single reference. It is like styling with a shortcut that reflects the visual identity of your brand: #&#160;Using&#160;a&#160;named&#160;style to apply anywhere from &#160;openpyxl.styles&#160; import &#160;NamedStyle , PatternFill, Border, Side custom_style&#160;=&#160;NamedStyle(name=&quot;custom_style&quot; ) custom_style.font&#160;=&#160;Font(size=12,&#160;bold=True,&#160;color=&quot;FF0000&quot;) custom_style.fill&#160;=&#160;PatternFill(start_color=&quot;ADD8E6&quot;,&#160;fill_type=&quot;solid&quot;) custom_style.border&#160;=&#160;Border( &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;left=Side(style= &apos;medium&apos;), &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;right=Side(style= &apos;medium&apos;), &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;top=Side(style= &apos;medium&apos;), &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;bottom=Side(style= &apos;medium&apos;) ) #&#160;Register&#160;style&#160;to&#160;workbook wb.add_named_style(custom_style) #&#160;Apply&#160;style&#160;to&#160;another&#160;cell ws[&apos;B9&apos;].style&#160;=&#160;&quot;custom_style&quot; #&#160;Save&#160;again (We are saving in a different file) wb.save(&quot;styled_named.xlsx&quot;) The resultant Excel file is as follows: Figure 8.10 : Excel sheet formatted via openpyxl As openpyxl works by reading and writing the underlying Office Open XML ( OOXML ), invalid or unsupported style assignments may not show, or cause Excel to repair the file. Once you understand a few key rules, you can style cells reliably. If you are facing any errors, we would advise you to apply one style at a time (e.g., just a fill, just a font) to isolate the issue. A checklist to avoid style errors is given as follows: Always use cell attributes such as .fill , .font , .alignment , etc. directly on a cell, never set them to .value . For example, use ws[&apos;A1&apos;].font = Font(...) and avoid ws[&apos;A1&apos;] = Font(...) as this wrongly sets the cell&apos;s value, not its formatting. Use a valid aRGB color using the hex code. For merged cells, only apply styles to the top-left cell. Avoid using too many unique styles . Refer to the official document at https://openpyxl.readthedocs.io/en/3.1/styles.html for any more details. You can also apply text and number formatting, such as currency, percentage, date formats, and more, after importing numbers from openpyxl.styles . Apart from this cell, sheet, or workbook protection, etc., can be done. Data validation and conditional formatting In advanced Excel workflows, data validation and conditional formatting play pivotal roles in maintaining data integrity and enhancing interpretability. Using openpyxl library, we can automate these safeguards. Validation rules In the products_data.xlsx file, we may want to prevent users from entering negative prices or highlight unusually high sales figures. Let us set a validation rule to prevent users from entering negative or zero values for product_price , i.e., column D: from &#160;openpyxl.worksheet.datavalidation&#160; import &#160;DataValidation #&#160;Create&#160;validation&#160;rule:&#160;Only&#160;allow&#160;decimal&#160;values&#160;&gt;&#160;0 dv_price&#160;=&#160;DataValidation(type=&quot;decimal&quot;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;operator= &quot;greaterThan&quot;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;formula1= &quot;0&quot;,&#160;showErrorMessage=True) dv_price.error&#160;=&#160;&quot;Price&#160;must&#160;be&#160;a&#160;positive&#160;number.&quot; dv_price.errorTitle&#160;=&#160;&quot;Invalid&#160;Price&quot; #&#160;Add&#160;the&#160;validation&#160;to&#160;the&#160;column&#160;dynamically max_excel_rows&#160;=&#160;1048576 &#160;&#160; #&#160;Excel&apos;s&#160;row&#160;limit dv_price.add(f&quot;D2:D{max_excel_rows}&quot;) ws.add_data_validation(dv_price) wb.save( &apos;products_data_validated.xlsx&apos;) This will prevent a user from entering invalid values in column D in Excel. Note about openpyxl data validation nuance: Openpyxl&apos;s role (manipulation): openpyxl&apos;s job is to manipulate the structure of the XLSX file. When you use the DataValidation object and methods, the library simply writes the XML code for the validation rule into the file structure. It has no mechanism to check the values being written by your Python code against that rule. Therefore, you can easily use openpyxl to write an invalid value (e.g., -5 into a cell validated for positive numbers) right next to the rule itself. Excel&apos;s role (enforcement): The validation rules are only activated and enforced when the file is opened by the Excel application (or another compatible spreadsheet software). Excel is the program that interprets that validation XML, prevents the user from manually typing invalid data, and visually flags existing invalid data if the feature (like the &quot;Circle Invalid Data&quot; option) is enabled. In short, openpyxl is a file editor, and Excel is the rule enforcer. Similarly, for text columns such as product_name , we can restrict text length by setting a suitable rule and apply to relevant column: #&#160;Create&#160;text&#160;length&#160;validation&#160;rule dv_text&#160;=&#160;DataValidation( &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; type=&quot;textLength&quot;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;operator= &quot;lessThan&quot;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;formula1= &quot;10&quot;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;showErrorMessage= True ) dv_text.error&#160;=&#160;&quot;Too&#160;long!&#160;Text&#160;must&#160;be&#160;under&#160;10&#160;characters.&quot; dv_text.errorTitle&#160;=&#160;&quot;Text&#160;Length&#160;Error&quot; #&#160;Apply&#160;validation&#160;to&#160;column B dv_text.add(f&quot;B2:B{max_excel_rows}&quot;) ws.add_data_validation(dv_text) If you are to choose a dropdown list for product_category , you can simply use type=“list” argument with other related arguments: #&#160;Create&#160;data&#160;validation&#160;for&#160;a&#160;dropdown&#160;list dv_cat&#160;=&#160;DataValidation( type=&quot;list&quot;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;formula1= &apos;&quot;Electronics,Fitness,Stationery&quot;&apos;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;allow_blank= False) dv_cat.prompt&#160;=&#160;&quot;Choose&#160;a&#160;value&#160;from&#160;the&#160;list&quot; dv_cat.error&#160;=&#160;&quot;Invalid&#160;entry.&#160;Please&#160;choose&#160;from&#160;the&#160;list.&quot; #&#160;Apply&#160;the&#160;data&#160;validation&#160;to&#160;column C ws.add_data_validation(dv_cat) dv_cat.add(f&quot;C2:C{max_excel_rows}&quot;) This safeguards data integrity by restricting input to predefined options. This is especially useful when handling categories like status fields, department names, or standard responses in forms, anything where variation could cause confusion or reporting issues. Conditional formatting For applying conditional formatting, CellIsRule class can be used from openpyxl.formatting.rule along with style formatting: from &#160;openpyxl.formatting.rule&#160; import &#160;CellIsRule from &#160;openpyxl.styles&#160; import &#160;PatternFill #&#160;Define&#160;formatting&#160;rule:&#160;highlight&#160;with&#160;green&#160;fill green_fill&#160;=&#160;PatternFill(start_color=&apos;C6EFCE&apos;,&#160;end_color= &apos;C6EFCE&apos;,&#160; &#9;&#9;&#9; fill_type=&apos;solid&apos;) rule&#160;=&#160;CellIsRule(operator=&apos;greaterThan&apos;,&#160;formula=[&apos;10&apos;],&#160; &#9;&#9; fill=green_fill) #&#160;Apply&#160;to&#160;&quot;Units&#160;Sold&quot;&#160;column&#160;(column E) last_row&#160;=&#160;ws.max_row E_range_str&#160;=&#160;f&quot;E2:E{last_row}&quot; ws.conditional_formatting.add(E_range_str,&#160;rule) This rule highlights all cells in column E (Units Sold) with a green fill if their value is greater than 10, helping visually track high-performing entries. Refer to the following figure: Figure 8.11 : Conditional formatting applied to Units Sold For conditional formatting, some advanced visual styles are not supported by openpyxl , but simple rules based on cell values work reliably. Also, the rules only apply to the range you specify, and they do not auto-extend as new rows are added, so for dynamic data, always apply validation to a generously sized range (like D2:D500). Creating charts and exporting images Openpyxl supports many of Excel’s core chart types, such as bar chart, line chart, pie chart, area chart, scatterplot, etc. Refer to the official document for more details at https://openpyxl.pages.heptapod.net/openpyxl/charts/introduction.html . To create a bar chart, we need to use the class BarChart() and add relevant values and categories (created by the Reference() class) to a chart object. Then we can add the chart to any sheet location. We can also set title labels, style, colors, legends, etc., as follows: from &#160;openpyxl.chart&#160;import&#160;BarChart,&#160;Reference chart&#160;=&#160;BarChart() chart.title&#160;=&#160;&quot;Units&#160;Sold&#160;by&#160;Product&quot; chart.x_axis.title&#160;=&#160;&quot;Product&quot; chart.y_axis.title&#160;=&#160;&quot;Units&#160;Sold&quot; categories&#160;=&#160;Reference(ws,&#160;min_col=2,&#160;max_col= 2,&#160; &#9;&#9;&#9;&#9;min_row=1,&#160;max_row=ws.max_row) values &#160;=&#160;Reference(ws,&#160;min_col= 5,&#160;max_col=5,&#160;min_row=1,&#160; &#9;&#9;&#9;&#9;max_row=ws.max_row) chart.add_data(values,&#160;titles_from_data=True) chart.set_categories(categories) ws.add_chart(chart,&#160;&quot;A10&quot;) wb.save(&quot;charts.xlsx&quot;) This exports bar chart is at A10. Similarly, to create a grouped bar chart, we can add data of two numerical variables, such as price and units_sold , for the same category reference: chart2&#160;=&#160;BarChart() chart2.title&#160;=&#160; &quot;Price&#160;vs&#160;Units&#160;Sold&quot; chart2.x_axis.title&#160;=&#160;&quot;Product&quot; chart2.y_axis.title&#160;=&#160;&quot;Value&quot; price_data&#160;=&#160;Reference(ws,&#160;min_col=4,&#160;min_row=1,&#160;max_row=ws.max_row) units_data&#160;=&#160;Reference(ws,&#160;min_col=5,&#160;min_row=1,&#160;max_row=ws.max_row) categories&#160;=&#160;Reference(ws,&#160;min_col=2,&#160;min_row=1,&#160;max_row=ws.max_row) chart2.add_data(price_data,&#160;titles_from_data=True) chart2.add_data(units_data,&#160;titles_from_data=True) chart2.set_categories(categories) ws.add_chart(chart2,&#160;&quot;J1&quot;) wb.save(&quot;charts.xlsx&quot;) The resultant charts are shown in the following figure: Figure 8.12 : Creating Excel charts from openpyxl You can also create a new sheet and export charts there. If you are willing to use pandas ( matplotlib ) or seaborn chart, you can export it as an image. Hence, it will not update with the change in data: import &#160;pandas&#160; as &#160;pd import &#160;matplotlib.pyplot&#160; as &#160;plt from &#160;openpyxl.drawing.image&#160; import &#160;Image&#160; as &#160;XLImage df&#160;=&#160;pd.read_excel(&apos;products_data.xlsx&apos;) df[[&apos;product_price&apos; ,&#160;&apos;Units&#160;Sold&apos;]].plot(kind=&apos;bar&apos;,&#160;figsize=(8,&#160;5)) plt.title(&quot;Price&#160;and&#160;Units&#160;Sold&#160;per&#160;Product&quot;) plt.ylabel(&quot;Value&quot;) plt.xticks(rotation=15) plt.tight_layout() plt.savefig(&quot;chart.png&quot;,&#160;bbox_inches=&quot;tight&quot;) ws2&#160;=&#160;wb.create_sheet(title=&quot;pandas&#160;chart&quot;) img&#160;=&#160;XLImage(&quot;chart.png&quot;) ws2.add_image(img,&#160;&quot;B2&quot;) wb.save(&quot;charts.xlsx&quot;) This code generates a chart using pandas , exports it as an image (see Figure 8.13 ), and inserts it into a new worksheet in the same Excel workbook. You can use XLImage() from openpyxl to export any image to Excel. Figure 8.13 : pandas/matplotlib charts are exported as a static image Working with tables and dynamic named ranges You can create Excel tables and defined named ranges using openpyxl . Creating and styling Excel tables You can create and style an Excel table by using Table() or TableStyleInfo() class from openpyxl.worksheet.table : from &#160;openpyxl&#160;import&#160;load_workbook from openpyxl.utils import get_column_letter from &#160;openpyxl.worksheet.table&#160;import&#160; Table ,&#160;TableStyleInfo wb&#160;= &#160;load_workbook( &apos;products_data.xlsx&apos;) ws&#160;= &#160;wb.active #&#160;Define &#160;the&#160; table &#160; range last_row = ws.max_row last_col_letter = get_column_letter(ws.max_column) &#160; table_ref&#160;= &#160; f&quot;A1:{last_col_letter}{last_row}&quot; table &#160; = &#160; Table(displayName=&quot;ProductsTable&quot;,&#160;ref=table_ref) # Optional: add style to table style = TableStyleInfo(name=&quot;TableStyleMedium9&quot;, showRowStripes=True) table.tableStyleInfo = style #&#160;Add &#160; table &#160; to &#160;worksheet ws.add_table(table) wb.save(&apos;products_with_table.xlsx&apos;) This creates an Excel table named ProductsTable and adds style to the same. Figure 8.14 : Excel table creation Dynamic named ranges To define a named range for product prices (D2:D7): from &#160;openpyxl.workbook.defined_name&#160; import &#160;DefinedName price_range&#160;=&#160;DefinedName( &apos;ProductPrices&apos;,&#160;attr_text=&apos;Sheet1!$D$2:$D$7&apos;) wb.defined_names.add(price_range) You can verify this in Excel through Formula Tab | Name Manager , shown as follows: Figure 8.15 : Named manager shows the named range and the Excel table Multi-workbook automation use-cases Excel power users might need a workflow to navigate multiple Excel files, read and compare data across workbooks, update or write to another workbook, merge or consolidate Excel files, clean up data from different sources, copy sheets between files, or even batch-process a folder of Excel files. Let us explore how to achieve such automation using openpyxl . Load and process multiple Excel workbooks: You can simply iterate through a collection of file names and read the desired file: from &#160;openpyxl&#160; import &#160;load_workbook files&#160;=&#160;[&apos;data1.xlsx&apos;,&#160;&apos;data2.xlsx&apos;] books&#160;=&#160;[load_workbook(f)&#160;for &#160;f&#160; in &#160;files] books[0].active&#160; #&#160;active&#160;sheet&#160;of&#160;first&#160;workbook books[1 ].sheetnames&#160; #&#160;sheet&#160;names&#160;of&#160;second&#160;workbook You can use your learnings from Chapter 5 , Automating File Management with Python , to batch process the files. Using file automation, combining with datetime or re modules to process files by week or month, backing up originals by renaming before saving, using NamedStyle to maintain formatting across files, pave the way for powerful automation. Some use cases are discussed in the following sub-sections. Automated integration of distributed product logs Imagine a mid-sized retail company where each department, e.g., Electronics, Fitness, Furniture, etc., maintains its own Excel workbook, say, electronics.xlsx, fitness.xlsx, stationery.xlsx, etc. These files log product sales weekly using a consistent structure. As an analyst, your job includes manually opening each file, copying and pasting data into a master sheet, and watching for duplicates or formatting errors. Traditionally, you use Excel to do this every week. Combining your learnings from Chapter 5 , Automating File Management with Python , with insights from the current chapter, to build a script given as follows: Loads all departmental files ( .xlsx files) from a folder Extracts and appends their content into a master workbook, e.g., consolidated_log.xlsx You can simply iterate through each row in the individual sheets and append the rows to a master sheet for consolidated analysis: import &#160;os from openpyxl import load_workbook, Workbook folder = &quot;department_logs&quot; master_wb = Workbook() master_ws = master_wb.active master_ws.title = &quot;All Departments&quot; master_ws.append([&apos;product_id&apos;, &apos;product_name&apos;, &apos;product_category&apos;, &apos;product_price&apos;, &apos;Units Sold&apos;]) for file in os.listdir(folder): if file.endswith(&apos;.xlsx&apos;): wb = load_workbook(os.path.join(folder, file)) ws = wb.active for row in ws.iter_rows(min_row=2, values_only=True): master_ws.append(row) master_wb.save(&quot;consolidated_log.xlsx&quot;) This code can be made efficient by using list comprehension instead of loop. You can also add data validation, conditional formatting, and an automated summary sheet in the workflow. Other similar use-cases are to aggregate product sales from multiple cities or regions, or revenue data from each week or month, etc. Inventory reconciliation Sometimes, we need to compare values across sheets or workbooks. For example, ERP_Inventory.xlsx has System-recorded stock levels , and Warehouse_Count.xlsx contains physically counted stock at the warehouse. We need to i dentify mismatches in item counts. It will help us detect shrinkage, prevent stockouts, overstocking, and update discrepancies or potential fraud. A simple Python script can achieve this: from openpyxl import load_workbook wb = load_workbook(&quot;Inventory_Comparison.xlsx&quot;) ws1 = wb[&apos;ERP_Inventory&apos;] ws2 = wb[&apos;Warehouse_Count&apos;] for row in range(2, ws1.max_row + 1): item_id = ws1[f&quot;A{row}&quot;].value erp = ws1[f&quot;D{row}&quot;].value warehouse = ws2[f&quot;D{row}&quot;].value if erp != warehouse: print(f&quot;Item ID {item_id}:\n System-recorded stock levels={erp}, Physically counted stock at warehouse={warehouse}\n*****&quot;) Other such use-cases are to compare two versions of products_data.xlsx for discrepancies in units sold, verifying data integrity between master and regional sales files, confirming that all records (IDs, prices, dates) transferred correctly after migrating from a legacy system to the new database, validating vendor billings, quality audits, financial reconciliation, or detecting price discrepancies, etc. Such a quick comparison helps correct accidental overwrites and validate revisions. Automated deficit detection in department budgets If you are a financial analyst, accountant, or auditor who is willing to automate financial data, checks across departmental budget spreadsheets. You can write a short script to scan through multiple Excel files in a folder, and identify if any department has overspent (i.e., negative values in budget summaries): import &#160;os from &#160;openpyxl&#160; import &#160;load_workbook #&#160;Folder&#160;containing&#160;all&#160;budget&#160;spreadsheets folder_path&#160;=&#160;&apos;department_budgets&apos; #&#160;Column&#160;and&#160;sheet&#160;to&#160;scan target_column&#160;=&#160;&apos;C&apos; &#160;&#160; #&#160;Adjust&#160;based&#160;on&#160;your&#160;layout sheet_name&#160;=&#160;&apos;Summary&apos; &#160;&#160; #&#160;Or&#160;use&#160;wb.active&#160;if&#160;sheet&#160;names&#160;vary #&#160;Scan&#160;all&#160;files for &#160;filename&#160; in &#160;os.listdir(folder_path): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;filename.endswith( &apos;.xlsx&apos;): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;file_path&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(folder_path,&#160;filename) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;wb&#160;=&#160;load_workbook(file_path,&#160;data_only= True) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;ws&#160;=&#160;wb[sheet_name]&#160; if &#160;sheet_name&#160; in &#160;wb.sheetnames&#160; else &#160;wb.active &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; for &#160;row&#160; in &#160; range(2 ,&#160;ws.max_row&#160;+&#160;1):&#160;&#160; #&#160;Skip&#160;header &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;cell&#160;=&#160;ws[ f&apos;{target_column}{row}&apos;] &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160; isinstance(cell.value,&#160;(int,&#160;float))&#160;and &#160;cell.value&#160;&lt;&#160; 0: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;Deficit&#160;in&#160;{filename} &#160;at&#160; {cell.coordinate}:&#160;{cell.value}&quot;) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; break &#160;&#160; #&#160;Stop&#160;after&#160;first&#160;deficit&#160;found&#160;in&#160;this&#160;file Such automation helps finance teams with budget validation across departments or spot-checking audit anomalies without opening each file manually. Cleaning and normalizing external files Incoming workbooks from different sources often have inconsistent formatting. As an Excel professional, you might be manually preparing files for reporting or uploading to ERP systems. For example, to normalize column widths and alignment effortlessly for creating client-facing versions or backups with a consistent appearance: for &#160;col&#160; in &#160;[ &apos;A&apos;,&#160;&apos;B&apos;,&#160;&apos;C&apos;,&#160;&apos;D&apos;,&#160;&apos;E&apos;]: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;src_ws.column_dimensions[col].width&#160;=&#160; 20 for &#160;row&#160; in &#160;src_ws.iter_rows(min_row= 2): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; for &#160;cell&#160; in &#160;row: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;cell.alignment&#160;=&#160;Alignment(horizontal= &quot;center&quot;) If you need to standardize inconsistent transaction data by cleaning up messy values like extra spaces, inconsistent casing, improperly formatted dates, and non-numeric prices, etc., to make the data ERP-ready, you can write a short script for the same. For example, you can create your own version of clean_text(), clean_price() and clean_date() functions and use those in your script: #&#160;Clean&#160;each&#160;row for &#160;row&#160; in &#160;ws.iter_rows(min_row= 2 ,&#160;values_only=True): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;product_id&#160;=&#160;row[ 0]&#160;if &#160;row[ 0]&#160;else &#160; &quot;UNKNOWN&quot; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;product_name&#160;=&#160;clean_text(row[ 1]) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;category&#160;=&#160;clean_text(row[ 2]) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;price&#160;=&#160;clean_price(row[ 3]) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;date_added&#160;=&#160;clean_date(row[ 4]) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;cleaned_ws.append([product_id,&#160;product_name,&#160;category,&#160;price,&#160;date_added]) #&#160;Save&#160;cleaned&#160;file cleaned_wb.save(&quot;Cleaned_Data.xlsx&quot;) A sample script is attached in the companion repo for you to modify and use as per your needs. This script will help you write a cleaned version of messy data to a new Excel file. You have witnessed how managing multiple Excel files programmatically accelerates turnaround time, reduces errors, and liberates analysts from manual repetition. Openpyxl allows power users to evolve from passive file consumers to active automation architects. Excel templates Excel template files ( .xltx ) are useful for creating reusable, styled workbooks that include predefined layouts, formulas, charts, and formatting. You can also create Excel templates using openpyxl by setting: wb.template&#160;=&#160;True wb.save( &quot;template_file.xltx&quot;) This marks the workbook as a template and saves it with the .xltx extension. You can also load a template file and save it as a regular .xlsx file by setting: wb.template&#160;=&#160;False wb.save(&quot;new_report.xlsx&quot;) Best practices and limitations Learning openpyxl equips you with a deeper control over Excel file structure than what pandas alone can offer. Here are some key takeaways from the chapter: Excel installation not required: You do not need Microsoft Excel installed; openpyxl works independently to read, write, and modify Excel files using pure Python. It means you can create Excel deliverables on any machine without having Excel installation. Workbook control: openpyxl allows granular access to Excel components, including sheets, cells, styles, merged regions, and formulas. Programmatic reporting: openpyxl can dynamically generate structured Excel reports, automate sheet population, and modify workbook elements, paving the way for scalable reporting workflows. File support: It supports loading and saving xlsx , xlsm , xltx , xltm files. Integration with pandas and other libraries: While openpyxl can be used alongside pandas (via engine =&apos; openpyxl &apos; ), it remains essential when structured formatting or Excel-native behavior is required. You can combine openpyxl with other libraries such as pandas , xlsxwriter , and more to expand functionality. Let us list down some best practices while working with openpyxl : It is recommended to always validate workbook structure when loading or writing user-generated files to prevent unexpected exceptions. You need to run workbook.save(&apos;filename.xlsx&apos;) to preserve any changes made in openpyxl , otherwise, the edits will not show up in the file. Changes like Excel formulas, data validation, and conditional formatting only become visible when the file is opened in Excel. You should verify these changes in Excel. Use read-only and write_only mode for large data files. It is also important to understand the limitations of openpyxl and proposed solutions as mentioned in the following list: File support : It does not support legacy .xls and binary .xlsb file formats. We will discuss other libraries for such files in Chapter 9 , Core Excel Read-Write Tools in Python . Limited charting and formatting : It does not offer support to export matplotlib and seaborn charts natively as Excel charts. You can create and export chart using openpyxl . Advanced styles and themes are not supported by openpyxl , for that you can refer xlsxwriter (to be covered in Chapter 9 , Core Excel Read-Write Tools in Python). Cannot execute Excel macros : It can read .xlsm files, but it cannot run, trigger, create, or embed VBA code. We will discuss working with VBA code in upcoming chapters. Formula limitations : Supports writing formulas, e.g., =SUM(A1:A10) , but it does not evaluate them. You need Excel to open the file to see the calculated results. For live Excel interactions, we will discuss xlwings in upcoming chapters. No live Excel interaction : All edits are file-based. Results are visible only when reopened in Excel. As discussed, for live Excel interaction, xlwings library can be used, which we have discussed in upcoming chapters. No native PDF export : It can create or manipulate Excel files but cannot render or export them as PDF directly, xlwings support this. Performance bottlenecks : For large Excel files, openpyxl can be memory-intensive compared to binary readers like xlrd or pyxlsb. When working with large Excel files in openpyxl , you should use read-only mode ( read_only=True ) to load massive files efficiently without consuming excessive memory through lazy loading. For generating large files, use write-only mode ( write_only=True ) to write data sequentially and flush to disk progressively. Avoid normal mode for huge datasets as it loads the entire workbook into memory, which can cause crashes. Conclusion In this chapter, we have explored the Python library openpyxl in depth as a powerful interface for reading, writing, and modifying Excel files programmatically. While many of you are already proficient with pandas for tabular data processing, openpyxl offers unique advantages when working directly with Excel workbooks, especially those containing rich formatting, multiple sheets, charts, formulas, and layout-sensitive content. In the next chapter, you will learn to use pandas’ ExcelWriter class with openpyxl engine to combine pandas’ tabular convenience with openpyxl’s formatting so you can update DataFrames and retain workbook styles and charts. Also, we will discuss other reader and writer packages that offer alternatives to openpyxl for speed, legacy support, and fine-grained formatting.
