  - Python-Powered Excel C HAPTER 7 Smart Tools for Faster Analysis Introduction Once your data is clean, structured, and ready for analysis, the next step is to quickly uncover patterns, summaries, and relationships. This process is known as Exploratory Data Analysis ( EDA ). In this chapter, you will learn how to accelerate these familiar tasks using powerful Python tools that extend your pandas skills. You will discover how numpy offers faster calculations, how Python libraries allow you to visualize the data, how automated profiling libraries generate instant summary reports, and how interactive interfaces simplify data exploration. These tools help you answer business questions faster and enable deeper insights with less effort. We will start with creating static charts using pandas and interactive ones using plotly. We will also introduce numpy which is building block for many scientific and numerical python libraries followed by numpy-financial for common finance functions. We will then introduce many free tools for automated profiling of data and interactive GUI based analysis before discussing few key points to consider while working with large data files. By the end of this chapter, you will have a set of efficient techniques to quickly deliver actionable insights and validate your data-driven decisions. Structure In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics: Data visualization NumPy essentials Computational finance with numpy-financial Automated profiling of datasets Interactive analysis with GUI tools Working with large data files Data visualization As an Excel user, you might have used charts to visualize your data. Python offers robust data visualization capabilities through libraries like matplotlib , seaborn , plotly , bokeh , etc. Additionally, pandas integrates well with these tools, allowing users to generate plots directly from data frames. Quick pandas plotting As you have worked with the pandas library, you can start using it to visualize your data. By default, it uses matplotlib to generate a wide range of charts. For producing Kernel Density Estimate ( KDE ) plots, pandas relies on the scipy library to perform the underlying statistical computations. If scipy is not installed, this specific plot would not work. Let us import a sample for generating basic charts using pandas: df &#160;=&#160;pd.read_csv( &apos;plotting_data.csv&apos;) For simple charts such as a pie chart, bar or column chart, histogram, line chart, or scatterplot, etc. df.plot() can be used by specifying an appropriate value for the kind argument. For example, to visualize the sales distribution, let us create a histogram (Refer to Figure 7.1 ): df[&apos;Sales&apos;].plot(kind=&apos;hist&apos;,&#160;bins=30,&#160;title=&apos;Sales &#160;Distribution&apos; ) Depending upon your IDE settings, plot can show up directly inside the console, in a separate plot window, or sometimes not at all unless you explicitly call plt.show() . In spyder, you can set Tools | Preferences | IPython Console | Graphics | Backend and set it to Inline . That way, plots will appear right inside the console. Alternatively, you can add these lines in your code: import &#160;matplotlib.pyplot&#160; as &#160;plt #&#160;Code&#160;for&#160;your&#160;plot&#160;here plt.show() Here, plt.show() is used to display all currently active figure objects. Depending on the environment you are working in, it can be skipped. For example, Colab and Jupyter notebooks usually automatically show all figures created in a code cell. Figure 7.1 : Histogram generated by pandas (matplotlib backend) For creating a scatterplot (shown in Figure 7.2 ), you need to supply two numeric variables and set the argument kind = ‘scatter’ as follows: df.plot(kind = &apos;scatter&apos;,&#160;x = &apos;Sales&apos;,&#160;y = &apos;Profit&apos;,&#160; alpha = 0.5,&#160;title = &apos;Sales&#160;vs&#160;Profit&apos;,&#160;figsize = (7, 5)) Here, the argument alpha introduced transparency, and figsize sets the figure size (width, height) in inches. You can further customize a chart by tweaking a few parameters to tailor the output more precisely to your data and preferences. Figure 7.2 : Scatterplot generated by pandas The code for some common charts is provided in the accompanying code file, with illustrative sample charts in the following figure: Figure 7.3 : Sample charts created in pandas Library pandas makes plotting easy by offering built-in functions where you can also customize things like color, layers, labels, grid, subplots, etc. Running pd.options.plotting.backend shows the backend used for plotting, which is by default matplotlib . Additionally, you can produce interactive plots directly from a data frame in pandas by using the plotly backend, without even needing to import plotly explicitly. It enables you to zoom in, select or deselect any category, and view detailed information by pointing to specific data. To activate this backend, you will need to have plotly installed, and then just need to set pd.options.plotting.backend to &apos;plotly&apos; and call .plot() to add interactivity to the pandas plot. In Jupyter and Colab, these charts will display as usual (in notebook), while in Spyder or other IDEs, you might need to export them into an HTML file using fig.write_html(&quot;myplot.html&quot;) . You can open the same by double-clicking the file and it will display in your browser. plotly also introduced changes in how figures are rendered, setting 1 renderers to browser will directly open interactive chart in browser. import&#160;plotly.io&#160;as &#160;pio pio.renderers.default &#160;=&#160; &apos;browser&apos; Using plotly explicitly If you have created a chart with plotly backend, you must have realized that, unlike static plots from Excel or matplotlib , plotly charts are interactive ones. If you are interested in creating impactful charts with animation, faceting, fine-tuned styling, customization, or choosing advanced charts, you can learn about creating charts using plotly library explicitly. It is a JavaScript-based library that provides a high-level API plotly.express for beginners to quickly create impressive visuals. Let us recreate the same scatterplot, which we had created with pandas , this time with plotly.express : import &#160;plotly. express &#160; as &#160;px px.scatter(df,&#160;x=&apos;Sales&apos;,&#160;y=&apos;Profit&apos;, title=&apos;Sales&#160;vs&#160;Profit&apos;, opacity&#160;=&#160;0.5) As discussed earlier, Colab or Jupyter shows this chart in output cell without explicitly setting pio.renderers.default . In Spyder, as plotly charts do not automatically show up, either we can set the renderer to browser or we can save the figure as an HTML file named scatterPlot.html . This file can be opened manually from the file system or programmatically by running this code: import &#160;webbrowser webbrowser.open(&quot;scatterPlot.html&quot;) Refer to the following figure to see the Sales vs. Profit distribution visualized by plotly: Figure 7.4 : Interactive scatterplot generated by plotly Creating a violin chart, that shows Profit across Region c ombined with box=True and points=&apos;outliers&apos; : fig&#160;=&#160;px.violin(df,&#160;x=&apos;Region&apos;,&#160;y=&apos;Profit&apos;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;box=True,&#160;points= &apos;outliers&apos;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;title= &apos;Profit&#160;Distribution&#160;by&#160;Region&apos;) fig.write_html(&quot;Violinplot.html&quot; ) In the resultant figure, shown as follows, you get a power trio of quartile information, density and outlier points for spotting trends: Figure 7.5 : Violin chart along with box plot and outliers generated by plotly To create an animated bar chart, we can use animation_frame argument in px.bar() after preparing data to summarize monthly sales by region: Figure 7.6 : Animated barchart generated by plotly This chart can be played or paused when viewed interactively. Other advanced charts supported by plotly.express are area charts, sunburst and treemap diagrams, funnel and funnel area charts, density heatmaps and contour plots, empirical cumulative distribution functions, strip plots, scatterplot matrices, parallel coordinates and categories, as well as icicle charts. NumPy essentials Having covered data visualization techniques using pandas and plotly , we now shift focus to numpy , which is the foundation of many libraries, including pandas , matplotlib , Excel automation tools like openpyxl and xlwings , etc. We have also used the numpy library to create sample datasets throughout this book. While pandas has many built-in operations, numpy offers faster, more flexible functions when working with arrays of numbers. If you have worked with MATLAB, you will find numpy similar to it for numerical computing and matrix operations. MATLAB is a standalone tool for scientific and engineering workflows, while numpy is a Python library that serves as the building block for many advanced Python libraries. A clear understanding of numpy strengthens your ability to manipulate data efficiently, both in standalone analyses and when automating Excel tasks. Once you are comfortable working with pandas DataFrames, it helps to understand the core building block underneath them, which is numpy array, also known as ndarray . Python’s built-in data structures, list, tuple, set, and dictionary, are all designed for flexibility so they can store data of different types. In contrast, numpy arrays are homogeneous data structures, which means all elements of the array must be of the same datatype. Also, numpy array stores data in contiguous blocks of memory, which makes it memory efficient. Another reason numpy operations are faster is that they are implemented in optimized, compiled C code. As an Excel user willing to learn Python, you can learn only the basics for now and dive deep into the library later, if needed. NumPy arrays NumPy arrays can be created by using Python sequences or using specific numpy functions. We will start by creating some simple arrays. One-dimensional or two-dimensional arrays can be created from a simple list or a nested list, as done here: sales &#160; = &#160; np.array([1500, &#160; 2200, &#160; 1850, &#160; 2400.]) sales_qtr &#160; = &#160; np.array([[1500, &#160; 1700, &#160; 1600], &#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 2200,&#160;2100,&#160;2300]]) sales.dtype &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;dtype(&apos;float64&apos;) sales_qtr.dtype &#160; #&#160;dtype(&apos;int32&apos;) Here, sales is a one-dimensional array that can be expressed as a row or a column of data, while sales_qtr is a two-dimensional array. Another point to note is that due to one floating-point data in the end, sales becomes float64 2 array while sales_qrt is an int32 datatype because numpy arrays are homogeneous. Unlike Python’s built-in int and float types, numpy provides more specific datatypes, such as int32 , int64 , float32 , and float64 , allowing for precise control over memory usage and computational performance. To create an array of ones or zeroes, you can use np.ones or np.zeros . For the creation of an identity matrix, np.eye is a helpful constructor. Many other useful constructors are there for generating pseudorandom numbers, a range of numbers, evenly spaced numbers, or normally distributed numbers in the form of a numpy array. Refer to the following: np.zeros(5)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;an array of 5&#160;zeros&#160; np.ones(3)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;an array of 3&#160;ones np.eye(5)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;5×5&#160;identity&#160;matrix&#160; np.arange(5)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;an array of numbers 0&#160;to&#160;4&#160;(5&#160;values like range(5)) np.arange(1,&#160;6)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;1&#160;to&#160;5&#160;(inclusive&#160;start,&#160;exclusive&#160;end) np.linspace( 0,&#160;1,&#160;5)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;5&#160;evenly&#160;spaced&#160;values&#160;from&#160;0&#160;to&#160;1 np.random.randn(1000)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;1000&#160;values&#160;from&#160;a&#160;std&#160;normal dist&#160; np.random.normal(loc=70,&#160;scale=10,&#160;size=1000) #&#160;normal&#160;dist_mean=70,&#160;std=10 np.random.rand(5)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;5&#160;random&#160;floats&#160;in&#160;[0,&#160;1) np.random.randint(5)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;One&#160;random&#160;integer&#160;from&#160;0&#160;to&#160;4 ids = [‘a01’, ‘a02’, ‘a03’, ‘a04’, ‘a05’] # a list np.random.choice(ids, size=3) #&#160;Randomly&#160;select&#160;3&#160;values&#160;from&#160;the&#160;list We can use such functions for creating synthetic data, statistical modeling, and initializing parameters for machine learning models or optimization routines. Vectorization You have seen pandas vectorized operations for faster column calculations. It is largely because it builds on top of numpy , and internally, many pandas operations rely on numpy arrays. Let us revisit a simple operation on a pandas series: s&#160;=&#160;pd.Series([1,&#160;2,&#160;3,&#160;4]) s&#160;+&#160;10&#160; #&#160;Vectorized&#160;addition&#160;in&#160;pandas Here, s is wrapping a numpy array under the hood ( s.values is a numpy array). When you add 10 to s , pandas calls the corresponding numpy operation behind the scenes: type(s.values)&#160;&#160; #&#160;&lt;class&#160;&apos;numpy.ndarray&apos;&gt; You can practice such operations with numpy arrays you have created: price &#160; = &#160; np.array([5, &#160; 7.5, &#160; 7, &#160; 8]) revenue = sales*price new_sales &#160; = &#160; np.array([1000, &#160; 2000, &#160; 3000, &#160; 4000]) total_sales = sales &#160; + &#160; new_sales &#160; If you perform arithmetic operations on arrays of different shapes, numpy (whenever possible) broadcasts or extends the smaller array over the larger array so that they have compatible shapes: unit_price &#160;=&#160; 5 &#160; #&#160;Zero&#160;dimensional &#160;array&#160;or&#160;scalar revenue &#160;=&#160;sales&#160;*&#160;unit_price revenue_qtr &#160;=&#160;sales_qtr&#160;*&#160;unit_price You can also apply statistical or mathematical functions 3 as you do on pandas series or DataFrame: sales_qtr.sum()&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Sum&#160;of&#160;all&#160;values&#160; sales_qtr.sum(axis=1)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Sum&#160;of&#160;each&#160;row sales_qtr.mean()&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Mean&#160;of&#160;all&#160;values&#160; sales_qtr.mean(axis=1)&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Mean&#160;of&#160;each&#160;row sales_qtr.std()&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Standard&#160;deviation&#160;of&#160;all&#160;values&#160; np.argmin(sales_qtr)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Index&#160;of&#160;minimum&#160;value&#160;in&#160;array np.argmax(sales_qtr)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Index&#160;of&#160;maximum&#160;value&#160;in&#160;array np.percentile(sales_qtr,&#160;10)&#160;&#160; #&#160;10th&#160;percentile&#160;of&#160;values np.cumsum(sales_qtr)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Cumulative&#160;sum&#160;of&#160;values You can also explore functions to reshape, flatten, or stack numpy arrays. Refer to the code file. Computational finance with numpy-financial Prior to numpy 1.20 (reference: https://numpy.org/neps/nep-0032-remove-financial-functions.html) , it used to offer useful financial functions such as pmt() , npv() , and fv() , etc. in numpy . These functions are now available separately as a numpy-financial package. It is a lightweight package that is safe to use even in constrained environments. These functions are especially useful for analyzing loan payments, cash flow, investment growth, etc. This library provides you with Excel-like functions useful for those who work in the finance sector. Just like Excel, it is recommended to use positive values for inflows, and negative for outflows, and to convert annual interest rates to monthly if payments are monthly: import&#160;numpy_financial&#160;as&#160;npf #&#160;1. Monthly&#160;Loan&#160;Payment&#160;(PMT)&#160;or&#160;Loan&#160;EMI principal&#160;=&#160;500000 monthly_rate&#160;=&#160;0.10&#160;/&#160;12 # annual rate = 10% n_months&#160;=&#160;5&#160;*&#160;12&#9; # 5 Years emi&#160;=&#160;npf.pmt(rate=monthly_rate,&#160;nper=n_months,&#160;pv=-principal) print (f&quot;Monthly&#160;Payment:&#160;₹{emi:,.2f}&quot;) # Monthly&#160;Payment:&#160;₹10,623.52 #&#160;2. Future&#160;Value&#160;of&#160;Recurring&#160;Investment&#160;(FV)&#160; monthly_investment&#160;=&#160;5000 monthly_rate&#160;=&#160;0.08&#160;/&#160;12 n_months&#160;=&#160;10&#160;*&#160;12 future_value&#160;=&#160;npf.fv(rate=monthly_rate,&#160;nper=n_months,&#160; &#9;&#9;&#9;pmt=-monthly_investment,&#160;pv=0) print(f&quot;Future&#160;Value:&#160;₹{future_value:,.2f}&quot; ) # Future&#160;Value:&#160;₹914,730.18 #&#160;Net&#160;Present&#160;Value&#160;(NPV)&#160; &#160; #&#160;Initial&#160;investment&#160;=&#160;₹1,00,000 #&#160;Returns&#160;over&#160;5&#160;years&#160;=&#160;₹20k,&#160;₹30k,&#160;₹40k,&#160;₹25k,&#160;₹15k cashflows&#160;=&#160;[-100000,&#160;20000,&#160;30000,&#160;40000,&#160;25000,&#160;15000] rate&#160;=&#160;0.10 npv&#160;=&#160;npf.npv(rate,&#160;cashflows) print(f&quot;Net&#160;Present&#160;Value&#160;(NPV):&#160;₹{npv:,.2f}&quot;) # Net&#160;Present&#160;Value&#160;(NPV):&#160;₹-583.05 #&#160;Present&#160;Value&#160;(PV)&#160; future_value&#160;=&#160;100000 rate&#160;=&#160;0.08 n_years&#160;=&#160;3 pv&#160;=&#160;npf.pv(rate=rate,&#160;nper=n_years,&#160;pmt=0,&#160;fv=-future_value) print(f&quot;Present&#160;Value:&#160;₹ {pv:,.2f}&quot;) # Present&#160;Value:&#160;₹79,383.22 #&#160;Internal&#160;Rate&#160;of&#160;Return&#160;(IRR)&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; irr&#160;=&#160;npf.irr(cashflows) print(f&quot;Internal&#160;Rate&#160;of&#160;Return&#160;(IRR):&#160;{irr&#160;*&#160;100:.2f}%&quot; ) # Internal&#160;Rate&#160;of&#160;Return&#160;(IRR):&#160;9.77% If you verify the calculations in Excel, you might sometimes notice a discrepancy in the value of IRR. This is because Excel uses a guess-based method and may return #NUM ! error if it fails to converge. While Python’s npf.irr() uses a different root-solving algorithm. It may find a root that differs from Excel’s. You can always cross-validate with NPV plots or alternative tools, or if you want an aligned result, you can use the custom scipy.optimize code example to show IRR more robustly. Automated profiling of datasets As an Excel user, you might be spending considerable time exploring and handling data quality issues such as missing values and their position, duplicates in the data, unique values in some column, presence of too many zeros, interaction or correlation between different columns, etc., before you start getting meaningful insights or catching some unusual pattern. For example, suppose a hospital noticed 20% of cholesterol data was missing. As a beginner analyst, you have done a quick fix by filling the data with the mean. Now, this can be misleading. By filling in missing values with the mean cholesterol level, you might artificially inflate the cholesterol values of healthy patients. This is risky because doctors will now potentially recommend unnecessary treatments or tests to healthy patients, or the classification model, if used, will classify healthy individuals as high-risk patients. Later, you decided to dig deeper into the root cause of the issue, after scanning carefully, you noticed that the real reason for the missing values in cholesterol data was something that you had earlier ignored. Patients who had normal vital signs were not tested for cholesterol during routine checkups. This shows that understanding why data is missing is just as important as the values themselves. Good diagnosis takes patience but prevents bad decisions later. Before working with any dataset, it is essential to explore and diagnose it carefully by using charts, statistical summaries, correlations, or pivot tables to check for missing values, duplicates, outliers, and unusual correlations. Let us consider another example. In a logistics firm, duplicate shipment records led to inflated sales figures, causing overstock and financial loss. Simply cleaning or summarizing data without understanding its structure and data quality issues can lead to serious business mistakes. This is why analysts spend a good amount of time exploring data before arriving at any conclusion. Even for basic reporting, a detailed diagnosis can uncover hidden inconsistencies and anomalies. As datasets grow larger, manually scanning through rows and columns to understand inconsistent types, skewed distributions, patterns, missing values, or anomalies consumes more time and may not be enjoyable. Although pandas offers robust tools for data exploration, you still need to write code for each task. Python offers a convenient solution through automated data profiling tools , which generate quick overviews, summary statistics, and visualizations of your data by running just one or two lines of code. These tools are not a replacement for pandas , but for a preliminary diagnostic, especially on unfamiliar data, they offer a faster, deeper overview than what most Excel workflows can deliver. Let us discuss some of the popular tools. Note: Some tools used in this chapter like dtale evolve very quickly, while some (like sweetviz ) are not actively updated and often require specific versions of dependencies. Installing them inside your main book environment can sometimes cause version conflicts with other libraries. So, for this chapter, it is recommended to create a separate experimental environment to explore these tools, while maintaining a clean and stable environment for everyday analysis and automation tasks. Versions used in this book for this chapter are: Python 3.9 sweetviz 2.3.1 numpy 1.23.5 ydata-profiling 4.16.0 dtale 3.19.1 pandasgui 0.2.15 sweetviz One very simple and lightweight Python library for automatic Exploratory Data Analysis ( EDA ) is sweetviz , which quickly generates beautiful visualizations and outputs an HTML application. This library automatically infers datatypes and visualizes each variable and their interactions. Just with two lines of code, you get datatype, missing values, unique values, duplicate rows, and most frequent values, numerical analysis like sum, min, max, range, quartiles, mean, mode, standard deviation, median absolute deviation, coefficient of variation, kurtosis, and skewness for relevant columns. For example: import &#160;sweetviz&#160; as &#160;sv import &#160;pandas&#160; as &#160;pd df&#160;=&#160;pd.read_csv(&quot;sales_data.csv&quot;) # To generate&#160;the&#160;report report&#160;=&#160;sv.analyze(df)&#160; #&#160;To save and display&#160;the&#160;report report.show_html(&quot;sales_sweetviz_report.html&quot;)&#160; It saves an html file in your local machine and opens the same in browser. In Jupyter Notebook, report.show_notebook() will embed the report inside a Notebook. Here is a glimpse of a report generated for sales_data in Figure 7.7 . You can also customize the layout, width, and height of the embedded view. Figure 7.7 : HTML report generated by sweetviz for sales data You can also compare two data sets by using sv.compare() . This feature can be extremely useful in comparing the dataset before and after cleaning, allowing you to assess how association, distributions, or data quality indicators change. Other practical use-cases include comparing performance across different geographies, such as sales in the east vs. west region, analyzing relative performance of products A and B to spot the differences in business matrices, or evaluating changes over time, such as January in comparison to February data. In data science or machine learning contexts, it can also be used to compare training and validation datasets to ensure they are representative of each other. For demonstration purposes, we created a cleaned version of sales_data by removing all missing values and compared it with the original sales_data : df_cleaned&#160;=&#160;df.dropna() #&#160;Compare&#160;two&#160;data&#160;sets&#160;&amp;&#160;generate&#160;report comp_report&#160;=&#160;sv.compare([df,&#160;&quot;Data&quot; ],&#160;[df_cleaned&#160;,&#160;&quot;cleaned&#160;Data&quot;]) comp_report.show_html(&quot;sales_sweetviz_compare_report.html&quot;)&#160; A glimpse of the comparison report generated can be seen in Figure 7.8 : Figure 7.8 : Comparison report generated by sweetviz Sweetviz is a lightweight and easy-to-use profiling tool, but it is no longer actively maintained. This can lead to compatibility issues with newer versions of NumPy or Python, sometimes requiring version downgrades in isolated environments. Let us look at some actively developed libraries for automated profiling of data. ydata-profiling This tool was formerly known as pandas-profiling . With the expansion of its capabilities to support Spark DataFrames, it was renamed to ydata-profiling to better reflect its broader scope and functionality. It can be used for generating an EDA summary in one line of code that can be exported in different formats such as html and json. In Jupyter or Colab, you can view the report directly within the output cell: from &#160;ydata_profiling&#160; import &#160;ProfileReport profile&#160;=&#160;ProfileReport(df,&#160;title=&quot;Dataset&#160;Profile&quot;) profile.to_file(&quot;report.html&quot;) You can open this html report from working directory. This profiling report is even more comprehensive and statistically rigorous than the one generated by sweetviz . Let us have a glimpse of the report for the same data in Figure 7.9 : Figure 7.9 : Part of ydata_profiling report for sales_data By clicking on the Alerts tab, you can see variables with missing values or high correlation with other variables. On top, you have a tab to browse to different sections of the report, such as interactions, correlations, missing values, or sample data, part of which is shown in Figure 7.10 : Figure 7.10: More of ydata_profiling report for sales_data To generate the report as a json file, you can run profile.to_file(&quot;sales_report.json&quot;) . The package outputs a simple and digested analysis of a dataset, including time-series and text. You can also compare two data sets. For very large datasets, ydata-profiling can be memory-intensive, so you can either use a reduced data set, shown as follows, for exploration or switch to Spark : df_sample&#160;=&#160;df_full.sample(frac=0.1,&#160;random_state=42) ProfileReport(df_sample).to_file(&quot;sample_report.html&quot;) You can also create a minimal report or disable expensive computations. The package is actively evolving, and you can check official documentation for the latest features, usage examples, and updates at https://docs.profiling.ydata.ai/latest/ . Interactive analysis with GUI tools Apart from automated profiling tools discussed so far, Python also provides several GUI-based tools for interactive analysis. These tools enable a user to analyze, visualize, and explore data in depth with just a few clicks without writing any code. Many of these tools also generate equivalent pandas code for the actions performed, offering a seamless transition between visual exploration and reproducible scripting. As an Excel user, you will surely appreciate the intuitive interface and powerful features these tools offer. The following sub-sections highlight some of the most popular options. dtale dtale is an amazing Python library that allows you to play with and explore your data set using an interactive graphical interface. This library supports various file formats, including csv, xls, xlsx, tsv, and PARQUET. This library would allow you to quickly use editing, filters, pivot tables, slicers, charts, with deeper insights, ease of action, and better performance. dtale brings a full-featured interactive UI for your pandas DataFrame, right inside the browser. It feels familiar yet vastly more capable. dtale was inspired by the data exploration capabilities of SAS and translated into Python’s open-source world. Hence, it is especially effective for Excel and SAS users who are accustomed to point-and-click interfaces. Running dtale.show(open_browser=True) in Spyder 4 will launch the GUI and let you choose your data interactively: import &#160;dtale dtale.show(open_browser=True) You can explore loading some sample data files in the interface, as shown in the following figure: Figure 7.11 : Loading sample data files Alternatively, you can pass the data frame to dtale.show() function. It outputs a URL which you can paste in your browser: df&#160;=&#160;pd.read_csv(&apos;sales_data.csv&apos;,&#160;nrows=1000) dtale.show(df) This opens a whole new world of possibilities to explore. Here are some game-changing features you can access in just a few clicks: Filter, sort, and rename instantly : You can click on any particular column and choose to sort, move, duplicate, or rename the column, get a summary, aggregation, format change, and advanced filter options from there. One-click pivot tables : Action tab at the top allows you to show or hide columns, custom filters, merge or stack DataFrames, feature analysis, and summarize data in the form of pivot tables, as shown in the Figure 7.12 : Figure 7.12 : Interface for creating a pivot able You can view the output as a table or as a pandas DataFrame. The table view is shown here: Figure 7.13: Pivot table generated by dtale Visualizations built-in : This tab offers to visualize missing values, analyze correlation, assess predictive power, and generate various types of charts such as line, bar, scatter, pie, histogram, worldcloud, heatmap, 3D scatter, surface, maps, candlestick, treemap, funnel, and pareto chart. The following figure shows a scatterplot between Quantity and Revenue grouped by Region and line fitted by the OLS method (you can change to lowess method): Figure 7.14 : Scatter plot generated by dtale There are many other options that allow you to highlight specific parts of data, edit data, perform string cleaning, handle missing values or duplicates, and even change the look and feel of the interface in the Settings tab with ease, as it requires no coding. Note: dtale handles moderately large datasets well (up to one to two million rows). Beyond that, performance can drop. However, in such cases, you can load less data or consider filtering your data first. You can also export pandas code for the tasks performed in the GUI. If needed, this code can be copied for running in your IDE. Figure 7.15 is a screenshot captured after performing basic operations and generating pandas code for the same using the Code Export option: Figure 7.15 : Python code generated by dtale for various actions performed d-tale , while actively developed, relies on a local web server and browser-based interaction, which limits its usability in Colab’s cloud-hosted environment. It internally uses a suite of powerful Python libraries like dateutil , numpy , pandas , statsmodel , matplotlib , seaborn , plotly , scikit-learn , missingno , dash , flask , and much more. It is like a Swiss Army knife for data exploration, offering an easy-to-use GUI powered by a rich ecosystem of Python libraries. It is not just a viewer, it is a full-fledged data exploration suite. PandasGUI PandasGUI is another tool that presents pandas DataFrames in a standalone, spreadsheet-like window, enabling users to view, filter, edit, and visualize data without writing code. You can launch the GUI by running this code: from &#160;pandasgui&#160;import&#160; show show() It opens a separate window, allowing you to import multiple datasets (if you need) through the import menu in the Edit tab as shown in the following figure: Figure 7.16 : Viewing sales_data.csv from multiple DateFrames that are loaded Alternatively, you can load any pandas DataFrame using show(df) or load one or more sample data set(s) using pandasgui.datasets module: from&#160;pandasgui.datasets &#160;import&#160;pokemon,&#160;titanic show(titanic) show(pokemon,&#160;titanic) If you want to load them all in one go, you can run this code: from&#160;pandasgui.datasets import all_datasets show(**all_datasets) Alternatively, you can import those using the GUI by Debug | Browse Sample Datasets . If the folder appears empty, run from pandasgui.datasets import all_datasets once. It initializes and loads all the datasets into memory. Now, reopen the GUI or the Browse Sample Datasets menu, and the sample datasets should appear. You can explore data editing, statistical summary, visualization, interactive filtering, etc. The following figure shows the violin chart backed by plotly created for a sample data set, named titanic : Figure 7.17 : Plotly backed chart created by PandasGUI Highly interactive desktop tools such as PandasGUI depend on local event loops, and UI frameworks, which are not fully supported in Colab. As a result, these tools are best used in local Python environments rather than browser-hosted notebooks. Unlocking conversational AI with PandasAI PandasAI is a promising open-source tool that brings the power of generative AI into your data analysis workflow with pandas. It allows users to ask questions in plain English and get answers, such as summaries, charts, or insights, directly from a DataFrame, much like querying an intelligent assistant built into your data. You can chat with your data with prompts like “ Which region had the highest total revenue ”, or “ Plot the bar chart for each region, using different colors ” . It supports natural language prompts through integration with large language models ( LLMs ) such as OpenAI, Anthropic Claude, Google Gemini, Hugging Face, and more. For example, to use OpenAI&apos;s GPT, you only need an API key from https://platform.openai.com/account/api-keys . Note: OpenAI’s API usage is paid unless you are on a free trial , and charges depend on the model and usage volume. PandasAI offers two approaches: one through SmartDataframe , which wraps your DataFrame and lets you interact using .chat() for conversational exploration, and another through the PandasAI.run() method, which is more direct and suited for quick, one-off queries. You can also use SmartDatalake &#160;instead of a&#160; SmartDataframe for queries with multiple DataFrames and Agent to keep track of the state of the conversation and multi-turn conversations For users familiar with Excel 365&apos;s Copilot or other AI-assisted tools, PandasAI offers similar capabilities to interact with data using natural language. While the tool is promising and steadily evolving, it is important to be aware of a few practical considerations. Installing PandasAI within a Conda environment may currently lead to version conflicts or interfere with existing packages, primarily due to dependency constraints. This is not uncommon with fast-developing Python packages that integrate with a range of external libraries and APIs. For those interested in experimenting with PandasAI , it is advisable to install it in a clean virtual environment using pip install pandasai . As development continues and adoption grows, improved compatibility and Conda support can be expected. Working with large data files At this point, you have already seen how numpy and pandas offer a major step up from Excel formulas. It is time to talk about how to optimize performance, reduce memory usage, and work comfortably with large datasets. While Excel 365 and Power Query allow handling larger files than before, performance can still suffer as file size grows, especially beyond a few hundred thousand rows. Python, using pandas, offers multiple smart ways to work with large files, discussed in the following sub-sections. Load only required columns and rows If you are not analyzing all columns or rows, you can load only specific columns or only a few rows using usecols or nrows argument of the reader function 5 while loading the data. For benchmarking, try loading the sales_data.csv that has one million rows of sales data that we have created for practice. Then, load the selected columns and fewer rows to compare the time consumed in reading the data and memory usage: #&#160;Loading&#160;full&#160;data&#160; start&#160;=&#160;time.time() df_full&#160;=&#160;pd.read_csv(&quot;sales_data.csv&quot;) end&#160;=&#160;time.time()&#160; print(f&quot; ⏱️ &#160; Time &#160; taken: &#160; {end&#160;-&#160;start:.2f} &#160;sec» )&#160; #&#160; ⏱️ &#160; Time &#160; taken: &#160; 0.72 &#160; sec df_full.info(memory_usage=&quot;deep&quot;)&#160; #&#160;224.6&#160;MB #&#160;Loading&#160;less&#160;data start&#160;=&#160;time.time() df_sel&#160;=&#160;pd.read_csv(&quot;sales_data.csv&quot;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;usecols=[ &quot;Date&quot;,&#160;&quot;Region&quot;,&#160;&quot;Revenue&quot;],&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;nrows= 100_000) end&#160;=&#160;time.time() print(f&quot; ⏱️ &#160; Time &#160; taken: &#160; {end&#160;-&#160;start:.2f} &#160;sec» )&#160; #&#160; ⏱️ &#160; Time &#160; taken: &#160; 0.08 &#160; sec df_sel.info(memory_usage=&quot;deep&quot;)&#160; #&#160;13.9&#160;MB As you can see, the data which we needed was loaded 90% faster and memory usages dropped by almost 95%. For larger datasets, loading the part of data that is needed can drastically reduce memory footprints. Use efficient datatypes As discussed in Chapter 6 , Data Manipulation with Pandas , you can convert a column with just a few unique values to a category column. Similarly, you can downcast numeric columns to their smallest suitable datatype, as pandas offers more fine-grained datatypes than base Python. For example, instead of int64 by default, int32 or int16 can be used as space-efficient integers based on their range. This is especially powerful when your data does not need large value ranges. You can downcast columns using pd.to_numeric() or astype() . While astype() requires you to manually pick the datatype, pd.to_numeric() intelligently picks the smallest integer or float type that safely fits your data, making it a smarter option for memory savings. By using more efficient datatypes, you can store larger datasets in memory as shown: df_full.memory_usage(deep=True).sum()&#160; #&#160;224.6&#160;MB df_downcast&#160;=&#160;df_full.copy() numeric_cols&#160;=&#160;[&apos;Quantity&apos;,&#160;&apos;UnitPrice&apos;,&#160;&apos;Revenue&apos;,&#160;&apos;Profit&apos;] df_downcast[numeric_cols]&#160;=&#160;df_downcast[numeric_cols].apply(pd.to_numeric,&#160;downcast=&apos;float&apos; ) cat_cols&#160;=&#160;[&apos;Region&apos;,&#160;&apos;Product&apos;] df_downcast[cat_cols]&#160;=&#160;df_downcast[cat_cols].astype(&apos;category&apos;) df_downcast[&apos;Date&apos;]&#160;=&#160;pd.to_datetime(df_downcast[&apos;Date&apos;],&#160;errors=&apos;coerce&apos;) df_downcast.memory_usage(deep=True ).sum()&#160; #&#160;28.61&#160;MB Here, we have achieved over 87% memory savings after downcasting and conversion. This kind of performance tuning is a game changer when scaling up analysis. Alternatively, you can use pandas_downcast library ( https://pypi.org/project/pandas-downcast/ ) to automatically scan and find the minimum viable type for each column for downcasting. Use chunking When data is too big to fit into memory, you can read data in smaller chunks and process them in batches. As long as each chunk fits in memory, you can work with datasets that are much larger than memory. Many key pandas readers, like read_csv() , read_table() , and read_sql() , allow you to read large files in memory-friendly batches using the chunksize argument. For other file types, skiprows and nrows arguments would do the job. When you set chunksize to some integer, the reader function&#160;returns&#160;an iterable that you can use in a&#160;for&#160;loop to get and process only a&#160;fragment of the dataset in each iteration. The processed chunks are then concatenated to the processed_data Dataframe. Let us read sales_data in chunks and for each chunk, filter rows where Revenue &gt; 7000 , without even loading the full dataset into memory. Chunking helps by processing one piece at a time , keeping memory usage low, and avoiding full DataFrame loading. This is useful especially if the full file is massive. Refer to the following: #&#160;Reading&#160;full&#160;data start&#160;=&#160;time.time() df_full&#160;=&#160;pd.read_csv(&quot;sales_data.csv&quot;) df_full_filtered&#160;=&#160;df_full[df_full[ &quot;Revenue&quot;]&#160;&gt;&#160;7000] end&#160;=&#160;time.time() print(f&quot;Total&#160;time&#160;taken_No&#160;chunking:&#160;{end&#160;-&#160;start:.2f}&#160;seconds&quot;)&#160; #&#160;1.35&#160;sec #&#160;Chunked&#160;Reading filtered_data&#160;=&#160;pd.DataFrame() start&#160;=&#160;time.time() #&#160;Reading&#160;in&#160;chunks&#160;and&#160;concatenating&#160;processed&#160;data for&#160;chunk&#160;in&#160;pd.read_csv(&quot;sales_data.csv&quot;,&#160;chunksize=200_000): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;filtered&#160;=&#160;chunk[chunk[ &quot;Revenue&quot;]&#160;&gt;&#160;7000] &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;filtered_data&#160;=&#160;pd.concat([filtered_data,&#160;filtered]) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; end&#160;=&#160;time.time() print(f&quot;Total&#160;time&#160;taken:&#160;{end&#160;-&#160;start:.2f}&#160;seconds&quot;)&#160; #&#160;1.13&#160;sec On small datasets, like the one we have used, chunking might be slower and save little memory. Its real benefit appears with much larger files, when avoiding memory overflow is more important than speed. For example, if you are working with a 1.5 GB CSV file on an 8 GB RAM machine, you can read it in small chunks , say 100,000 rows at a time, the process will be slower, but memory usage will remain low and predictable. You should use chunked reading when you are dealing with huge files and stability is more important than speed. It ensures your system does not freeze, even if it takes a few extra seconds. Note: Chunking is a suitable option for the operations with no or minimal coordination between chunks. For complicated workflows, Python offers other libraries. Using Sparse series If a column has lots of missing values ( NaN ) or zeros, you can use pandas ’s Sparse format for compressing data. It stores only non-zero or non-missing values, and keeps track of where they are, which reduces memory usage. If you have a column with 10,000 rows, but only 100 have values, Excel still stores the full 10,000. Pandas ’s Sparse format avoids that. For example: print(df_full[ &quot;Bonus&quot;].memory_usage(deep=True)/(1024*1024))&#160; #&#160;7.63MB df_full[&quot;Bonus_sparse&quot;]&#160;=&#160; (df_full[&quot;Bonus&quot;] &#9; .astype(pd.SparseDtype(&quot;float&quot;,&#160;fill_value=0.0))) print(df_full[&quot;Bonus_sparse&quot;].memory_usage(deep=True)/(1024*1024))&#160; #&#160;5.72&#160;MB Vectorization and memory clean-up We would like to remind you about a basic yet important coding practice that can help in general with performance optimization. Using vectorized operations instead of loops, using numpy where possible, avoiding creation of temporary variables for chained operations, deleting the objects you no longer need are some habits you should develop early in your coding journey. The following code shows using numpy for column calculation: #&#160;Slow&#160;way_ &#160; pandas operation start&#160;=&#160;time.time() def &#160;label_profit(val): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; return &#160; &quot;High&quot; &#160; if &#160;val&#160;&gt;&#160; 500 &#160; else &#160; &quot;Low&quot; df_full[&quot;ProfitLabel_map&quot;]&#160;=&#160;df_full[ &quot;Profit&quot;].map(label_profit) end&#160;=&#160;time.time() print(f&quot;Mapping&#160;a&#160;fuction&#160;time:&#160;{end&#160;-&#160;start:.2f} &#160;seconds» )&#160; #&#160;0.16 #&#160;Fast way&#160;_numpy operation start&#160;=&#160;time.time() df_full[ &quot;ProfitLabel_vec&quot;]&#160;=&#160;np.where(df_full[&quot;Profit&quot;]&#160;&gt;&#160;500,&#160;&quot;High&quot;,&#160;&quot;Low&quot;) end&#160;=&#160;time.time() print(f&quot;Vectorized&#160;time:&#160;{end&#160;-&#160;start:.2f} &#160;seconds» )&#160; #&#160;0.08 Although Python automatically manages memory using a built-in garbage collector ( GC ), it tracks and reclaims memory when objects are no longer needed. You can still perform a forced garbage collection using gc.collect() from the gc module when: You are working with large data sets. You are inside long-running scripts or notebooks handling multiple large datasets. You have explicitly deleted large objects using del . You see memory not being released (e.g. via memory_profiler , psutil , etc.). It is like clearing cache in Excel after large queries. Other libraries For very large datasets such as 10M+ rows or multi-GB files (beyond memory limits), Python offers tools 6 like dask (an extension of pandas ) that mimics pandas code. The upcoming chapters will include further insights as we dive into specific reader and writer packages. Conclusion In this chapter, you have learned about a wide variety of Python tools that can speed up data exploration and report generation. Many of these tools are evolving, so you can always check official documentation and tutorials when you plan to use them. We have demonstrated all these tools using Spyder and Jupyter as our development environments. If you are working in Google Colab, some tools may require additional installations 7 , or specific configuration or may simply not work due to dependence on local setup. Be sure to refer to each library’s official documentation for environment-specific instructions, installation commands, or usage tips. In the upcoming chapters, you will learn about Excel-specific reader and writer packages in detail. This default behavior prioritizes numerical precision, aligning with Python&apos;s native floating-point type (which is implemented as a C double). Users can manually specify a lower-precision type like float32 to reduce memory usage when high precision is not required. 3 For common statistical functions, numpy offers both methods like arr.sum() and functions like np.sum(arr) that do the same thing.
