  - Python-Powered Excel C HAPTER 5 Automating File Management with Python Introduction Managing files efficiently is crucial for Excel professionals working in any domain, whether they are handling financial reports, sales data, or operational records. In this chapter, we will explore Python’s built-in file handling capabilities and automate key file management tasks using libraries such as os, shutil, and glob. We will cover how to read, write, move, copy, rename, and archive files dynamically, ensuring efficient workflow automation. This learning will help readers in implementing logging and scheduling mechanisms to track file changes and automate recurring tasks later. By the end of this chapter, readers will be able to streamline their file operations, reducing manual work and minimizing errors. Back in Chapter 1 , Python for Excel Users , you saw how Maya could generate a YouTube analytics report with a single click. Every month, she manually updates the data and reruns the same script to get her latest report. But what if she did not have to do this manually at all? With just a few extra lines of Python code written once, she can a utomatically rename and timestamp her downloaded YouTube data files, o rganize files into monthly folders, sort files by timestamp, or automatically identify to load the most recent file for analysis without any manual effort. In this chapter, we will learn how to make this a reality, turning repetitive tasks into effortless automation. For that to happen, let us start with the basics of file paths. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: Absolute and relative file paths File and directory operations Advanced file search with pattern-matching Reading and writing files Automation use cases A bsolute and relative file paths In Chapter 3 , Python Fundamentals , you have learned about the working directory. Apart from that, for several file handling tasks, such as moving files from one folder to another based on file names, extension, or date of creation, you need to understand how file paths work and what differentiates an absolute path from a relative path. You might have already guessed it, as the terms are simple to translate from English. Relative path means related , i.e., with reference to the current directory or older. For example, ./test.py is a relative path that means a file named test.py in the current directory. And the full file path starting from the root directory is called the absolute path. For example, D:/Excel_Python/test.py is an absolute path. While opening and saving files, you can mention the absolute path, making it explicit, or the relative path, making it shorter. As an absolute path contains full path information starting from the root, you can access files from any location (irrespective of your current working directly). This practice seems more convenient to beginners if they are okay with typing a little more. However, a script using an absolute path is not portable. This means that if your file 1 The location is D:/Excel_Python/test.py; it would not work on another PC where the file is in a different location (imagine you are buying a new laptop in the future, and the code breaks). If you move your file to another folder or you want to share your script with others, it will cause issues. We would recommend starting to use the relative path early on, and you can always generate an absolute path whenever needed using os module as follows: import os #&#160;Relative &#160;path&#160;(uncomment&#160;these&#160;relative&#160;paths&#160;one&#160;by&#160;one&#160;to&#160;notice&#160;the&#160;diff) relative_path&#160;=&#160;&apos;test.py&apos; # File in the current directory #&#160;relative_path&#160;=&#160;&apos;scripts/test.py&apos; # File inside a subfolder ‘scripts’ #&#160;If file&#160;is&#160;outside&#160;the&#160;current&#160;directory,(../ indicates one level up) # relative_path = &quot;../test.py&quot; # relative_path = &quot;../../test.py&quot; #&#160;Convert&#160;to&#160;absolute&#160;path absolute_path&#160;=&#160;os.path.abspath(relative_path)&#160;&#160; print(&quot;Absolute&#160;Path:&quot;,&#160;absolute_path)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Now&#160;it&apos;s&#160;a&#160;full&#160;path # Again, Getting Relative Path from absolute path rel_path = os.path.relpath(absolute_path) print(&apos;\nAgain Getting Relative Path:&apos;, rel_path) The path you create using absolute or relative methods does not necessarily have to exist, its existence can be checked using functions like os.path.exists() that we will discuss soon. More about file paths Python can help you check if a particular path exists or not, if a given path is a file or a directory, by using simple functions that are easy to remember, as the names are self-explanatory. This is something great about Python, just by reading function names, you can guess what it does. We recommend you try out some staple exercises on each of these functions. Run this code and observe what it does: filename&#160;=&#160;&apos;test.py&apos; if &#160; os.path.exists(filename): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;The&#160;file&#160;&apos;{filename}&apos;&#160;exists&#160;in&#160;the&#160;current&#160;working&#160;directory.&quot;) else: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;The&#160;file&#160;&apos;{filename}&apos;&#160;does&#160;not&#160;exist&#160;in&#160;the&#160;current&#160;working&#160;directory.&quot;) You might have realized os.path.exists(test.py) checks if the file test.py exists in the current working directory or not and returns True or False accordingly. Similarly, os.path.exists(&apos;path/to/file.txt&apos;) checks if the specified path exists. Many times, you will need to verify if the given path is a file or not, and the same for a directory; you can achieve this readily by: os.path.isfile(&apos;path/to/file.txt&apos;)&#160; #&#160;True&#160;for&#160;files os.path .isdir(&apos;path/to/directory&apos;)&#160;&#160; #&#160;True&#160;for&#160;directories Likewise, os.path.join(current_folder, file_name) concatenates path segments using the correct directory separator ( \ on windows and / on macOS or linux). Instead of manually concatenating directory names with separators, os.path.join() ensures paths are constructed in a crossplatform manner without worrying about the correct slash or delimiter. Note that it is not compulsory that the path you create using os.path.join() actually exists. current_folder&#160;=&#160;os.getcwd() # &apos;D:\\Excel_Python&apos; on windows machine file_name&#160;=&#160;&quot;test.py&quot; full_path = os.path.join(current_folder, filename) print(full_path) # D:\Excel_Python\test.py If your file is nested in a subfolder of any folder, you can still get the absolute file path by using the same function. os.path.join(&apos;folder&apos;, &apos;subfolder&apos;, &apos;file.txt&apos;) joins the path components into a single path: current_folder = os.getcwd() subfolder&#160;=&#160;&quot;scripts&quot; filename&#160;=&#160;&quot;test.py&quot; full_path&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(current_folder,&#160;subfolder,&#160;filename) print(full_path) # D:\Excel_Python\scripts\test.py on windows If you pass an empty string to os.path.join() , it simply ignores it. If a path segment is on a different drive or is an absolute path, all previous segments are ignored, and the drive is reset. os.path.join(&quot;folder&quot;,&#160;&quot;&quot;)&#160;&#160; #&#160;&apos;folder\\&apos; &#160;&#160; os. path.join(&apos;C:\\scripts&apos;,&#160;&apos;D:\\Excel_Python\\test.py&apos;)&#160; #&#160;&apos;D:\\Excel_Python\\test.py&apos; Warning: Avoid using a leading slash ( /filename or \filename ) in os.path.join() , as it treats a path with a slash as an absolute path and then all previous path segments are ignored. os.path.join( &apos;D:\\Excel_Python&apos;,&#160;&apos;\scripts\\test.py&apos;)&#160; #&#160;&apos;D:\\scripts\\test.py&apos; If you are working with user input paths, or reading paths from config files, databases, or APIs, or want to write cross-platform scripts, you would prefer to ensure a consistent format of file paths. As Windows uses \ (backslash) while Linux and macOS use / (forward slash) , running the same code on different machines can format paths differently. Leave the worry to Python as the function os.path.normpath() has you covered. For example, this script asks the user to submit file path ( input() function comes handy for user input) and the user copy-pastes D:/Excel_Python (from documentation or some other source) as file path, running the following code on my Windows machine will result in a path with mixed slashes potentially causing issues (notice the output): folder&#160;=&#160; input(&apos;Enter&#160;File&#160;Path:&#160;&apos;)&#160; #&#160;Type to the prompt_ &#160;D:/Excel_Python filename&#160;=&#160;&quot;test.py&quot; full_path&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(folder,&#160;filename) print(full_path)&#160; # D:/Excel_Python\test.py on windows machine We can clean the mess by running os.path.normpath(full_path) , as it gives a path based on the operating system . os.path.normpath(full_path)&#160; #&#160;&apos;D:\\Excel_Python\\test.py&apos; on windows machine Note: It is best practice to use os.path.normpath() or similar function while performing automated file operations where paths are generated dynamically. If you want to fetch a file name from a full file path like above, you can use os.path.basename() : file_path&#160;=&#160; &quot;D:/Projects/Reports/marketing_data.xlsx&quot; os.path.basename(file_path)&#160; #&#160;Output:&#160;marketing_data.xlsx Using os.path.splitext(file_name) helps you remove the extension (refer to the code file or try it yourself). Apart from dealing with working directory and file paths, os module also offers great functions to interact with the operating system in a portable way. Along with this module, shutil , glob , zipfile , fnmatch , and pathlib modules 2 open doors for amazing automation functionalities. Let us explore some of these if you are excited to automate your file management tasks with Python. File and directory operations As you are now familiar with file paths, you can now create, move, rename, and delete files or folders 3 to start with. For such operations, we have created test files and folders (provided in Chapter 05 folder of the companion repo). We would recommend you set Chapter 05 folder as your working directory. Otherwise , you can always use os.path.join() to get inside the chapter folder from your current working directory D:/Excel_Python . To get a list of all available files and subfolders in any folder, you can use os.scandir() 4 : os.scandir()&#160; #&#160;An iterator containing all &#160;files /folders &#160;in&#160;current directory list(os.scandir()) #&#160;list&#160;of&#160;files&#160;from&#160;your current working directory folder = &apos;D:\\Excel_Python&apos; # write path of any folder on your machine list(os.scandir(folder)) # list of files/folders from that path3. You might be thinking of getting files of a specific type, e.g., all Excel files. Explore it if you are super excited, or we will cover it soon. For now, you know how many total files there are in a folder (hope you remember the len() function!). Try to create, remove , or delete folders using these simple commands: #&#160;create &#160;a&#160;folder/directory&#160; in &#160;current&#160;working&#160;directory os.mkdir(&apos;test&apos;) # rename folder os.rename(&apos;test&apos;, &apos;test_backup&apos;) #&#160;Delete&#160;the&#160;folder (only if it’s empty, otherwise it raises an error) os.rmdir(&apos;test_backup&apos;) #&#160;rename any file (We have kept an excel file ‘empty.xlsx’ in Chapter 05 ) os.rename(‘empty.xlsx’,&#160;‘no_data.xlsx’) #&#160;delete&#160;any &#160;file os.remove (&apos;no_data.xlsx&apos;) You can also create nested folders using os.makedirs() (notice the spelling change and extra s ) and delete using shutil.rmtree() from shutil module (refer to the code file for detailed examples), which is another standard module for high-level file operations such as move, copy, zip, and unzip folders/files. For example, shutil.copy(&apos;source.txt&apos; , &apos;destination.txt&apos;) copies the source file to the destination, while s hutil.move(&apos;source.txt &apos;, &apos; destination_folder&apos;) moves the source file to the destination folder (refer to the code file). Also, let us see how to zip and unzip a folder: base_path = os.getcwd() folder_to_compress&#160;=&#160;&apos;Back-upFiles&apos; &#160; #&#160;Folder&#160;(in current dir) to&#160;compress&#160; zip_location&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(base_path, &apos;Back-upFiles&apos;)&#160; format &#160;=&#160; &apos;zip&apos; &#160;#&#160;Archive&#160;format&#160;such&#160;as&#160;&apos;tar&apos;,&#160;&apos;gztar&apos;,&#160;&apos;bztar&apos;,&#160;&apos;xztar&apos; #&#160;Create&#160;the&#160;archive shutil.make_archive(zip_location,&#160;format,&#160;folder_to_compress) Creates a compressed archive of the specified folder #&#160;To&#160;unzip&#160;a&#160;folder archive_path&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(base_path, &apos;Back-upFiles.zip&apos;) extract_to&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(base_path,&#160;&apos;restored_oldFiles&apos;) #&#160;Unpack&#160;the&#160;archive shutil.unpack_archive(archive_path,&#160;extract_to)&#160;&#160; For now, these tasks might feel trivial to do with Python, but this is needed to give you a stepping stone for file organization and automation, as you will see later in this chapter. Renaming files based on dates or departments, consolidating and transferring files to different folders, and archiving old reports, many such tasks can be done with these basics. As a next step, you might want to explore if there is any way to traverse nested folders and search for files (that will help you in searching files in large folders or organizing files even if those are deeply nested) without even opening your file explorer (Windows) or Finder (macOS). The os.walk() function in Python is used to traverse directories and get a list of all folders, subfolders, and files inside a given path. This function walks through the folder structure and returns a generator 5 that creates a tuple of 3 values ( current_folder , sub-folder in this, files in the same). You can either use next() or a for loop to traverse the path. Every time the generator is called, it will follow each folder recursively until no further sub-folders are available from the initial folder that the walk was called upon. To illustrate, we have created a folder named Report s with a structure as given in Figure 5.1 in the Chapter 05 folder. Figure 5.1 : Folder structure of Reports folder You can also use the same to replicate the code example or try out the functions on any other folder on your machine, and your output would vary accordingly: reports_folder =&#160;os.path.join(os.getcwd(), “Reports”) # &#160;Change&#160;this&#160;to&#160;your&#160;actual&#160;folder for &#160;root,&#160;dirs,&#160;files&#160; in &#160;os.walk( reports_folder): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot; &#x1f4c2; &#160; Root &#160;Directory:&#160; {root}&quot;)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Prints&#160;each scanned &#160;directory&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;&#160;Subfolders:&#160;{dirs}&quot;)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Prints&#160;list&#160;of&#160;subfolders &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;&#160;Files:&#160;{files}&quot;)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Prints&#160;list&#160;of&#160;files &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(&quot;-&quot; &#160;*&#160; 40)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Just&#160;a&#160;separator&#160;for&#160;clarity This will print the root directory, its subfolders, and all files iteratively. Else, you can use next() keyword to fetch the 3-tuple: my_gen&#160;=&#160;os.walk(reports_folder) next(my_gen)&#160; #&#160;Keep&#160;doing&#160;it&#160;until&#160;it&#160;covers&#160;all&#160;subfolders You can do a lot more with this function, like searching, sorting, and organizing files or folders across multiple folders, identifying old files for cleanup, or excluding folders from your search, etc. This is just the beginning, and there is a lot that can be achieved further. At this point, we have already discussed a lot. Take a pause, and experiment with your own files and folders to fully grasp what is covered so far, and then start fresh for learning powerful pattern-matching with the glob module. Advanced file search with pattern-matching On Linux and Unix systems, the shell uses glob methods for matching files with a specific pattern. Inspired by the same, Python offers such functionalities in the glob module. It is like searching for files or folders in Windows Explorer or macOS Finder, but using Python. Get all the files and folders in a specified folder If you have created a Reports folder as discussed in the previous section, you will get the same output: import &#160;glob reports_folder&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(os.getcwd(),&#160;&apos;Reports&apos;) content&#160;=&#160;glob.glob(f&quot;{reports_folder}/*&quot;) # *&#160;means&#160;&quot;everything&quot; print(content)&#160;&#160; [&apos;D:\\ Excel_Python\\Chapter 05\\Reports\\Q1&apos;, &apos;D:\\ Excel_Python\\Chapter 05\\Reports \\Q2&apos;, &apos;D:\\ Excel_Python\\Chapter 05\\Reports\\summary.docx&apos;] As you noticed, glob.glob(f&quot;{reports_folder}/*&quot;) returns a list of all files and folders inside the reports_folder directory. A subtle difference, you might have noticed that glob. glob() returns full paths while os.listdir() and os.scandir( ) which gives file names such as summary.docx. Get specific types of files You can also fetch a specific type of files, such as Word, Excel, PDF, images, or more: docx_files&#160;=&#160;glob.glob(f&quot;{reports_folder}/*.docx&quot;) print(docx_files) # *.docx means all docx files xlsx_files&#160;=&#160;glob.glob(os.path.join(reports_folder,&#160;&apos;Q1&apos;,&#160;&apos;*.xlsx&apos;)) print(xlsx_files) # *.xlsx means all xlsx files Here, to find .xlsx files in the subfolder Q1, we have mentioned the specific path manually. You can search for files inside the current folder, and its subfolders with recursive=True using ** xlsx_files = glob.glob(os.path.join(reports_folder, &apos;**&apos;, &apos;*.xlsx&apos;), recursive= True) # Finds all .xlsx files inside &quot; Reports&quot; and all its subfolders, regardless of depth print(xlsx_files)&#160; Get files by prefix, suffix, and more During the curation of this book, Word files were created and kept in a folder called Book Writing inside the ongoing working directory. However, soon we realized the need to organize that folder. Many Word files were preliminary chapter drafts, and the names were like ch_1 Intro, ch_2 setup version2, exercises_ch_3 fundamentals , etc. However, there were other supporting Word files too. We wanted to collect all chapter drafts (maybe to put in the chapter-wise folders later). Here is what followed next: book&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(os.getcwd(),&#160; &apos;Book&#160;Writing&apos;) chapters&#160;=&#160;glob.glob(os.path.join(book,&#160;&apos;*ch_*.docx&apos;)) print(&apos;Chapter&#160;Drafts:&#160;\n&apos;,&#160;chapters) You can try out one such example on your own files. To collect all Word files that end with Python and to collect all Word files that contain extra , we run this code that helped us in further organizing our folder: #&#160;finding&#160;files&#160;with&#160;suffix&#160;&apos;Python&apos; topic_python&#160;=&#160;glob.glob(os .path.join(book,&#160;&apos;*Python.docx&apos;)) print(&apos;Files&#160;Related&#160;to&#160;topic&#160;Python:&#160;\n&apos;,&#160;topic_python) #&#160;files&#160;that&#160;contain&#160;&apos;extras&apos;&#160;anywhere extra_files&#160;=&#160;glob.glob(os.path.join(book,&#160;&apos;*extra*.docx&apos;)) print( &apos;Extra&#160;stuff&#160;for&#160;chapters:&#160;\n&apos;,&#160;extra_files) You must have noticed the use of capital P in Python . What if we write python with lowercase letters? It is a good time to warn you that on a Windows machine, glob patterns are case-insensitive, so *Python.docx will match both Excel_Python.docx and excel_python.docx . On Mac and Linux, you would need lower() method to make the search case-insensitive. It is recommended that you apply searches on your own files and folders. So now, we can ask Python to create the required folder structure and segregate files nicely in those folders. It is a quick and error-free process, and we can reuse that Python script with minor changes to do such organization elsewhere too. We would recommend you practice using Python with your own file management tasks. Once you practice all these, you can be in a state of ease for your automation tasks. Know that there is no expectation to mug it up. You can always come back to this chapter for all your file management-related needs. Soon, we will explore different use cases which you might find useful (or at least related to what you do on a regular basis). One more step before we discuss use cases, it will help you to read or write text and binary files directly using Python. Let us see it in action! Reading and writing files To create log files or summary reports, you might need to work with text files. Also, sometimes for automation or exporting reports to web pages, you might need to write json or html files. Python allows you to use open() method for working with Text, CSV, JSON, HTML, XML files, etc. Text files The preferred way of reading or writing such files is to use the context manager with . Let us understand using some examples: #&#160;Reading&#160;a&#160;text&#160;file&#160;‘sample_1.txt’ from current working directory with &#160; open(&apos; sample_1.txt&apos;,&#160;&apos;r&apos;)&#160;as &#160;file: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;content&#160;=&#160;file.read() print(content) # prints the content of the file #&#160;Writing&#160;content to&#160;a&#160;new text&#160;file&#160;named ‘sample_2.txt&apos; with &#160; open(&apos;sample_2.txt&apos;,&#160;&apos;w&apos;)&#160;as &#160;file: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;file.write( &apos;Hello,&#160;I&#160;am&#160;learning&#160;Python&apos;) You can also read or write files directly using the open() method. However, you would need to close the file after using it. See the following example: #&#160;Reading&#160;a&#160;text&#160;file&#160;‘sample_1.txt’ file = open(&apos; sample_1.txt&apos;,&#160;&apos;r&apos;)&#160; content&#160;=&#160;file.read() print(content) # prints the content of the file file.close() CSV files Likewise, you can also read and write .csv files. However, we usually prefer to use . csv module (as shown below) or pandas library to do so (to be discussed in the next chapter): #&#160;Reading&#160;a&#160;csv&#160;file with &#160; open(&quot;sample_3.csv&quot;,&#160;&quot;r&quot;)&#160;as &#160;file: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;content&#160;=&#160;file.read()&#160;&#160; #&#160;Works&#160;fine print(content) #&#160;Writing&#160;a&#160;CSV&#160;file&#160; with &#160; open(&quot;sample_4.csv&quot;,&#160;&quot;w&quot;)&#160;as &#160;file: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;file.write( &quot;Date,Product,Quantity,Revenue\n&quot;)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Header&#160;row &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;file.write( &quot;2024-04-01,Laptop,5,5000\n&quot;) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;file.write( &quot;2024-04-02,Mouse,15,300\n&quot;) JSON file You might also need to use a JSON file to import data from APIs or web services and to create structured log files for automation use cases. A JavaScript Object Notation ( JSON ) 6 file is a simple text file used to store structured data, in a key-value format, similar to a Python dictionary. To write or read json file, you can use json module: #&#160;Writing&#160;JSON&#160;file&#160;using&#160;json&#160;module import &#160;json data&#160;=&#160;{&quot;name&quot;:&#160;&quot;Alice&quot;,&#160;&quot;age&quot;:&#160;25 } with &#160; open(&quot;sample_5.json&quot;,&#160;&quot;w&quot;)&#160;as &#160;f: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;json.dump(data,&#160;f) #&#160;Reading&#160;JSON file using json module with &#160; open(&quot;sample_5.json&quot;,&#160;&quot;r&quot; )&#160;as &#160;f: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;loaded_data&#160;=&#160;json.load(f) print(loaded_data) From json module, json.dump() saves (writes) data to a JSON file and json.load() reads data from a JSON file back into Python. More examples of working with html or xml files, etc., are mentioned in the code file. Automation use cases Whether you are doing automation testing, software development, or analysis and reporting, you perform various file management tasks, such as organizing or sorting files and folders, managing reports, log files, consolidating multiple CSV or Excel files for analysis, or renaming files based on date, department, or something else. You can allow Python to assist you with repetitive and manual tasks while you get all the praise for efficiency. Some simple yet powerful use-cases will help you connect the dots and mechanize boring stuff. Batch renaming files In case you are planning to rename multiple files, you basically need to locate the file folder and then loop through the files to rename (including or excluding the subfolders). For the purpose of the demo, you can use a folder named testfiles given in Chapter 05 folder of the companion repo. This folder contains some files and a subfolder. The following code will add a prefix Jan_ to all filenames (skipping the subfolder): folder_path = os.path.join(os.getcwd(), &apos;testfiles&apos;) prefix&#160;=&#160;&quot;Jan_&quot; &#160; #&#160;Prefix&#160;to&#160;add&#160;to&#160;filenames #&#160;Loop&#160;through&#160;all&#160;files&#160;in&#160;the&#160;directory&#160;to&#160;add&#160;prefix&#160;to&#160;all&#160;file&#160;names&#160; (skipping&#160;sub-folders) for &#160;entry&#160; in &#160;os.scandir(folder_path): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;entry.is_file():&#160;&#160; #&#160;Skip&#160;directories,&#160;process&#160;only&#160;files &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;old_path&#160;=&#160;entry.path&#160;&#160; #&#160;Directly&#160;get&#160;full&#160;path &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;new_filename&#160;=&#160;prefix&#160;+&#160;entry.name&#160;&#160; #&#160;Add&#160;prefix &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;new_path&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(folder_path,&#160;new_filename) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;os.rename(old_path,&#160;new_path)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Rename&#160;file &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;Renamed:&#160;{entry.name} &#160; ➝ &#160; {new_filename}&quot;) If you need to undo the renaming of files, you can do that with basic string methods such as startswith() and slicing (refer to the code file). However, it is recommended to create a log file (that saves original names before renaming) and use it to revert anytime you need. The following code does exactly the same: prefix&#160;=&#160;&quot;Jan_&quot; &#160; #&#160;Prefix&#160;to&#160;add&#160;to&#160;filenames log_file&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(folder_path,&#160;&quot;rename_log.json&quot;)&#160; #&#160;To&#160;store&#160;original&#160;names #&#160;Task&#160;1:&#160;To&#160;save&#160;original&#160;filenames&#160;before&#160;renaming rename_log&#160;=&#160;{} for &#160;entry&#160; in &#160;os.scandir(folder_path):&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;entry.is_file()&#160; and &#160; not &#160;entry.name.startswith(prefix):&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;old_path&#160;=&#160;entry.path&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;new_filename&#160;=&#160;prefix&#160;+&#160;entry.name&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;new_path&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(folder_path,&#160;new_filename) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;os.rename(old_path,&#160;new_path) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;rename_log[new_filename]&#160;=&#160;entry.name&#160;&#160; #&#160;Store&#160;mapping&#160;in&#160;dict &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;Renamed:&#160;{entry.name} &#160; ➝ &#160; {new_filename}&quot;) #&#160;Save&#160;the&#160;mapping&#160;to&#160;a&#160;JSON&#160;log&#160;file with &#160; open(log_file,&#160;&quot;w&quot;)&#160;as &#160;f: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;json.dump(rename_log,&#160;f) print (&quot; ✅ &#160;Batch&#160;renaming&#160;complete!&#160;Original&#160;names&#160;saved&#160;in&#160;rename_log.json» ) #&#160;Task&#160;2:&#160;To&#160;load&#160;the&#160;log&#160;file&#160;and&#160;revert&#160;names &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; with &#160; open(log_file,&#160;&quot;r&quot;)&#160;as &#160;f: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;rename_log&#160;=&#160;json.load(f) current_files&#160;=&#160;[entry.name&#160;for &#160;entry&#160; in &#160;os.scandir(folder_path)] #&#160;Get&#160;current&#160;files&#160;in&#160;directory for &#160;new_name,&#160;old_name&#160; in &#160;rename_log.items(): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;old_path&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(folder_path,&#160;new_name) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;new_path&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(folder_path,&#160;old_name) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;new_name&#160; in &#160;current_files: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;os.rename(old_path,&#160;new_path) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;Reverted:&#160;{new_name} &#160; ➝ &#160; {old_name}&quot;) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;os.remove(log_file)&#160;#&#160;Remove&#160;log&#160;file&#160;after&#160;reverting print(&quot; ✅ &#160;Undo&#160;complete!&#160;All&#160;files&#160;restored&#160;to&#160;original&#160;names.» )&#160; Python even allows you to create a simple GUI where you can do this all with a button click. Possibilities are unlimited, you can explore the creation of such applications on your own later, if you are interested in GUI application development. Categorizing files by extension As we have mentioned earlier, in the initial phase of writing this book, we had created a folder Book Writing and kept all related work there, be it a word file, image, data, or code. When we planned a proper structure of the directory, we wrote a few lines of Python code to isolate all word files (mostly chapter drafts) from code files and images (that we are creating for the book). To give you an idea, here is the sample code (you can modify as per your requirements): import &#160;os,&#160;shutil folder_path&#160;=&#160;&apos;path/to/directory/you&#160;want&#160;to/organize&apos; # or folder_path = os.path.join(os.getcwd(), &apos;testfiles&apos;) #&#160;Categorizing&#160;files&#160;by&#160;extension file_extensions&#160;=&#160;{ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;.docx&apos;:&#160;&apos;WordFiles&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;.jpg&apos;:&#160;&apos;Images&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;.py&apos;:&#160;&apos;CodeFiles&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;.ipynb&apos; :&#160;&apos;CodeFiles&apos; &#160; #&#160;Notebooks } for &#160;entry&#160; in &#160;os.scandir(folder_path): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;entry.is_file(): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;file_ext&#160;=&#160;os.path.splitext(entry.name)[ 1] &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;file_ext&#160; in &#160;file_extensions: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;sub_folder_name&#160;=&#160;file_extensions[file_ext]&#160; #&#160;Creating&#160;sub-folders&#160;with&#160;names&#160;by&#160;file&#160;types &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;sub_folder&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(folder_path,&#160;sub_folder_name) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;os.makedirs(sub_folder,&#160;exist_ok= True) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Moving&#160;files&#160;to&#160;respective&#160;folder &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;source&#160;=&#160;entry.path &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;destination&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(sub_folder,&#160;entry.name) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;shutil.move(source,&#160;destination) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;Moved&#160;{entry.name} &#160;→&#160; {sub_folder_name}&quot;) Deleting older Excel files To comply with the company policy of data retention, you are to delete all the older Excel files and free up storage. You can do it by running a short Python script like the one given here. Warning: Running this script on any folder will permanently delete Excel files older than three years. We suggest creating a copy of the demo folder to safely test this code. #&#160;This&#160;script&#160;removes&#160;all&#160;.xlsx&#160;and&#160;.xls&#160;files&#160;that&#160;haven&apos;t&#160;been&#160;modified&#160;in&#160;over&#160;3&#160;years. import &#160;os,&#160;time folder_path&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(os.getcwd(),&#160;&apos;demo&apos;)&#160; #&#160;Change&#160;to&#160;your&#160;folder&#160;path three_years_ago&#160;=&#160;time.time()&#160;-&#160;(3 &#160;*&#160; 365 &#160;*&#160; 24 &#160;*&#160; 60 &#160;*&#160; 60)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Convert&#160;years&#160;to&#160;seconds for &#160;entry&#160; in &#160;os.scandir(folder_path): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;file_path&#160;=&#160;entry.path &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;entry.is_file()&#160; and &#160;entry.name.endswith(( &apos;.xlsx&apos;,&#160;&apos;.xls&apos;)): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;mod_time&#160;=&#160;os.path.getmtime(file_path)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Last&#160;modified&#160;time &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;mod_time&#160;&lt;&#160;three_years_ago:&#160;&#160; #&#160;Check&#160;if&#160;older&#160;than&#160;3&#160;years &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;os.remove(file_path)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Delete&#160;file &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print (f&quot;Deleted&#160;old&#160;file:&#160;{entry.name}&quot;) If you need to send files or folders to the trash (or Recycle Bin), you can&#160;use the third-party library&#160; send2trash &#160;rather than&#160; os.remove() &#160; or&#160; shutil.rmtree() , which deletes files permanently.&#160;These files can be restored from the Recycle Bin. send2trash &#160;works across all platforms (Windows, macOS, Linux) and provides a safer alternative.&#160; Timestamp and organize by month Remember, Maya from Chapter 1 , Python for Excel Users . She uses a Python script to automatically timestamp her downloaded data and organize it in month-wise folders for consistency and better structure of her work. Sample code she might be using: import &#160;os,&#160;shutil,&#160;time source_folder&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(os.getcwd(),&#160;&apos;organizeByTimeStamp_Case&apos;)&#160; #&#160;Change&#160;to&#160;your&#160;folder&#160;path destination_root&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(current_dir,&#160;&apos;Organized_Reports&apos;)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Where&#160;to&#160;organize&#160;files os.makedirs(destination_root,&#160;exist_ok=True)&#160; for &#160;entry&#160; in &#160;os.scandir(source_folder): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;entry.is_file():&#160;&#160; #&#160;Ensure&#160;it&apos;s&#160;a&#160;file &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;mod_time&#160;=&#160;os.path.getmtime(entry.path)&#160; #&#160;Get&#160;file&apos;s&#160;last&#160;modified&#160;timestamp &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;formatted_time&#160;=&#160;time.strftime( &quot;%Y-%m-%d&quot;,&#160;time.localtime(mod_time))&#160;&#160; #&#160;YYYY-MM-DD &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Extract&#160;year&#160;and&#160;month&#160;for&#160;folder&#160;organization &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;year_month&#160;=&#160;time.strftime( &quot;%Y-%m&quot;,&#160;time.localtime(mod_time))&#160;&#160; #&#160;YYYY-MM &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Create&#160;destination&#160;folder&#160;for&#160;that&#160;year&#160;and&#160;month &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;destination_folder&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(destination_root,&#160;year_month) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;os.makedirs(destination_folder,&#160;exist_ok= True) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;New&#160;file&#160;name&#160;with&#160;timestamp &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;name,&#160;ext&#160;=&#160;os.path.splitext(entry.name) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;new_filename&#160;=&#160; f&quot;{name}_{formatted_time}{ext}&quot; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;new_path&#160;=&#160;os.path.join(destination_folder,&#160;new_filename) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;shutil.move(entry.path,&#160;new_path)&#160;#&#160;Move&#160;and&#160;rename&#160;file &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;shutil.copy(entry.path,&#160;new_path)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Copy&#160;and&#160;rename&#160;file &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; print(f&quot;Moved/Copied:&#160;{entry.name} &#160; ➝ &#160; {new_filename} &#160;in&#160; {destination_folder}&quot;) There can be many more such use cases that you can explore now, for example, try moving all Excel files from multiple folders into one master folder. Conclusion If you have come this far, you might have realized that learning Python is a good investment of your time, and it can do things that many other modern Excel-related tools cannot. In summary, this chapter covered file and directory operations, working with file paths, and reading or writing text files, along with some automation use cases. We have used Python standard modules such as os, shutil (abbreviated for shell utility), and glob in this chapter. If you are experienced Python users, you can also explore the pathlib module, which provides an object-oriented approach to handling filesystem paths. In the subsequent chapters, you will see how Python can do the heavy lifting, analyzing data through summaries, charts, and pivots. Once that is done, you will likely want to write those reports back to files on your machine. The learnings from this chapter will aid you in organizing those files. You should create a log file and use it to undo the rename again. Add timestamps, such as filename_yyyy-mm-dd, to file names in a test folder. E.g., Report.xlsx should be renamed as Report_2025-02-04.xlsx in this folder. How can you use Python to quickly find all invoices related to a specific client ? Your script works perfectly on Windows, but when run on Mac or Linux, it fails due to incorrect file paths. How can you modify it to work across different operating systems? What are some possible errors when renaming or moving files using Python, and how can you handle them to prevent script failures? If you are a cybersecurity professional, how would you use Python to monitor a folder for unauthorized file modifications? 1 In windows folder path is written using backslashes (\) while on Mac or Linux it is forward slash (/). Since backslash (\) is also an escape character in Python (e.g., \n, \t), you often need to replace it with forward slash (/), double backslashes (\\) or raw strings (r&quot;...&quot;) to avoid warnings/errors or unintended characters. 2 Modules like os , shutil , glob , zipfile , fnmatch , and pathlib are all built into Python&apos;s standard library, so you can use them without needing to install. 3 Folder and directory are often used interchangeably; directory is the technical term used in programming. 4 listdir: os.listdir(path) works similarly, but os.scandir() is faster and more efficient since its DirEntry objects provide file type ( is_file() , is_dir() ) and size ( stat().st_size ) without extra os.path calls 5 In Python, a generator is a special type of object that produces values one at a time instead of returning them all at once. It is memoryefficient because it generates items on demand, making them useful for handling large datasets or streams of data. 6 You can open a JSON file in Notepad, any text editor or web browser.   - Python-Powered Excel C HAPTER 2 Setting Up Python Introduction In this chapter, you will learn how to install Python and the required external packages (with or without setting up a virtual environment) on your machine. You will also learn to work on IDEs such as Spyder, and Jupyter, as well as cloud-based environment, such as Google Colab. Alternatively, you can choose any other IDE of your preference for running Python code. By the end of this chapter, you will be able to run your first Python (Hello World) program using various options. Structure This chapter will cover the following topics: Google Colab for Python execution without setup Python distribution and Anaconda Complete installation guide for Anaconda Python IDEs and code editors Python packages Setting up your first virtual environment Your first Python program Google Colab for Python execution without setup If you want to practice Python without installing anything. You can use Google Colaboratory (Colab in short) ( https://colab.research.google.com/ ), a cloud-based platform to write and run your Python code in your browser, just like an online Excel sheet. You will need an internet connection and a Google account for using Google Colab. It can be used for quick tutorials as it allows you to practice or teach Python from anywhere, at home, in an office, or a café, while offering amazing features (we found some features better than Jupyter 1 ). It allows users to save, open, and share notebooks directly with Google Drive or GitHub. Notebooks are files with .ipynb extension that not only include code and output but also text, headings, and images, all in one place. These are like an Excel workbook with formulas, charts, and comments explaining the analysis. Instead of writing all your code at once, you write in small blocks (called cells) and run them one by one. Cells help you test and tweak small parts of your code easily. You see results instantly below the code. In Chapter 1 , Python for Excel Users, you have used a Colab notebook to run code and generate a report. Follow these steps to create your first Colab notebook and write some code: Open browser and go to Google Colab ( https://colab.research.google.com/ ) and sign-in with your Gmail account. Click New Notebook . In the cell (where you see your cursor blinking), type: print(&quot;Hello,&#160;Excel&#160;Users!&#160;Welcome&#160;to&#160;Python!&quot;) Press Shift + Enter to run. Your output appears below, and a new cell code is added. If you press Ctrl + Enter , cell runs but no new cell is added. Explore options to add code or text cells, run code, etc. Figure 2.1 shows basic navigation: Figure 2.1 : First look at Colab’s interface Also, Colab comes with generative AI features to assist in coding. We recommend you turn off generative AI features while learning Python. Go to Tools | Settings | AI Assistance and click on Hide generative AI features as shown in the following figure: Figure 2.2 : Turning off Colab’s generative AI assistance You might also want to look at other settings such as dark mode of editor, font and font size etc. Try it out and make yourself comfortable with the tool. Opening, saving, and uploading files To start with, you would need to open, save, rename the notebook, or upload one from your local machine. This is how you can do these tasks: To save your work, click on File and then Save . To rename the file: your notebook is saved as Untitled0.ipynb (or similar) by default. To rename it, either: Click directly on the filename at the top (where it says Untitled0 ), type a new name (like Python Basics.ipynb ), and press Enter . (Refer Figure 2.1 ) Or go to File | Rename and enter the new name there. To open an existing notebook, click File | Open notebook , then choose from Google Drive 2 , GitHub 3 (public repo), or Upload (from your computer). You might not have any files yet, except for the one you have recently saved or any notebooks you have downloaded from a GitHub repo. Running Python notebook from GitHub This book’s code is available in the companion GitHub repo. You can either type the examples yourself or download the code files ( .py or .ipynb extensions) to open on your machine. Also, you can run the provided notebooks (files with .ipynb extension) directly in Colab. Here are two easy ways to run those notebooks in Colab without even downloading: Using the Open in Colab button (Recommended for first-time users. This was done in Chapter 1, Python for Excel Users ): Open the GitHub repo link of the book. Look for the Open in Colab button (usually at the top of the notebook or README). Click the button, the notebook opens directly in Colab and sign-in to your google account. Go to Runtime | Run all (shortcut Ctrl + F9 or Cmd + F9 on macOS) to run the entire notebook. You can also explore other run options from this menu. To run a single cell, use Ctrl + Enter ( Cmd + Enter on macOS) to execute without moving to the next cell or Shift + Enter to execute and move to the next cell. Using the GitHub Link (Also works for notebooks without the Open in Colab button): Copy the link to the notebook ( .ipynb file) from GitHub. Open Google Colab . Go to File | Open notebook | GitHub tab. Paste the link and click Search . Choose your notebook from the list and open it. Execute entire notebook with Runtime | Run all or Run cell by cell. For notebooks available in your local machine, you can go to File | Open notebook | Upload and browse the file from its location to open in Colab. Basic markdown guide for notebooks In notebooks like Colab, you can use markdown to add headings, notes, lists, links, images, videos, and more, helping you create clean, readable, and professional-looking reports. You may have noticed that, along with code cells, there are text cells. This is where you write your notes using Markdown and run them to display nicely formatted text. Let us add a Text Cell and type: #&#160;This&#160;is&#160;a&#160;Heading Just&#160;adding&#160;Some&#160;Text. Trying&#160;out&#160; **Bold Text** &#160;&#160;and&#160; *Italic Text* &#160;&#160; 1. &#160;Trying&#160;to&#160;add&#160;numbering. 2. &#160;As&#160;we&#160;do&#160;in&#160;MS&#160;Word. 3. &#160;I&#160;can&#160; also&#160;see&#160;preview&#160;here. Now, add another Text Cell and run this: ##&#160;This &#160; is &#160; a &#160; SubHeading Trying &#160; to &#160; add &#160; `code &#160; chunk` &#160; and &#160; [link](&#160;https: //www.linkedin.com/in/drnishaarora/)&#160;here. •&#160;Also&#160;adding&#160;bullet&#160;points. •&#160;You&#160;can&#160;explore&#160;inserting&#160;image,&#160;emoji&#160;or&#160;even&#160;latex&#160;typing&#160;here. Figure 2.3 displays Google Colab Notebook with Markdown text and Table of contents: Figure 2.3 : Google Colab Notebook with Markdown text You might have noticed, it is super easy to create documents or reports in Colab using these basic markdown symbols 4 : What you want Use this in Colab text cell Example output Level 1 heading # Heading 1 Heading 1 Level 2 heading ## Heading 2 Heading 2 Level 3 heading ### Heading 3 Heading 3 Bold text **Bold Text** Bold Text Italic text *Italic Text* Italic Text Numbered list 1. First item 2. Second item 1. First item 2. Second item Bulleted list - Item one - Item two Item one Item two Link [Click Here](https://www.example.com) Click Here Inline code `print(x)` &#160; print(x) Table 2.1 : Markdown basic syntax You can also do text formatting by clicking on the respective button in a text cell as shown in the figure: Click here Figure 2.4 : Text formatting options (left) and Markdown cell preview (right) in Colab Colab offers many helpful features, like an automatic Table of Contents, which makes it easy to navigate through your notebook. You can explore more features as you go. Notebook versus Python script You can consider notebooks ( .ipynb file) as interactive documents, like an eBook or a report. They can include your Python code, the output (like results and charts), headings, a table of contents, and even notes, images, videos, links, all in one place. These are perfect for exploration, explanations, and sharing your work in a single file. However, if you only want to keep just the code, without all the extra explanations and outputs, it is better to use a Python script . These are simple text files with a .py extension, made purely for writing and saving code. They are like a list of formulas, intended to be reused elsewhere. Python scripts are lightweight and can even be opened and edited using any basic text editor (like Notepad or Notepad++). You can choose to create Python files or notebooks (code remains the same) as per your needs. In Colab, you can easily download your work as either a Notebook ( .ipynb ) or a Script ( .py ). However, downloading a notebook as a .py file is often not very helpful because you will lose the nice structure, headings, and outputs. The comments and markdown notes do not carry over well, and the file may feel messy. Usually, Colab or Jupyter is preferred for working with notebooks and other IDEs such as Spyder are used for Python scripts. We will talk about IDEs soon. Python distribution and Anaconda Although you can run and practice Python code in Colab, if you are serious about learning more, you should also install Python on your machine. You can quickly download and install Python from its official website https://www.python.org/ for free. However, it is better to use a Python distribution to have access to a wide range of pre-installed libraries and maybe other features too. You might be wondering about Python distribution and what it involves. A Python distribution is a pre-packaged bundle that includes the Python interpreter, standard library, and a collection of additional packages and tools. Anaconda, Miniconda, and WinPython (Windows only) are some popular Python distributions. Anaconda is perfect for analysts or Excel users who want an easy-to-use Python environment without much setup. It is designed for data tasks and comes with many pre-installed packages, saving you time. Anaconda Navigator make it simple to install, update, and launch tools like JupyterLab and Spyder without using the command line. Let us understand it using a simple analogy. Imagine you just bought a brand-new smartphone. Before you start using it, you need to: Set it up properly. Install the essential apps. Configure settings to make sure everything runs smoothly. Now, think of Python as your smartphone, a powerful tool that can do amazing things. But just like a phone without apps and configurations, a plain Python needs some set-up and installations. This is where Anaconda Distribution comes in. Anaconda is like a fully loaded smartphone that comes with all the essential apps pre-installed. Instead of manually downloading every app (or package) one by one, Anaconda gives you a complete package with Python, useful data analysis tools, and some easy-to-use interfaces (such as Spyder and Jupyter). Installing vanilla Python (plain Python from Python.org) is like getting a bare-bones phone, you will have to manually install and configure everything yourself. This gives you more control but also leads to more hassle. Other options like Miniconda or WinPython are lighter but require you to set things up yourself, which can be confusing for beginners. With Anaconda, you just install and start working, no extra hassle. System requirement for Anaconda Anaconda works on all major operating systems like Windows, Mac, and Linux. As of November 2025, you need Windows 10 version 1809 or later, 64-bit macOS 12.1 + (for Apple Silicon), or Linux , including Ubuntu 20.04, Red Hat/AlmaLinux/Rocky Linux 8+, and others. If your operating system is older than what is currently supported, you can find older versions of the Anaconda installers in the archive that might work for you. Anaconda is a full Python toolkit, so it may take up to 5–6 GB after installation. If you create a separate environment for this book (recommended), it may take a few extra GB, depending on operating system, cached downloads, and package dependencies. If you are short on space, you can still follow the book by installing packages directly in the base environment. Complete installation guide for Anaconda Now, let us get Anaconda installed. Follow this step-by-step guide, and you will be ready to use Anaconda. Downloading Anaconda The steps are as follows: Go to Anaconda Home Page at https://www.anaconda.com/download and click on Skip registration . Refer to Figure 2.5 : Figure 2.5 : Anaconda distribution home page Click the right installer (Refer to Figure 2.6 ) based on your operating system (Windows, MacOS, or Linux) and flavor. For Mac users, we would recommend using a suitable Graphical Installer. Figure 2.6 : Available installers Downloading should start automatically. If it does not, click Try Again . Installing Anaconda Instructions for Windows users are as follows: Locate the downloaded .exe file (usually in the Downloads folder). Double-click to run it. The Anaconda Setup Wizard will open. Click Next to begin the installation, shown as follows: ” and “Cancel” buttons." /> Figure 2.7 : Anaconda Setup Wizard Accept the license agreement by clicking I Agree , as follows: Figure 2.8 : License Agreement Choose Just Me (unless you want to install it for all users). Click Next . Refer to Figure 2.9 : Figure 2.9 : Installation Type The default location is fine (e.g., C:\Users\UserName\Anaconda3 for windows and ~/anaconda3/ for macOS/Linux). However, if your C drive is low on space , you can install Anaconda on the D drive or another partition with enough free space. Refer to the following figure: Figure 2.10 : Choose Install Location Keep the other default settings the same. Refer to Figure 2.11 : Figure 2.11 : Advanced Installation Options Click Install and wait for the process to be completed (this may take a few minutes). Once done, click Next . Refer to Figure 2.12 : Figure 2.12 : The next step Once installation is done, click Finish , as follows: Figure 2.13 : Completing installation After successful installation, it opens Getting Started guide and Anaconda Navigator. If Anaconda Navigator is not launched immediately, you can open it as discussed next. Verifying the installation To check if Anaconda was installed correctly: On Windows : Click on the Start menu and search for Anaconda Navigator and press Enter . Or if you are on macOS or Linux : Open Terminal, type anaconda-navigator , and press Enter . It takes time to load. You should see the Anaconda GUI, as shown in Figure 2.14 , with options like Spyder, Jupyter Notebook, etc. You have now installed Anaconda correctly. Figure 2.14 : Anaconda Navigator When you install Anaconda, it comes with a default Python setup called the base environment , which is the starting workspace that includes core packages and tools. Alternatively, you can verify by opening Anaconda Prompt 5 (on Widows; startup menu | type anaconda prompt ) or Terminal (on macOS or Linux). Type conda&#160;--version and hit Enter . You should see something like conda 25.5.1 (or other similar number). You can also check Python version by running python --version , and it should return Python 3.X.X , refer to Figure 2.15 : Figure 2.15 : Anaconda Prompt Python IDEs and code editors An integrated development environment ( IDE ) is a powerful tool that helps you write, test, and debug your code all in one place. It comes with features like auto-suggestions, error highlighting, and debugging tools, making coding easier and more efficient. There are so many Python IDEs available. Spyder (which we recommend), Jupyter Notebook, Jupyter Lab, Visual Studio Code (or VS Code in short) and PyCharm are the popular ones. You have already seen that you can run your Python code without any installation using Google Colab. You can even write Python code in Notepad and run it from the command line. It is like using Notepad instead of Excel for data analysis. It works, but you would not want to do it. A text editor like Notepad or Notepad++ is a simpler and lightweight tool focused mainly on writing and editing code. But there is no fun coding like this. Code editors like Atom has been retired and Sublime Text has lost market share to other IDEs, both were once used by many Python developers. However, the best and easiest way to efficiently learn and work on Python projects is to use a full-fledged IDE. Let us see a few reasons behind recommending Spyder. Spyder IDE, the best choice for Excel users Scientific Python Development Environment ( Spyder ) https://www.spyder-ide.org/ is particularly suited for Excel users transitioning to Python due to its user-friendly interface and powerful features tailored for data analysis and scientific computing. Here is a comparison table for various features of popular Python IDEs: Feature Spyder (Recommended) Jupyter Notebook VS Code PyCharm Best for Data analysis, transition from Excel Data exploration, reports generation, tutorials General coding, advanced users Professional development, advanced users Ease of use Simple, user-friendly User-friendly, different style, uses markdown Moderate, more setup needed Moderate Installation requirement No (Pre-installed with Anaconda, no extra setup needed) No (Pre-installed with Anaconda, runs in a browser) Yes Yes (Bulky installation) Virtual environments Uses Anaconda, making it easier to manage dependencies Uses Anaconda, making it easier to manage dependencies Managed via terminal, can feel complex to new users Advanced support but can feel complex to new users Table 2.2 : Comparison of popular Python IDEs If you are already using another IDE like VS Code, PyCharm or cloud services like Colab, you can still follow along. If you are using cloud platforms like Colab, note that some features such as working with local files or Excel automation may be limited. Spyder IDE walkthrough Spyder comes pre-installed with Anaconda Distribution. If you have installed Anaconda, Spyder is ready to use, you just need to launch it. You can launch or open Spyder in any of the following ways: From Anaconda navigator : Open Anaconda Navigator (search for it in Start menu), Scroll to Spyder under Home , and click Launch . Directly from Start menu (Windows only) : Click on Start menu and type Spyder, click to open. Tip for faster access (Windows only): Next time, pin Spyder to the taskbar or Start menu for quick access! Understanding Spyder’s interface After launching Spyder for the first time, you can click on Start tour to get a glimpse of its interface, refer to Figure 2.16 : Figure 2.16 : Spyder IDE’s interface Spyder is your new Excel for Python . Here, instead of cells, formulas, and pivot tables, you will be working with code, variables, and plots. Do not worry, Spyder is designed to be user-friendly. We recommend exploring Spyder a bit before getting started with Python. Let us look at its interface: •&#9; Console (instant results are shown here): o&#9; It is the bottom-right panel, which you can imagine as the output area to display the results of your calculations/code. o&#9; Type 2+2 and hit Enter . You will see the result right there. •&#9; Editor (your Python Spreadsheet for writing code): o&#9; The large area at the top-left creates Python scripts (files) to keep your code saved for future use. o&#9; Code typed directly in the Console is lost when the session ends, so use a script to store and reuse your work. o&#9; Type this code in the script area: a&#160;=&#160;10 &#160;&#160; b&#160;=&#160;20 &#160;&#160; print(a&#160;+&#160;b)&#160;&#160; To run this code, select these lines and press F9 or click the Run Selection button on the toolbar. The result will appear in the Console. You can save the file 6 (use Ctrl + S Windows or Command ( ⌘ ) + S on Mac ) by giving it a name like example.py ( .py is the extension for Python files, just like .xlsx for Excel files). Please refer to the section titled Notebook versus Python script in this chapter. Later, you can reopen the .py file anytime, edit the code, and run it again just like opening and updating an Excel file when needed. Take some time to explore various options, such as creating new files, opening recent ones, editing (cut, copy, paste, and more), using find and replace, running the entire script ( F5 ) etc. Variable explorer (your data at a glance) One of Spyder’s best features for Excel users. It automatically shows your variables like an interactive Excel table. If you have run the earlier code, you will see variables like a and b here. Imported data (like from Excel) will also appear here. Manage your variables easily just like working with ranges in Excel. Think of it as Excel’s Name Manager or Power Query preview . Let us look at a quick data example. Run this code: import &#160;pandas&#160; as &#160;pd&#160;&#160; #&#160;Creating&#160;a&#160;simple&#160;table&#160;(DataFrame) data&#160;=&#160;pd.DataFrame({&quot;A&quot; :&#160;[1,&#160;2,&#160;3],&#160;&quot;B&quot;:&#160;[4,&#160;5,&#160;6]})&#160;&#160; #&#160;Show&#160;the&#160;DataFrame data&#160;&#160; Notice, the Variable Explorer shows your table just like Excel. You can double-click on data in the Variable Explorer to see it in table format. Refer to Figure 2.16 ). Plots (your Python chart tool) Any graph you create appears here. Try running this code: import matplotlib.pyplot as plt import &#160;seaborn&#160; as &#160;sns scores&#160;=&#160;[5,8,6,9,7,6 ,5,10,8,9,6,8,8,6,5,6 ] sns.histplot(scores,&#160;kde&#160;=&#160;True) plt.show() You got your first Python plot, which appears in the console and in plot window too. Help (your built-in function guide) Like Excel’s formula tooltips. You can browse documentations, Spyder shortcuts, tutorial videos etc. here. To make your experience in Spyder more comfortable, you can adjust settings like font size and theme (dark or light mode). Just go to Tools | Preferences | Appearance to customize how Spyder looks. Also, using keyboard shortcuts can save you time while coding. Go to Tools | Preferences | Keyboard Shortcuts inside Spyder. There, you can see all available shortcuts and even customize them if you want. Here are a few helpful ones (for Windows) to get started: Run a line or selection : F9 Run all : F5 Open variable explorer : Ctrl + Shift + V Cut : Ctrl + X Copy : Ctrl + C Paste : Ctrl + V Undo or Redo : Ctrl + Z or Ctrl + Y Find and replace: Ctrl + F Comment or uncomment lines: Ctrl + 1 Many Spyder shortcuts are the same on both Windows and Mac (especially F9 , F5 ), but for common actions like copy, paste, or undo, redo, just swap Ctrl with Command ( ⌘ ) on Mac. By now, you should be comfortable with Spyder. Spyder comes packed with features that make your transition from Excel seamless, e.g., you get auto-completion and help as you type, making coding easier and less prone to errors. Keep exploring and experimenting with simple codes. Assist yourself with the resources available at: https://docs.spyder-ide.org/current/videos/index.html Jupyter Notebook So far, you have been using Google Colab or Spyder to write and run Python code. However, there are other ways 7 too! If you need to create notebook but want to do it locally or do not have active internet connection, Jupyter notebook is what you need. You can open or launch Jupyter notebook by using any of the following options: Open Anaconda Navigator | Click Jupyter Notebook Open Anaconda Prompt (Windows) or Terminal (Mac) and type jupyter&#160;notebook This will also start the notebook server and open Jupyter in your browser, allowing you to create and work with notebooks easily. The interface is quite similar to Colab. Create a new notebook and explore its features. If you are curious, JupyterLab is a more advanced version of Jupyter Notebook with extra features like tabs, file browsing, and a cleaner layout, but the basic idea and usage are the same. Shutting down a Jupyter Notebook properly helps free up system resources and prevent unintended code execution. You can do this by going to File | Close and Shut Down Notebook or clicking Running | Shutdown from the Jupyter Notebook dashboard. To learn more, you can refer to the following: https://jupyterlab.readthedocs.io/en/stable/user/index.html Python packages Now that you have successfully installed Anaconda and explored the Spyder IDE, it is time to talk about packages. Python packages are like Excel Add-ins, providing extra functionality. They are free and more versatile. Python has some standard built-in packages (no installation needed) and many external packages (which you install separately to add more functionality). Installing any package can be done by running one line of code in the command line, Anaconda Prompt, Terminal, or your IDE like Spyder. With Anaconda, you already have most essential libraries pre-installed, such as pandas , numpy , matplotlib , seaborn , etc. To see the complete list of pre-installed packages, run conda list in Anaconda Prompt or !conda list in Spyder. Throughout this book, we will use many packages. Let us understand ways to install packages. Installing packages There are two ways to install Python packages: using conda or using pip . In Anaconda Prompt (Windows) or Terminal (Mac or Linux), you can run 8 conda install package name or pip install package name . To install a specific version, you can use = while using conda commands or == while using pip command: # Using conda conda&#160;install&#160;package_name&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Install&#160;latest&#160;version&#160;&#160; conda&#160;install&#160;package_name=x.y.z &#160;&#160; #&#160;Install&#160;specific&#160;version # Or using pip&#160;&#160; pip&#160;install&#160;package_name&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Install&#160;latest&#160;version&#160;&#160; pip&#160;install&#160;package_name==x.y.z &#160;&#160; #&#160;Install&#160;specific&#160;version&#160; &#160; If you are puzzled about conda vs. pip , it is best to use Conda when working with Anaconda and only use pip to install those packages that are not available via conda. The reason is conda handles dependencies better and ensures compatibility. Even if the details feel overwhelming to you, it is to be discussed for realizing the technical nuances. You can also install multiple packages in one go, for example: conda&#160;install&#160;numpy-financial&#160;xlrd&#160;xlwt&#160;xlutils&#160;pyxlsb&#160;xlsxriter Once you confirm the installations, this will install latest possible versions of these packages in base environment. Warning: If you install updated versions of any package in future, a few outputs may look slightly different. Usually, libraries maintain backward compatibility, but if that is not the case, your code might run into issues. If you are willing to use exact versions of the packages that are used in the book, you can create a separate virtual environment (to be discussed in the next section) and install specific versions of packages in that environment. Beginners often install packages as and when needed, but that is not the best way to work with packages while building real world projects. We would prefer to tell you the best way to do this to avoid conflicts and dependency issues later. Here is what they mean: Dependencies : When a package relies on other packages to function correctly. Version conflicts : Occur when different packages require different versions of the same dependency, causing potential issues. Tip: As a beginner, you can learn and practice most of the codes from this book by installing the packages you need as you go. If you are eager to start learning Python right away, you can skip the following section and move to the section Your first Python program and learn about virtual environment, when you are ready. If you have little more patience to get started with learning Python, we recommend learning about creating a virtual environment first. It will be the best investment of your time, saving you hours of effort debugging, searching through Stack Overflow, and managing package conflicts later on in Python journey. Roll up your sleeves to learn about creating a virtual environment. Setting up your first virtual environment Experienced Python users prefer to use separate virtual environments for their projects. If you learn about the same right from the beginning, you are going to derive a significant advantage. Understanding virtual environment and its importance A virtual environment in Python is like having separate workspaces for different projects. Imagine you work with different clients, and each client uses a different version of Excel and specific add-ins. If you try to use everything in the same Excel setup, things might break due to version conflicts. In Excel, you cannot easily keep separate setups for different projects. Python solves this pain with virtual environments, where each project can get its own isolated workspace, with its own packages and versions, so nothing interferes with anything else. Let us understand why is it helpful. When you follow Python code from different places, it might not work the same on your system because they may use different versions of libraries. Even if your own code works perfectly today, a library update tomorrow could break it if the update is not backward compatible. That is why managing Python environment is so important. A virtual environment helps you keep things organized by creating an isolated space where only the needed versions of Python and packages exist, as shown in Figure 2.17 . This way, one project’s setup would not mess up another’s, and you can easily switch between them when needed. Figure 2.17 : Virtual Environments There are different ways to create a virtual environment, using conda , using pip , or using Anaconda Navigator (GUI). We recommend creating a conda environment for this book. Step-by-step guide to creating Conda environment You can follow these steps to create, activate, or deactivate a conda environment. Step 1: Creating and activating an environment Open Anaconda Prompt (Windows user) or Terminal (Mac or Linux user) and type: conda&#160;create&#160;--name&#160;excel_python &#160; Python=3.11 This creates a clean workspace named excel_python with Python 3.11 (same as in this book). To activate it, run: conda&#160;activate&#160;excel_python Now, any packages you install will only affect this environment. Step 2: Installing required packages Instead of manually installing packages, use the environment.yml (download it from the companion repo) file for a smooth setup. Navigate to the folder where environment.yml is saved. Run this command to navigate that folder: cd path\to\folder Install all necessary libraries using (recommended): conda env update --name excel_python --file environment.yml –prune This ensures you get the exact versions used in the book. Alternative : The alternative is to directly create and install using .yml file If you have not created an environment yet , you can skip Step 1 and Step 2 and run just one command to create the environment and install all required packages in one go: conda env create --file environment.yml This will create a new environment (with the name specified inside environment.yml ) and i nstall all necessary packages. After installation , activate the environment : conda activate excel_python Now, you can perform Step 3, Verifying installed packages . Step 3: Verifying installed packages Check all installed packages with: conda&#160;list If everything looks good, you are all set! You can also see the path of your environment by running: where python This will show the location of the python.exe file for the currently active environment. This path will be used in Spyder to use the environment. If you ever need an exact snapshot of my setup, you can find the complete Conda environment export in conda_packages_full_export.yml (includes every dependency with exact versions). Deactivate, if needed If you close the terminal or Anaconda Prompt, the environment automatically deactivates. However, if you are running related commands or want to use another environment. The steps are as follows: You should deactivate the conda environment by running conda&#160;deactivate , and it will take you back to the base environment. If you want to delete the environment completely, run the following command: conda conda remove -n excel_python --all To see the list of all environments, you can run conda env list or conda info –envs . To work in this newly created environment in Spyder, you need to change Spyder settings as discussed in the following section. Using a conda environment in Spyder There are multiple ways to activate conda environment in Spyder. We would recommend following these steps: Open Spyder, go to Tools | Preferences | Python Interpreter . Select Use the following interpreter . Browse to path\to\anaconda3\envs\excel_python\python.exe (Here, you need to paste the full path you got from where python ). Apply and restart Spyder. You can verify this by checking the interpreter displayed at the bottom-right corner of the Spyder interface. By following these steps, you will have a well-managed Python environment, making it easier to follow along with this book and avoid any dependency conflicts. Happy coding, and let us continue this exciting journey into Python-powered Excel. Your first Python program Now that your Python setup is ready, let us get a feel for writing some real Python code. Do not worry, this is not about memorizing anything yet. Think of it like exploring a new Excel sheet for the first time: clicking around, trying formulas, and getting familiar. The steps are as follows: Open Spyder (or any other IDE you are using) and type this simple line to simply print a welcome message: print(&quot;Hello,&#160;Excel&#160;User!&quot;) Now, run the code and see the result appear, just like pressing Enter after writing a formula in Excel. Here, print() is a function that displays text on the screen. Notice the text inside the brackets is in quotes, this is how we tell Python we are working with words (called strings ). You can use single or double quotes; both work fine. Try a few more lines: #&#160;Some &#160;calculation print(10 &#160;+&#160; 20) score&#160;=&#160; 68 #&#160;Adding&#160;2 print(score&#160;+&#160;2) #&#160;print(20&#160;+&#160;30) &#160; #&#160;Nothing&#160;happens&#160;here&#160;because&#160;this&#160;line&#160;is&#160;commented&#160;out You may have noticed that some lines start with # . Also, notice that when you run the last line, nothing is printed. That is because lines beginning with # are comments , and Python completely ignores them when running the code. Comments are needed as they allow users to add notes to explain what the code does, just like leaving a comment on a tricky Excel formula to remind yourself (or others) why it was written that way. Comments are also useful for temporarily turning off lines of code without deleting them. Quick shortcut to comment or uncomment lines: Spyder: Ctrl + 1 (Windows) or Cmd + 1 (Mac) Colab or Jupyter: Ctrl + / (Windows) or Cmd + / (Mac) Quick tip on best practices We would recommend you to: Add spaces around operators like + or - to make your code easier to read. For example, print(10 + 20) is much clearer than print(10+20) . Add comments whenever something in your code might not be obvious later. There are plenty more good practices like these. For now, just focus on trying things out and exploring Spyder. Later, we will introduce you to PEP 8 , Python’s style guide, which is like a rulebook for writing clean, professional code. Conclusion In this chapter, you have learned different methods to run Python code. You have also learned to set up Python using Anaconda distribution and create a conda environment for isolating your projects. In the next chapter, you will learn the fundamentals of Python programming. If you already have some programming experience, you will see that the core of programming fundamentals remains similar, only syntax changes. Try it yourself Just for practice purposes, create a new conda environment named test_env with Python 3.12, install numpy latest version and pandas 2.2.1 on it, verify the installation, and delete the environment again. If needed, it will prompt you to confirm downgrading numpy, type y and press Enter to continue. You will realize it takes less than five minutes for all this if Anaconda is installed properly. For any newly created conda envionement, try to rename it, create a copy of the same and then delete those. Concept check Read and attempt to answer the following questions to check your understanding: What is Google Colab, and what are the benefits of using it? What are some benefits of using Anaconda instead of installing Python manually? What is the difference between an IDE and a code editor? Why is setting up a virtual environment important? What is the difference between .py file and .ipynb file? 1 Jupyter Notebook is similar but runs on your computer, using local Python installation. We will discuss it later. 2 To access files outside Google Drive’s &quot;Colab Notebooks&quot; folder or save outputs directly to Drive, open the file browser in Google Colab, click &quot;Mount Drive&quot;, and complete the authentication steps. 3 For a private GitHub repository, sign in to GitHub and authorize Colab to access private files. 4 https://www.markdownguide.org/basic-syntax/ 5 Anaconda Prompt is available only on Windows. macOS and Linux users should use the Terminal to run conda and Python commands. 6 When you create or save a Python file in Spyder, it is usually saved in C:\Users\&lt;YourUsername&gt; for Windows users or /Users/&lt;YourUsername&gt; for Mac users. We can change this location as needed. We will explain how in the next chapter when we talk about the working directory. 7 You can also run your Python code directly from Anaconda Prompt or Command Prompt (Windows), or Terminal (macOS/Linux). 8 Adding -y to conda install package_name automatically confirms the installation without prompting, but it is optional. In Spyder, prefixing a command with ! runs it as a terminal (bash) command. !conda install package -y may not always work as expected in Jupyter. It is recommended to install packages directly from Anaconda Prompt or Terminal for better reliability.
