  - Python-Powered Excel C HAPTER 9 Core Excel Read-Write Tools in Python Introduction After gaining a working knowledge of Python and libraries like pandas and openpyxl, you also need to learn another library, xlsxwriter, which offers a specialized, high-performance solution for writing .xlsx files with precision and control. Unlike general-purpose libraries that support both reading and writing, xlsxwriter is a write-only engine to generate Excel files from scratch. While pandas is great for data analysis, but has limitations when it comes to Excel-specific features like formatting, charts, and formulas. This chapter starts with diving deep into pandas I/O tools such as ExcelFile and ExcelWriter class, and then transitions into a detailed guide to xlsxwriter, a powerful library for creating and formatting Excel files programmatically. Additionally, we will explore pyxlsb for reading binary Excel files (.xlsb) and briefly touch on xlrd, xlwt, xlutils for legacy .xls file support. By the end of this chapter, readers will be able to generate Excel reports with rich formatting, automate file creation, and handle different file formats efficiently. Structure In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics: Popular libraries for Python Excel integration More on Pandas I/O tools Comprehensive guide to xlsxwriter Working with legacy .xls files Reading binary Excel files with pyxlsb Mini project: Dashboard automation Handling package switching Popular libraries for Python Excel integration So far, we have used Python’s built-in open() function to handle plain text files, the csv module to work with comma-separated values, and pandas to manage tabular data from both CSV and Excel. While pandas can import or export Excel files, its core strength is in analyzing data. For more advanced Excel operations, such as create, read, modify, insert Excel formulas, formatting, and charts, we turned to openpyxl . Many Python users stop there and never look beyond it. However, in practice, you may come across situations where openpyxl is not the best or only option. This is where other Excel-related packages come in. Python offers several other libraries that can be useful depending on the type of Excel file you are dealing with, the size of your data, or the level of formatting and automation required. The selection of a library for Python Excel integration depends on these factors: Compatibility with different Excel formats : Excel has been around for decades, and not all files follow the modern .xlsx format. Older files use the .xls format (pre-2007). These cannot be opened with openpyxl , but libraries like xlrd , xlutils , and xlw t were designed specifically for them. Some organizations generate or store binary Excel files ( . xlsb ), which are compact and fast to load in Excel but tricky for Python. For those cases, pyxlsb provides a reliable reader. Thus, if you are dealing with legacy systems, archives, or files created by third-party applications, you may need one of these specialized libraries. Advanced features and special use cases : Some packages are built with special features in mind. For example, xlsxwriter offers highly polished control over formatting, charts, and layout. If you are preparing Excel reports for clients or management, its output can look more professional than what you might achieve with openpyxl . Also, for writing large amounts of data efficiently, xlsxwriter often outperforms openpyxl , especially when generating complex reports with formatting and charts. pandas can act as a bridge, allowing you to read and write Excel files seamlessly during data analysis. While you may not control the file format in detail, it is the quickest way to move data between Excel and Python. Filling the gaps : You might have realized that no single library is perfect for all Excel tasks. While there are many libraries designed for Excel-Python integration, becoming familiar with the most widely used ones can significantly enhance your workflow. Some of these are: o &#9; pandas for seamless data import or export, efficient data analysis or quick plotting. o &#9; openpyxl for reading, writing, and editing .xlsx , but not .xls or . xlsb . o &#9; xlrd for reading legacy .xls files. o &#9; xlwt for writing legacy .xls files o &#9; pyxlsb for fast reading of binary .xlsb workbooks. o &#9; xlsxwriter for creating . xlsx files with rich formatting and charts. Another important library is xlwings , which connects Python directly with a running Excel application. Unlike the libraries discussed here, xlwings is designed for automation and interactive use, rather than just reading or writing files. We will explore it in detail in subsequent chapters. By understanding the strengths of each tool, you can select the right one depending on the type of file you encounter and the result you want to achieve. Before we explore these new libraries, let us reinforce our understanding of pandas reader and writer functions, building on the concepts introduced in the previous chapter. More on Pandas I/O tools We had discussed reading Excel files using pandas in Chapter 6 , Data Manipulation with Pandas . We had explored arguments like index_col , sheet_name , na_values , header , skip_rows , use_cols , etc., and you might have explored a few more arguments as per your requirement. Here, we will explore the engine parameter of read_excel() and to_excel() functions, the ExcelFile class, and the ExcelWriter class. The engine parameter While reading and writing Excel files using pandas ’ read_excel() , you might have noticed there is a parameter engine 1 , which can be any of these: openpyxl , calamine , odf , pyxlsb , xlrd with a default None . By default, pandas determine the engine based on this logic: engine=odf is used for OpenDocument format ( .odf, .ods, .odt ). engine=xlrd is used for xls format file. engine=pyxlsb is used for xlsb format file. So, based on the type of Excel file ( .xls , .xlsb , .xlsx , etc.) you are trying to read, pandas automatically decides a suitable engine. Otherwise, you can choose an appropriate engine for your file type as shown in Figure 9.1 : Figure 9.1 : Engine parameter of read_excel() Even though calamine supports many formats, it is not the default as it is newer and still evolving, while openpyxl and xlrd have been around longer and are more widely used and tested. Another point is that calamine is not a pure Python library but a wrapper around a Rust library ( rust-calamine ), which adds complexity for some users. Similarly, pandas ’ writer function df.to_excel() allows you to choose the engine parameter. You can use openpyxl or xlsxwriter for .xlsx files and xlwt for legacy support for .xls files. You can also set the default engine globally via: pd.options.io.excel.xlsx.writer = &apos;xlsxwriter&apos; For simple .xlsx exports, the engine usually does not matter. However, if you need advanced features like appending to an existing file or adding charts and formatting, you must use ExcelWriter , and then the choice of engine becomes important. The ExcelFile class We also need to show you an alternative way to read Excel files that pandas offers. ExcelFile is a class (in pandas ) that lets you load an Excel file once and then parse multiple sheets efficiently. So, if you are reading multiple sheets from the same file, ExcelFile avoids re-parsing the file each time: import &#160;pandas&#160; as &#160;pd file&#160;=&#160;pd.ExcelFile(&quot;demo_sales.xlsx&quot;) #&#160;Read&#160;multiple&#160;sheets&#160;without&#160;reloading&#160;the&#160;file df1&#160;=&#160;file.parse(&quot;Customers&quot;) #&#160;Or using sheet index (0-based) df1&#160;=&#160;file.parse(0)&#160; #&#160;first&#160;sheet df2&#160;=&#160;file.parse(1) #&#160;second&#160;sheet df3&#160;=&#160;file.parse(2) #&#160;third&#160;sheet To retrieve a list of all sheet names in the workbook, you can use xls.sheet_names . The following code builds a dictionary where keys are sheet names, and values are the corresponding DataFrames: #&#160;Loop&#160;through&#160;all&#160;sheets dfs &#160;=&#160;{sheet:&#160;xls.parse(sheet)&#160;for&#160;sheet&#160;in&#160;xls.sheet_names} dfs.get(&apos;Sales_Jan&apos; ) This is more efficient than calling pd.read_excel() repeatedly. You can also use ExcelFile class as a context manager: #&#160;Using&#160;context&#160;manager with &#160;pd.ExcelFile( &quot;demo_sales.xlsx&quot;)&#160;as &#160;file: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;df1&#160;=&#160;file.parse( &quot;Customers&quot;)&#160; #&#160;with&#160;sheet&#160;name &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;df2&#160;=&#160;file.parse( 1)&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;or&#160;with&#160;sheet&#160;index &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;df3&#160;=&#160;file.parse( 2) Method preference shaped by intended outcome We have discussed different ways to read multiple Excel sheets; each method has its strengths depending on your use case. Here is a breakdown to help you choose the best one for your scenario: Use pd.read_excel(sheet_name=[...]) when you know exactly which sheets you want and prefer a concise, one-liner approach. Best for quick reads of 2 or 3 sheets. Use ExcelFile.parse() in a loop when you need to load all sheets dynamically , especially if the sheet names or count may vary. Ideal for automation and scalable workflows such as: with &#160;pd.ExcelFile( &quot;demo_sales.xlsx&quot;)&#160; as &#160;file: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;all_dfs&#160;=&#160;[] &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; for &#160;sheet&#160; in &#160;file.sheet_names: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; if &#160;sheet.startswith( &apos;Sales&apos;):&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;only&#160;sales&#160;related&#160;sheets &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;df&#160;=&#160;file.parse(sheet_name=sheet) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;df[ &quot;sheet&quot;]&#160;=&#160;sheet&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;all_dfs.append(df) combined&#160;=&#160;pd.concat(all_dfs,&#160;ignore_index=True) Use a with pd.ExcelFile(...) context manager when writing production-grade scripts where resource management matters. It ensures the file is properly closed after reading. This method is efficient for selective reading Use ExcelFile.parse( ) manually for each sheet when you want fine-grained control over how each sheet is read. For example, applying different parsing options per sheet. The to_excel() method Usually, we use the to_excel() method to write Excel files with a suitable engine type such as openpyxl or xlsxwriter (default). You can also specify sheet_name , start_row , start_col , and other arguments as per requirement. A simple example is as follows: import &#160;pandas&#160; as &#160;pd #&#160;Sample&#160;DataFrame df&#160;=&#160;pd.DataFrame({ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Flight&quot;:&#160;[&quot;AI101&quot;,&#160;&quot;BA202&quot; ,&#160;&quot;LH303&quot;], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Passengers&quot;:&#160;[180,&#160;220,&#160;150] }) #&#160;Write&#160;DataFrame&#160;to&#160;a&#160;custom&#160;sheet&#160;name df.to_excel(&quot;flights.xlsx&quot;,&#160;sheet_name=&quot;Aviation_Report&quot;,&#160;index=False ) By default, pandas writes to a sheet called Sheet1 . The sheet_name argument lets you name the sheet something meaningful. You can start writing in a custom row or column: #&#160;Zero-indexed&#160;in&#160;pandas,&#160;so &#160;Excel&#160;shows&#160;it&#160;as&#160;row&#160;6,&#160;col&#160;C df.to_excel(&quot;flights_offset.xlsx&quot;,&#160;sheet_name=&quot;Flights&quot;,&#160; &#9;&#9;startrow=5,&#160;startcol=2,&#160;index=False) By default, to_excel() writes a single DataFrame onto a sheet. If you want to place multiple DataFrames in the same sheet at different positions, or distribute them across multiple sheets, you need to create an ExcelWriter object. This allows you to specify the target file and control the placement of each DataFrame within it. The ExcelWriter class As discussed, you need to use ExcelWriter class explicitly when you need to write data to multiple sheets, place DataFrames at specific cell ranges within the same sheet, append new data, or apply formatting and styles (with engines like xlsxwriter or openpyxl ). The writer should be used as a context manager. Otherwise, call close() to save and close any opened file handles. You can find the full documentation on pandas &apos; official site https://pandas.pydata.org/docs/reference/api/pandas.DataFrame.to_excel.html . Refer to the following: flights&#160;=&#160;pd.DataFrame({ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Flight&quot;:&#160;[&quot;AI101&quot;,&#160;&quot;BA202&quot;,&#160;&quot;LH303&quot;], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Passengers&quot;:&#160;[180,&#160;220,&#160;150 ] }) cargo&#160;=&#160;pd.DataFrame({ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Flight&quot;:&#160;[&quot;AI101&quot;,&#160;&quot;BA202&quot;,&#160;&quot;LH303&quot;], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Cargo_Tons&quot;:&#160;[20,&#160;35,&#160;15 ] }) #&#160;Use&#160;ExcelWriter&#160;to&#160;control&#160;placement with &#160;pd.ExcelWriter( &quot;aviation_report.xlsx&quot;,&#160;engine=&quot;openpyxl&quot;)&#160;as &#160;writer: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;flights.to_excel(writer,&#160;sheet_name= &quot;Summary&quot;,&#160;startrow=1,&#160;startcol=0,&#160;index=False) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;cargo.to_excel(writer,&#160;sheet_name= &quot;Summary&quot;,&#160;startrow=1,&#160;startcol=4,&#160;index=False) The output file is shown in the following figure: Figure 9.2 : Two DataFrames at specific cell ranges within the same sheet If you received more passenger data that you want to append to the flight data in Excel: new_flights&#160;=&#160;pd.DataFrame({ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Flight&quot;:&#160;[&quot;UA404&quot; ,&#160;&quot;EK505&quot;], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &quot;Passengers&quot;:&#160;[190,&#160;210] }) #&#160;Append&#160;data&#160;to&#160;existing&#160;sheet&#160;without&#160;writing&#160;column&#160;headers&#160;again with &#160;pd.ExcelWriter( &quot;aviation_report.xlsx&quot;,&#160;engine=&quot;openpyxl&quot;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;mode= &quot;a&quot; ,&#160;if_sheet_exists=&quot;overlay&quot;)&#160;as &#160;writer: &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;new_flights.to_excel(writer,&#160;sheet_name= &quot;Summary&quot;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;startrow= 5,&#160;startcol=0,&#160;index=False,&#160;header=False) The resultant Excel file is shown in Figure 9.3 . This cannot be done with xlsxwriter , because that engine only creates new files. In the next section, we will take a closer look at what xlsxwriter has to offer. Figure 9.3 : Appending rows to existing data using openpyxl We have seen that pandas allows integration with some reader or writer package through its engine parameter. And in the previous chapter, we have explored the capabilities of openpyxl in detail. In this chapter, we will discuss a few more commonly used reader and writer tools, highlighting their features and typical use cases. Comprehensive guide to xlsxwriter Library xlsxwriter truly excels in its ability to produce complex, well-formatted workbooks that mirror professional Excel reports. It supports advanced features such as: Rich formatting (cell styles, conditional formatting, merged cells) Charts, images, and data validation Worksheet and cell protection Memory-efficient writing of large datasets and much more Unlike openpyxl , which reads and modifies existing Excel files, or pandas , which focuses on tabular data export, xlsxwriter is ideal when the goal is to generate polished Excel output programmatically , especially for reporting, dashboards, or automated exports. In summary, xlsxwriter is not a replacement for data analysis tools but a powerful complement for those who need to deliver Excel files that look and behave like they were crafted manually. Creating Excel files and sheets Let us re-create the same product_data.xlsx we had created earlier using openpyxl . We will name this file toy_data.xlsx to avoid confusion: import &#160; xlsxwriter wb &#160;=&#160;xlsxwriter.Workbook( &apos;toy_data.xlsx&apos;) ws&#160;=&#160;wb.add_worksheet(&apos;products&apos;) header_format&#160;=&#160;wb.add_format({&apos;bold&apos;:&#160;True,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;bg_color&apos;:&#160;&apos;#DCE6F1&apos;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;font_color&apos;:&#160;&apos;#223A54&apos; }) headers&#160;=&#160;[&apos;product_id&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_name&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_category&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_price&apos;] for &#160;col_num,&#160;header&#160;in&#160;enumerate(headers): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;ws.write( 0,&#160;col_num,&#160;header,&#160;header_format) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; wb.close() Here, we have iterated through a list of headers and used write(row,&#160;col,&#160;data, cell_format) method to write data and apply formatting to it. It writes each cell individually, giving you fine-grained control over formatting per column or cell. Finally, we have used close() method, which is a required call to close and write the Excel file, unless you are using the&#160;with&#160;context manager. We can also write an entire row in one go, applying the same format to all cells with: ws.write_row(row=0,&#160;col=0,&#160;data=headers,&#160;cell_format=header_format) Note: Contrasting openpyxl , which lets you modify existing Excel files and apply formatting after writing, in xlsxwriter , all data and formatting must be applied at the time of writing data, as it does not support editing or reading existing workbooks. Unlike openpyxl , xlsxwriter doesn&apos;t have a separate save() method. The filename is specified when creating the workbook, and close() is what finalizes and writes the file. Another point to note is xlsxwriter uses are zero-based indices for cell navigation, so row (or column) 0 means the first row (or column) in Excel. While, openpyxl uses one based indices for its cell API and also supports A1 notation You can also write data to individual columns using write_column() , perfect for inserting new variables in data. Sample code is as follows: column_data&#160;=&#160;[ &apos;A001&apos;,&#160;&apos;A002&apos;,&#160;&apos;A003&apos;,&#160;&apos;A004&apos;] ws.write_column(&apos;E2&apos;,&#160;column_data)&#160;&#160; #&#160;Writes&#160;down&#160;from&#160;cell&#160;E2 You can add more sheets and set tab color: analytics_ws&#160;=&#160;wb.add_worksheet(&apos;Analytics_Report&apos;) analytics_ws.set_tab_color(&apos;#1072BA&apos;) However, xlsxwriter does not support operations like sheet reordering, copying or deleting sheets, and inserting rows or columns. Worksheets are created strictly in the order they are added. This limitation exists because xlsxwriter is a write-only library, designed solely for generating new Excel files, not for reading or modifying existing ones. Writing data and applying formatting We will create a .xlsx file and populate data from a nested list in the products sheet: import &#160;xlsxwriter wb&#160;=&#160;xlsxwriter.Workbook(&apos;toy_data.xlsx&apos;) ws&#160;=&#160;wb.add_worksheet(&apos;products&apos;) header_format&#160;=&#160;wb.add_format({&apos;bold&apos;:&#160;True,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;bg_color&apos;:&#160;&apos;#DCE6F1&apos;,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;font_color&apos;:&#160;&apos;#223A54&apos;}) headers&#160;=&#160;[&apos;product_id&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_name&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_category&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_price&apos;] ws.write_row(0,&#160;0,&#160;headers,&#160;header_format) #&#160;Sample&#160;data&#160;(nested&#160;list) data&#160;=&#160;[ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 101,&#160;&apos;Wireless&#160;Mouse&apos;,&#160;&apos;Electronics&apos;,&#160;25.99], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 102 ,&#160;&apos;Yoga&#160;Mat&apos;,&#160;&apos;Fitness&apos;,&#160;15.50], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 103,&#160;&apos;Notebook&apos;,&#160;&apos;Stationery&apos;,&#160;3.99], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 104,&#160;&apos;Pen&apos; ,&#160;&apos;Stationery&apos;,&#160;10.00], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 105,&#160;&apos;USB&#160;Cable&apos;,&#160;&apos;Electronics&apos;,&#160;150.00], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 106,&#160;&apos;Chair&apos;,&#160;&apos;Furniture&apos; ,&#160;45.50], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 107,&#160;&apos;Water&#160;Bottle&apos;,&#160;&apos;Fitness&apos;,&#160;10.00] ] #&#160;Writing&#160;data&#160;row-wise&#160;from&#160;row&#160;1&#160;and&#160;col&#160;0&#160;(in&#160;excel&#160;2nd&#160;row&#160;and&#160;1st&#160;col) for &#160;row_num,&#160;row_data&#160; in &#160; enumerate (data,&#160;start=1): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;ws.write_row(row&#160;=&#160;row_num,&#160;col&#160;=&#160; 0,&#160;data&#160;=&#160;row_data) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Adding&#160;a&#160;new&#160;data/variable units_sold&#160;=&#160;[5,&#160;8,&#160;15,&#160;30,&#160;6,&#160; 10,&#160;20] ws.write(0,&#160;len(headers),&#160;&apos;Units&#160;Sold&apos;,&#160;header_format)&#160; #&#160;header&#160; #&#160;Writing&#160;data&#160;column-wise&#160;(starting&#160;row=1&#160;since&#160;row=0&#160;is&#160;header) ws.write_column(1,&#160;len(headers),&#160;units_sold) ws.set_column( &apos;A:E&apos;,&#160;15) &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; wb.close()&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; We have used write_row() to add data horizontally (row-wise) and write_col() to add data vertically (column-wise). Both methods automatically adjust to the length of the data provided. There is no need to manually specify the number of columns or rows; the methods handle it dynamically. We have also adjusted the column width. The output Excel file is shown in the following figure: Figure 9.4 : Excel file created by xlsxwriter As xlsxwriter is write-only, every time we add a new column, sheet, or a feature (formatting, charts, validation), we recreate the workbook. To avoid repeating code, from now onwards, we will be providing the code that can be added to existing code, and it will overwrite the same file. If you want to avoid replacing files, you can give a suitable file name while exploring new features (as done in the chapter code scripts). Modern Excel formula support Unlike openpyxl , which lets you pass a formula string directly to ws.write() or cell.value , xlsxwriter treats formulas as a distinct type and requires its dedicated method, ws.write_formula() . Let us add a column revenue based on price and units sold, using ws.write_formula(row, col, formula) method: #&#160;Add&#160;&quot;Revenue&quot;&#160;header ws.write(0,&#160;len(headers)&#160;+&#160;1,&#160; &apos;Revenue&apos;,&#160;header_format) #&#160;Write&#160;formula&#160;row-wise for &#160;row_num&#160; in &#160; range(1,&#160;len(data)&#160;+&#160;1): &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;price_cell&#160;=&#160; f&apos;D{row_num&#160;+&#160;1 }&apos; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Column&#160;D&#160;=&#160;product_price &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;units_cell&#160;=&#160; f&apos;E{row_num&#160;+&#160;1}&apos; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; #&#160;Column&#160;E&#160;=&#160;Units&#160;Sold &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;formula&#160;=&#160; f&apos;={price_cell}*{units_cell}&apos; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;ws.write_formula(row_num,&#160; len (headers)&#160;+&#160;1,&#160;formula)&#160;&#160;&#160; After adding the preceding code to the existing code, we get this Excel: Figure 9.5 : Adding Excel formula using xlsxwriter Note that Excel stores formulas in the format of the US English version, so all formula function names written using xlsxwriter must be in English: ws.write_formula(&apos;E9&apos;,&#160;&apos;{=SUM(E2:E8)}&apos;)&#160; #&#160;Works ws.write_formula(&apos;E9&apos;,&#160;&apos;{=SOMME(E2:E8)}&apos; )&#160; #&#160;French _ #NAME Error&#160;on&#160;load It computes the sum of cells E2 up to E8 and writes the result in E9, as shown in the following figure: Figure 9.6 : Formula names must be in English Using new functions Unlike openpyxl , xlsxwriter does not require you to write the _xlfn . prefix for newer Excel functions such as IFS , TEXTJOIN , or LET . These formulas will work as expected in xlsxwriter , provided that the version of Excel used to open the file supports them: ws.write_formula(&apos;I2&apos;,&#160;&apos;=TEXTJOIN(&quot;:&#160;&quot;,&#160;TRUE,&#160;B2:C2)&apos;) ws.write_formula(&apos;H2&apos;,&#160;&apos;=IFS (F2&gt;500,&#160;&quot;High&quot;,&#160;F2&gt;300,&#160;&quot;Medium&quot;,&#160;F2&gt;0,&#160;&quot;Low&quot;)&apos;) The resultant Excel file is shown in the following figure: Figure 9.7 : Adding new Excel formulas and using use_future_functions=True option Sometimes, these newer functions, such as IFS , TEXTJOIN may initially show #NAME? . This is not an error. Simply double-click the cell and hit Enter , and Excel will compute it correctly. Otherwise, you can simply enable the use_future_functions=True option in the Workbook() constructor, and xlsxwriter will handle the prefixing automatically: wb &#160;=&#160;xlsxwriter.Workbook( &apos;filename.xlsx&apos;,&#160;{&apos;use_future_functions&apos;:&#160;True}) Working with dynamic array formulas You can also use dynamic array functions, introduced in Excel 365. Dynamic arrays are ranges of return values that can change in size based on the results. The following code shows leveraging modern Excel features like SORT , FILTER , or UNIQUE that spill results across cells automatically, using write_dynamic_array_formula() method: ws.write_dynamic_array_formula(&apos;A11:A15&apos;,&#160;&apos;=UNIQUE(C2:C8)&apos;) ws.write_dynamic_array_formula(&apos;B11:B15&apos;,&#160;&apos;=SORT(B2:B8)&apos;) ws.write_dynamic_array_formula( &apos;D11:D20&apos;,&#160;&apos;=FILTER(B2:D8,&#160;D2:D8&#160;&gt;&#160;20)&apos;) After adding the heading, the resultant Excel is as follows (These formulas might not work in older Excel versions): Figure 9.8 : Dynamic array formulas (Excel 365) using xlsxwriter Implementing Lambda expressions If you are someone who loves the powerful new feature of Excel 365 called LAMBDA() . You might recall the lambda function in Python that was discussed in Chapter 3 , Python Fundamentals . For calculating revenue, we can write a lambda in Python: Revenue &#160;=&#160;lambda&#160;price,&#160;units:&#160;price&#160;*&#160;units In ws.write_formula() method, we can use Excel’s LAMBDA() function, and parameters in the LAMBDA() function must have a _xlpm prefix for compatibility with how the formulas are stored in Excel. These prefixes would not show up in the formula: ws.write_formula(&apos;K2&apos;,&#160;&apos;=LAMBDA(_xlpm.price,&#160;_xlpm.units,&#160;_xlpm.price&#160;*&#160;_xlpm.units)(D2,&#160;E2)&apos;) The following figure shows implementation of lambda function (in Excel 365) to compute revenue: Figure 9.9 : Adding Lambda functions You might have noticed that Excel displays formula without the prefix. Referencing Excel table Another powerful feature of xlsxwriter is that you can add worksheet tables with xlsxwriter using the add_table() method. By default, tables are named as Table1, Table2, and so on, in the order that they are added. However, it can also be explicitly set by the user using the name parameter, which is useful when: You want to reference the table by name in formulas or VBA. You are generating multiple tables and need predictable identifiers. You are integrating with other tools or workflows that expect specific table names. Figure 9.10 shows Excel tables created by xlsxwriter : Figure 9.10 : Adding Excel tables using xlsxwriter Charts and images Library xlsxwriter allows you to create highly customizable charts such as column, bar, line, pie, scatter, area, radar, stock, sparklines, and more. It provides you with more fine-grained control over every chart element, such as titles, axes, gridlines, data labels, legends, colors, styles, and formatting series. You can embed charts directly into worksheets at specific positions with pixel offset, add multiple series with full formatting, secondary axes, and even trendlines. Let us replicate the simple charts (column chart and combo chart) that we had created using openpyxl : #&#160;Create&#160;a&#160;column&#160;chart chart&#160;=&#160;wb.add_chart({&apos;type&apos;:&#160;&apos;column&apos;}) #&#160;Configure&#160;the&#160;series chart.add_series({ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;name&apos;:&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&apos;Units&#160;Sold&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;categories&apos;:&#160;f&apos;=products!$B$2:$B${len(data)+ 1}&apos;,&#160;&#160; #&#160;Product&#160;Names &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;values&apos;:&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;f&apos;=products!$E$2:$E${len(data)+1}&apos;,&#160;&#160; #&#160;Units&#160;Sold &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;fill&apos;:&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;{ &apos;color&apos;:&#160;&apos;#4F81BD&apos;}, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;border&apos;:&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;{&apos;color&apos;:&#160;&apos;#1F497D&apos;}, &apos;trendline&apos;: {&apos;type&apos;: &apos;linear&apos;, &apos;display_equation&apos;: True, &apos;line&apos;: {&apos;color&apos;: &apos;#C00000&apos;, &apos;width&apos;: 2.25, &apos;dash_type&apos;: &apos;long_dash&apos;}} }) #&#160;Add&#160;chart&#160;title&#160;and&#160;axis&#160;labels chart.set_title({ &apos;name&apos;:&#160;&apos;Units&#160;Sold&#160;by&#160;Products&apos;}) chart.set_x_axis({&apos;name&apos;:&#160;&apos;Units&#160;Sold&apos;}) chart.set_y_axis({&apos;name&apos;:&#160;&apos;Product&#160;Name&apos;}) #&#160;We can also set&#160;chart&#160;style chart.set_style(11) #&#160;Insert&#160;the&#160;chart&#160;into&#160;the&#160;worksheet ws.insert_chart( &apos;A10&apos;,&#160;chart,&#160;{&apos;x_offset&apos;:&#160;25,&#160;&apos;y_offset&apos;:&#160;10})&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; # Another chart combo_chart&#160;=&#160;wb.add_chart({&apos;type&apos;:&#160;&apos;column&apos;}) #&#160;Revenue&#160;series combo_chart.add_series({ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;name&apos;:&#160;&apos;Price&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;categories&apos;:&#160;f&apos;=products!$B$2:$B${len(data)+1}&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;values&apos;:&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;f&apos;=products!$D$2:$D$ {len(data)+1}&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;fill&apos;:&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;{&apos;color&apos;:&#160;&apos;#4472C4&apos;} }) #&#160;Units&#160;Sold&#160;series&#160; combo_chart.add_series({ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;name&apos;:&#160; &apos;Units&#160;Sold&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;categories&apos;:&#160;f&apos;=products!$B$2:$B${len(data)+1}&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; ‘values’:&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;f’=products!$E$2:$E${len(data)+ 1}’, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; ‘fill’:&#160;{‘color’:&#160;‘#ED7D31’} }) combo_chart.set_title({&apos;name&apos;:&#160;&apos;Price&#160;vs&#160;Units&#160;Sold&apos;}) combo_chart.set_x_axis({&apos;name&apos;:&#160; &apos;Product&apos;}) combo_chart.set_y_axis({&apos;name&apos;:&#160;&apos;values&apos;}) ws.insert_chart(&apos;J1&apos;,&#160;combo_chart) You might have noticed that we have added a nicely formatted trendline to the first chart. The resultant Excel file is shown in Figure 9.11 . You can also play with the y-axis of the combo chart by slightly changing the code. These charts are embedded as Excel objects; you can also edit them directly in Excel. Figure 9.11 : Basic charts created by xlsxwriter We recommend that you create other useful charts and play with the chart elements to make them publication-ready. As discussed previously, matplotlib or pandas charts can be imported as a static image, so they might not be very useful. You can insert any image (in PNG, JPEG, GIF, BMP, WMF or EMF format) and set its position or size by using worksheet.insert_image(row, col, image, options) . You can even embed images into a worksheet cell and have the image automatically scale to the width and height of the cell by using embed_image(row,&#160;col,&#160;filename[,&#160;options]) . The X/Y scaling of the image is preserved, but the size of the image is adjusted to fit the largest possible width or height, depending on the cell dimensions. This is the equivalent of Excel’s menu option to insert an image using the option to Place in Cell , which is only available in Excel 365 versions from 2023 onwards. For older versions of Excel, a #VALUE! error is displayed. Working with VBA macros Macros are a common way to automate tasks in Excel using Visual Basic for Applications ( VBA ) . A macro-enabled Excel file ( .xlsm ) is the same as a xlsx file except that it contains an additional vbaProject.bin file, which contains macros. Using xlsxwriter , you can embed an existing macro project into a new workbook. Note that you cannot create or edit VBA code, but you can take an existing compiled macro file ( vbaProject.bin ) and insert it into your .xlsm output. To interact with macros (write, edit, or run VBA) programmatically, you will either need to use COM automation (Windows)/AppleScript. (MacOS) or you can use Python’s xlwings library, which lets you control a live Excel session, write and call VBA macros, or even replace VBA with Python functions. In the chapter folder, we have given one demo_macro.xlsm and one vbaProject.bin . You can press Alt+F11 to open the VBA editor, and it will show you a module with the VBA code: Sub &#160;HelloWorld() &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;MsgBox&#160; &quot;Hello&#160;from&#160;VBA&#160;Macro!&quot; End &#160; Sub To attach the extracted macro to a new file named macro_enabled.xlsm , you can run this code: import &#160;xlsxwriter workbook&#160;=&#160;xlsxwriter.Workbook(&apos;macro_enabled.xlsm&apos;) #&#160;Attach&#160;an&#160;existing&#160;VBA&#160;project&#160;(compiled&#160;as&#160;vbaProject.bin) workbook.add_vba_project(&apos;vbaProject.bin&apos;) ws&#160;=&#160;workbook.add_worksheet(&apos;demo&apos;) #&#160;Write&#160;some&#160;text ws.write(&quot;A1&quot;,&#160; &quot;This&#160;is&#160;generated&#160;by&#160;Python!&quot;) ws.write(&quot;A3&quot;,&#160;&quot;Run&#160;the&#160;macro&#160;&apos;HelloWorld&apos;&#160;from&#160;Developer&#160;&gt;&#160;Macros.&quot;) workbook.close() The add_vba_project() method can be used to add macros or functions to a workbook using a binary VBA project file that has been extracted from an existing Excel xlsm file. Only one vbaProject.bin file can be added per workbook. The name does not have to be vbaProject.bin. Any suitable path or name for an existing VBA bin file will do. You can check macro_enabled.xlsm file, which has text written in cells A1 and A3. Pressing Alt+F11 will show you a module with the same VBA code. If you run it from the VBA editor (shortcut F5 ) or from a worksheet control, a modal message box pops up in Excel showing the text Hello from VBA Macro! (Refer to the following figure): Figure 9.12 : Inserting and running an existing macro to a new xlsm file If you are curious about how to create a compiled macro file (such as vbaProject.bin ) for your macro-enabled Excel file. You can: Rename the .xlsm file to a .zip file. Navigate the xl folder in the folder structure and find vbaProject.bin file. Copy the xl/vbaProject.bin file. Simply drag it out or copy it to your working directory. Use it in your code with xlsxwriter . Protecting sheets and cells If you are generating reports where users should view but not edit certain cells, you can protect worksheets, lock specific cells, and hide formulas or values using xlsxwriter . The protect() method is used to protect a worksheet from modification, which locks all cells by default. A locked cell cannot be edited; you must explicitly unlock cells that might need edits. A hidden cell will display the results of a formula but not the formula itself. The following code protects the products worksheet, locks the header row, and locks and hides formulas in the Revenue column. Meanwhile, it keeps all data cells unlocked, allowing users to edit them freely: import &#160;xlsxwriter wb&#160;=&#160;xlsxwriter.Workbook(&apos;protected.xlsx&apos;) ws&#160;=&#160;wb.add_worksheet(&apos;products&apos;) header_format&#160;=&#160;wb.add_format({&apos;bold&apos;:&#160;True,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;bg_color&apos;:&#160;&apos;#DCE6F1&apos; ,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;font_color&apos;:&#160;&apos;#223A54&apos;, &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; &apos;locked&apos;:&#160;True}) headers&#160;=&#160;[&apos;product_id&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_name&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_category&apos;,&#160;&apos;product_price&apos; ] ws.write_row(0,&#160;0,&#160;headers,&#160;header_format) locked_format&#160;=&#160;wb.add_format({&apos;locked&apos;:&#160;True}) unlocked_format&#160;=&#160;wb.add_format({&apos;locked&apos;:&#160;False}) hidden_formula_format&#160;=&#160;wb.add_format({&apos;locked&apos;:&#160;True ,&#160;&apos;hidden&apos;:&#160;True}) data&#160;=&#160;[ &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 101,&#160;&apos;Wireless&#160;Mouse&apos;,&#160;&apos;Electronics&apos;,&#160;25.99], &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;[ 102,&#160;&apos;Yoga&#160;Mat&apos; ,&#1

  - Python-Powered Excel C HAPTER 4 External Packages and Date-time Handling Introduction As you are well-equipped with Python fundamentals, now, it is the right time to dive into using external packages, which are essential for enhancing Python&apos;s functionality. In Chapter 2 , Setting Up Python , we have discussed ways to install any package you might need. This chapter presents various methods to import and utilize modules, based on your requirements. We will also cover date and time handling in Python (using libraries like datetime and dateutil), which is crucial for Excel users analyzing datasets with columns such as application dates, order dates, delivery dates, and visit times. By the end of this chapter, you will be equipped with the knowledge to leverage external packages, unlock new capabilities, and comfortably do date-time arithmetic in Python. Structure In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics: Modules and packages Methods for importing modules or packages Foundation of dates and times in Python Creating dates and times values Working with dates and time values Modules and packages In Excel, you might have seen or used add-ins or macros for additional functionality. Similarly, in Python, we use modules and packages 1 for specific tasks. These are basically collections of ready-made tools created by other people, so we can conveniently use their functions without needing to code everything from scratch. For example, if you want to interact with your operating system , such as creating, deleting, renaming, or moving folders or files, joining, splitting, or checking file paths, you can use the os module. To work with dates and times , perform date arithmetic, manage calendars, and handle time zones, you can use the datetime module. While it is not essential to comprehend the taxonomy of terms modules, packages and libraries, it is useful to know how to use these. Still, if you are curious about the difference between the three names which you come across commonly in Python books, let me simplify it for you: Module is simply a Python script ( .py file) that contains variables, functions, or classes (templates for creating things like dates) designed to be imported and used in other files. Package is basically a collection of related modules that work together to provide specific functionality. A&#160; library &#160;is a broader term that can include multiple individual modules that can provide a wide range of functionality. Python users use these names interchangeably but now, you understand that module is a single Python file while package is a folder of modules. The following figure shows the structure of package 1, which contains two modules and a sub package along with a _ _ init_ _.py file 2 . Figure 4.1 : Modules and packages In the second chapter, we have discussed that Anaconda gives you many pre-installed packages and you can install other packages using pip or conda . Once installed, the package is available to use but you need to import it in your session. There are various ways to import a module or a package. Let us understand these in the next section. Methods for importing modules or packages In Python, you can use import keyword to import modules. You might have seen codes like import datetime , import datetime as dt , or from datetime import datetime at different places and might be wondering what is the difference. It would not be a nuisance anymore, you will understand the use cases of various methods to import modules and packages. Basic import You can import a module by running impor t&#160;module_name and to call a function from this module, you need to use module_name.method_name(argument) . For example: import &#160;math #&#160;Calling&#160;different&#160;methods&#160;from&#160;&apos;math&apos;&#160;module math.sqrt(16) # 4.0 math.log(10)&#160; #&#160;2.302585092994046 math.sin(2 ) # &#160; 0.9092974268256817 You have used this method in the previous chapter to set and check your current working directory using os module. This method imports the entire module, making all functions and classes available through the module name allowing us to easily identify where each function and class comes from. However, we will need to type module_name.method_name everytime, which is a bit more effort. Note: This method is recommended in many cases f or small libraries but for large modular libraries, selective imports are the norm and better practice as discussed next. Import specific parts If you do not want to type the module_name every time you use a function, you can import specific functions or classes that you intend to use in your code, such as from&#160;module_name&#160;import&#160;method_name . This method is helpful when you need to use only a few things from the module as it requires less typing and avoid importing entire module. For example: from &#160;math&#160; import &#160;sqrt,&#160;log #&#160;Calling&#160;method&#160;names&#160;directly sqrt(16) # 4.0 log(10) #&#160;2.302585092994046 For large libraries like sklearn or tensorflow , importing the whole module is impractical as they are massive and organized into submodules, so selectively importing only what you need is the standard and accepted practice. Wildcard import Python also allows you to import everything (all the functions, classes and values) from a module by using from&#160;module_name&#160;import&#160;* However, it is often considered as a bad practice because it can create name conflicts. Generally, you should avoid using this method unless you are sure there will not be any conflicts. from &#160;math&#160; import &#160;* sqrt(16) # 4.0 log(10) #&#160;2.302585092994046 Just like you avoid using SELECT ALL in a large Excel sheet without knowing what is in every column, avoid using wildcard imports unless you are 100% sure of what is being imported, and there is no risk of conflicts. Importing a module with an alias This is a commonly used method with some of the popular libraries to shorten its name for convenience or by convention. For example, importing the popular library numpy as np : import &#160;numpy&#160; as &#160;np array_1d&#160;=&#160;np.array([1,&#160;2,&#160;3,&#160;4,&#160;5]) print(&quot;1D&#160;Array:&quot;,&#160;array_1d)&#9; # 1D&#160;Array:&#160;[1&#160;2&#160;3&#160;4&#160;5] Or importing the mighty pandas library as pd : import &#160;pandas&#160; as &#160;pd&#160;&#160; data&#160;=&#160;pd.DataFrame({“A”:&#160;[1,&#160; 2,&#160;3],&#160;“B”:&#160;[4,&#160;5,&#160;6]})&#160; Quick summary Here is the guideline for selecting the appropriate import method: Use import package when you want clarity, and you are using multiple parts of a package (safest and most common). Use from package import item when you only need specific parts and want shorter code. Use import package as alias when the package name is long and commonly abbreviated (like import pandas as pd ). Avoid from package import * as it is unclear, risky, and rarely needed outside of quick experiments. Foundation of dates and times in Python If you have worked with dates and times in Excel, you know how important it is to convert text to dates, format dates, calculate differences between dates, and work with time zones. Python offers powerful capabilities for these and similar tasks using specific packages. Understanding date and datetime in python is essential for tasks such as aging calculations, trend analysis, sorting data by date, calculating durations, grouping data by month or year and time-based comparisons. In Python, datetime module provides classes for working with date and time values. The core classes of datetime module are: The date class : It works with calendar (Gregorian calendar) dates with attributes year, month and day. The time class : It represents hours, minutes, seconds, microseconds, and optional time zone information independent of a date. The datetime class : It combines both date and time. The timedelta class : It represents durations or differences between date or datetime instances for arithmetic operations. The timezone class : It manages time zone data. Creating dates and time values Let us start using the datetime package by importing it. The datetime library provides three classes that make up the high-level interface that most people will use: datetime.date (for dates), datetime.time (for times), and datetime.datetime (for date and time combination). Displaying current date and time We can use today() function from date class of datetime module to display current local date. It displays the date in year-month-day format: from &#160;datetime&#160; import &#160;date&#160; current_date&#160;=&#160;date.today() print(current_date)&#160; #&#160;2025-03-05 (it will print current date) Else, we can use now() function from the datetime class of the same module to capture exact moment. It returns the current local date and time, including hours, minutes, seconds, and microseconds: from &#160;datetime&#160; import &#160;datetime current_datetime&#160;=&#160;datetime.now() print(current_datetime) # 2025-03-05 12:17:20.503186 # (it will print current date and time) The exact output of these functions will vary depending on when you execute the code, the examples shown here reflect the results at the time of my execution. You can access individual components such as year, month, day or hours, minutes, seconds, etc. by using dot notation. year&#160;=&#160;current_datetime.year month&#160;=&#160;current_datetime.month day&#160;=&#160;current_datetime.day current_hour&#160;=&#160;current_datetime.hour current_minute&#160;=&#160;current_datetime.minute current_second&#160;=&#160;current_datetime.second current_microsecond&#160;=&#160;current_datetime.microsecond Creating date-time objects You can create specific date objects by supplying the year, month, and day in the datetime.date(year, month, day) format. You need to ensure that months and days are valid numbers (e.g., there is no month 13 or day 35). For single-digit days or months, do not use 0 at the beginning. The great thing is, it supports a wide range of years from 1 to 9999 , as opposed to Excel, where you need a workaround for dates prior to 1/1/1900. from &#160;datetime&#160; import &#160;date #&#160;Creating&#160;a&#160;date&#160;object&#160;for&#160;October&#160;30,&#160;2025 specific_date&#160;=&#160;date(2025,&#160;10,&#160; 30) print(specific_date) # 2025-10-30 (Human-readable format) Warning: When creating dates with Python’s date() function, always use the full 4-digit year. Unlike Excel, Python does not assume 25 means 2025. Instead, it will literally use year 25 AD. Try it out yourself with some experiments. #&#160;Using&#160;two-digit&#160;years&#160;is&#160;discouraged&#160;to&#160;prevent&#160;confusion incorrect_date&#160;=&#160;date(25,&#160;10,&#160;30)&#160;&#160; print(incorrect_date) # Output: 0025-10-30 print(incorrect_date.year) # Output: 25 You can also create a time object for applications such as scheduling events or setting a notification at a specific time, where the date is irrelevant by importing the time class. from&#160;datetime&#160;import&#160;time specific_time&#160;=&#160;time(13 ,&#160;45,&#160;5,&#160;23) print(specific_time) For creating a combined date-time instance, you can use the datetime class. Here, you need to supply valid integers as arguments. Time-related arguments are optional: from &#160;datetime&#160; import &#160;datetime dt&#160;=&#160;datetime(year=2025,&#160; month=3,&#160;day=31,&#160; &#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160; hour=13,&#160;minute=14,&#160;second=31 ) print(dt)&#9; # 2025-03-31 13:14:31 Working with date and time values While working with data and creating reports, many times we need to either format dates as text in desired format or convert dates written as text into date or datetime object for further processing. We also need to add or subtract time periods or manage time zones. All of these can be done smoothly in Python. Let us discuss basics of how these tasks can be done, which will lay the groundwork for analysing date-based datasets using pandas library (to be introduced in Chapter 6 , Data Manipulation with Pandas . Formatting date-time as strings If you need to convert dates to nicely formatted strings for use in reports, filenames, or to display user-friendly date information, you can use the strftime() function from the datetime library. Using str() will also help convert it to a string as is, but strftime() is powerful because you can get any format of date or datetime you want. The strftime() function takes one parameter, format , to specify the format of the returned string. To display the date/datetime in a specific format, you need to use format codes ( refer to Table 4.1 ), such as %d/%m/%Y for day/month number/four-digit year . Let us see it in practice. my_date&#160;=&#160;datetime(2025,&#160;10,&#160;30,&#160;4,&#160; 30) print(my_date) # 2025-10-30&#160;04:30:00 print(type(my_date)) # &lt;class&#160;&apos;datetime.datetime&apos;&gt; short_date&#160;=&#160;my_date.strftime(&quot;%d/%m/%Y&quot;) print(short_date)&#160; # 30/10/2025 print(type (short_date)) # &lt;class&#160;&apos;str&apos;&gt; long_date&#160;=&#160;my_date.strftime(&quot;%A,&#160;%B&#160;%d,&#160;%Y&quot;) print(long_date) # Thursday,&#160;October&#160;30,&#160;2025 When using strftime() , you must provide a string containing special directives/format codes that define the formatting. Some of those are mentioned here: Description Format code Sample value Year (as four-digit integer) %Y 2025 Year (as short-digit integer) %y 47 (for year 1947) Month (as 0-padded decimal) %m 08 (for August) Month Name (Abbreviated) %b Aug Month Name (Full Version) %B August Date (as 0-padded decimal) %d 03 Hour (as 0-padded decimal with 24-hours clock) %H 14 Minute (as 0-padded decimal) %M 50 Second (as 0-padded decimal) %S 35 Table 4.1 : Format codes for date-time formatting For more format codes, you can refer to https://strftime.org/ or https://docs.Python.org/3/library/datetime.html#strftime-and-strptime-format-codes. Converting strings to date or date-time objects While working with datasets, you will often need to convert text such as application dates, birthdates, time of signing an application, etc. into datetime objects . This allows you to validate dates or, even more importantly, perform date and time arithmetic, such as finding the difference between dates or adding days to a given date. Converting strings to datetime objects is simplified using the strptime() function from the datetime module. The function datetime.strptime(string,&#160;format) takes : A string (the one you want to convert) and A format code (that specifies the format of this string) And returns a datetime object from the given string only if the string is in a certain format. If the string (first argument) and the format code (second argument) passed to strptime() do not match, you will get a ValueError . In the following example, date string is in day-month-year format while the format we are supplying in strptime() is month-day-year , so we get an error. date_string&#160;=&#160;&quot;31-01-2020&#160;14:45:37&quot; format_string&#160;=&#160;&quot;%m-%d-%Y &#160; %H :%M:%S&quot; datetime.strptime(date_string,&#160;format_string) # ValueError To fix this, we need to change format_string accordingly, and it will successfully parse the string into a datetime object: format_string&#160;=&#160;&quot;%d-%m-%Y &#160; %H :%M:%S&quot; datetime.strptime(date_string,&#160;format_string)&#160; #&#160;2020-01-31&#160;14:45:37 Difference between strftime() and strptime() You might have noticed that strptime() does the reverse of strftime() , though they conveniently use the same format codes. And the function names are self-explanatory, in strptime() , p denotes parse , while in strftime() , f denotes format . date_string&#160;=&#160;&quot;27&#160;Mar,&#160;2025&quot; print(date_string) # 27&#160;Mar,&#160;2025 print(type(date_string)) # &lt;class&#160;&apos;str&apos;&gt; date_object&#160;=&#160;datetime.strptime(date_string,&#160; &quot;%d&#160;%b,&#160;%Y&quot;) print(date_object) # 2025-03-27&#160;00:00:00 print(type(date_object)) # &lt;class&#160;&apos;datetime.datetime&apos;&gt; #&#160;Converting&#160;datetime&#160;object&#160;to&#160;a&#160;string&#160;again&#160;(in&#160;a&#160;new&#160;format) datetime.strftime(date_object,&#160;&quot;%d/%m/%y&quot; )&#160; #&#160;&apos;27/03/25&apos; Date-time arithmetic Adding/subtracting days or time components can be done with timedelta() from datetime module easily. from &#160;datetime&#160; import &#160;date,&#160; datetime, timedelta #&#160;Add or subtract&#160;days today = date.today() next_week&#160;=&#160;today&#160;+&#160;timedelta(days=7) yesterday&#160;=&#160;today&#160;-&#160;timedelta(days=1 ) print(f&apos;Today:&#160;{today},&#160;Yesterday:&#160;{yesterday},&#160;Next_week:&#160;{next_week}&apos;) # Today:&#160;2025-03-05,&#160;Yesterday:&#160;2025-03-04,&#160;Next_week:&#160;2025-03-12 # Add or subtract time components now&#160;=&#160;datetime.now() after3hours&#160;=&#160;now&#160;+&#160;timedelta(hours&#160;=&#160;3) print(now.strftime( &apos;%H:&#160;%M:&#160;%S&apos;)) # 04:&#160;44:&#160;18 print(after3hours.strftime(&apos;%H:&#160;%M:&#160;%S&apos;)) # 07:&#160;44:&#160;18 print(after3hours&#160;-&#160;now) # 3:00:00 For adding/subtracting months or years, we can use dateutil.relativedelta from dateutils library as timedelta does not handle months and years. from &#160;dateutil.relativedelta&#160; import &#160;relativedelta next_month&#160;=&#160;today&#160;+&#160;relativedelta(months=1) last_year&#160;=&#160;today&#160;-&#160;relativedelta(years=1) print(f&apos;Today:&#160;{today},&#160;Next&#160;Month:&#160;{next_month},&#160;Last&#160;Year:&#160;{last_year}&apos;) # Today:&#160;2025-03-06,&#160;Next&#160;Month:&#160;2025-04-06,&#160;Last&#160;Year:&#160;2024-03-06 As compared to Excel&apos;s EOMONTH, relativedelta can shift by years, months, and days simultaneously, snap to month-end, and handle leap years, all in a single expression. To find the difference between date or datetime instances, you can simply use the subtraction operator: start &#160;=&#160; date(2024,&#160;3,&#160;1) end &#160;=&#160; date(2025,&#160;6 ,&#160;15) difference&#160;=&#160;end &#160;-&#160; start print(&quot;Difference&#160;in&#160;Days:&quot;,&#160;difference.days) # Difference in Days: 471 For a human-readable breakdown of differences like 1 year, 3 months, and 14 days, you can use relativedelta , which is much more convenient than Excel’s DATEDIF function. If you need to change a specific part of a date or a date-time object, for example, setting all times to midnight or adjusting dates for reporting, you can use the replace() method. A few examples are shown here: #&#160;Create&#160;a&#160;date&#160;object d&#160;=&#160;date(2025,&#160;3 ,&#160;15) print(&quot;Original&#160;date:&quot;,&#160;d)&#160; #&#160;Original&#160;date:&#160;2025-03-15 #&#160;Replace&#160;year&#160;and&#160;month d_replaced&#160;=&#160;d.replace(year=2026,&#160;month=12)&#160; #&#160;Replaced&#160;date:&#160;2026-12-15 print(&quot;Replaced&#160;date:&quot;,&#160;d_replaced) #&#160;Create&#160;a&#160;time&#160;object t&#160;=&#160;time(14,&#160;30,&#160;45) print(&quot;Original&#160;time:&quot;,&#160;t)&#160; #&#160;Original&#160;time:&#160;14:30:45 #&#160;Replace&#160;hour&#160;and&#160;minute t_replaced&#160;=&#160;t.replace(hour=9 ,&#160;minute=0) print(&quot;Replaced&#160;time:&quot;,&#160;t_replaced)&#160; #&#160;Replaced&#160;time:&#160;09:00:45 #&#160;Create&#160;a&#160;datetime&#160;object dt&#160;=&#160;datetime(2025,&#160;3,&#160;15,&#160;14,&#160;30 ,&#160;45) print(&quot;Original&#160;datetime:&quot;,&#160;dt)&#160; #&#160;Original&#160;datetime:&#160;2025-03-15&#160;14:30:45 #&#160;Replace&#160;day&#160;and&#160;hour dt_replaced&#160;=&#160;dt.replace(day=20,&#160;hour=8) print(&quot;Replaced&#160;datetime:&quot;,&#160;dt_replaced)&#160; #&#160;Replaced&#160;datetime:&#160;2025-03-20&#160;08:30:45 Time zones manipulations Excel lacks support for time zones, which is a significant limitation for global business data involving multiple regions, time-sensitive transactions, or cross-timezone scheduling. Let us discuss what Python offers for dealing with time zones. So far, we have created and printed date-time objects that do not include information about the time zone; we call them naive objects. They contain date and time components, yet do not include time zone context. Naive datetimes are fine for simple calculations. When working with global data, scheduling, reports, or logs where time zones matter, we need aware datetime objects. Aware datetimes mean objects that include time zone information. To create time zone-aware objects, you use the time zone class from the datetime module. from &#160;datetime&#160; import &#160;datetime,&#160;timezone naive_time&#160;=&#160;datetime.now() print( f&apos;Naive&#160;Time:&#160;{naive_time},&#160;TimeZone:&#160;{naive_time.tzname()}&apos;) # Naive&#160;Time:&#160;2025-03-06&#160;08:37:36.510722,&#160;TimeZone:&#160;None aware_time&#160;=&#160;datetime.now(tz&#160;=&#160;timezone.utc) # in UTC timezone print(f&apos;Aware&#160;Time:&#160;{aware_time},&#160;TimeZone:&#160;{aware_time.tzname()}&apos;) # Aware&#160;Time:&#160;2025-03-06&#160;03:07:39.108157+00:00,&#160;TimeZone:&#160;UTC In datetime.now() , the tz argument specifies the timezone in which the current date and time should be returned. By passing timezone.utc , the result is an aware datetime object representing the current time in Coordinated Universal Time ( UTC ). With timezone class from datetime module, you can get time zone info and handle fixed offsets such as UTC-5:30, etc. However, for more realistic time zone manipulations such as Asia/Kolkata to America/New_York , you need libraries like dateutil , arrows or pytz . As you are familiar with dateutil , let us see how to handle time zones with same. Later, you can explore arrows or pytz if the need arises. To create naive and aware current datetime instances using dateutil library: from &#160;dateutil&#160; import &#160;tz from &#160;datetime&#160; import &#160;datetime naive_time&#160;=&#160;datetime.now() print(f&apos;Naive&#160;Time:&#160;{naive_time},&#160;TimeZone:&#160;{naive_time.tzname()}&apos;) # Naive&#160;Time:&#160;2025-03-06&#160;0.5:55:25.512249,&#160;TimeZone:&#160;None aware_time&#160; =&#160;datetime.now(tz=tz.tzlocal())) # in Local timezone print(f&apos;Aware&#160;Time:&#160; {aware_time},&#160;TimeZone:&#160;{aware_time.tzname()}&apos;) # Aware Time:&#160;2025-03-06&#160;05:55:40.571098+05:30,&#160;TimeZone:&#160;India&#160;Standard&#160;Time Here, tz.tzlocal() sets time zone to your machine&apos;s local 3 timezone (for the author, it is India Standard Time). To create the date-time aware object, you can use datetime class by providing time zone info in tzinfo argument. Refer to https://timezonedb.com/time-zones for names of all time zones: local_time&#160;=&#160;datetime(2025,&#160; 3,&#160;4,&#160;15,&#160;0,&#160;0,&#160;tzinfo&#160;=&#160;tz.tzlocal()) utc_time&#160;=&#160;datetime(2025,&#160;3,&#160;4,&#160;15,&#160;0,&#160; 0,&#160;tzinfo&#160;=&#160;tz.UTC) #&#160;Australia&#160;time&#160;(Sydney) aus_tz&#160;=&#160;tz.gettz(&quot;Australia/Sydney&quot;) aus_time&#160;=&#160;datetime(2025,&#160;3,&#160;4,&#160;15,&#160;0,&#160;0,&#160;tzinfo&#160;=&#160;aus_tz) print(local_time) # 2025-03-04 15:00:00+05:30 print(utc_time) # 2025-03-04 15:00:00+00:00 print(aus_time) # 2025-03-04 15:00:00+11:00 For converting time zones, you can use dateutil module’s astimezone() function as: local_time&#160;=&#160;datetime(2025,&#160;3,&#160;4,&#160;10,&#160;0,&#160;0,&#160;tzinfo=&#160;tz.tzlocal()) print(&quot;Local&#160;Time:&quot;,&#160;local_time)&#160; #&#160;Local&#160;Time:&#160;2025-03-04&#160;10:00:00+05:30 #&#160;Convert&#160;to&#160;America/New_York&#160;time ny_zone&#160;=&#160;tz.gettz(&quot;America/New_York&quot;) ny_time&#160;=&#160;local_time.astimezone(ny_zone) print(&quot;New&#160;York&#160;Time:&quot;,&#160;ny_time)&#160; #&#160;New&#160;York&#160;Time:&#160;2025-03-03&#160;23:30:00-05:00 You have got a great understanding of dealing with date and time objects. If you work with time series data frequently, pandas provides suitable features for computing time-based aggregations (for example, upsample from monthly to daily frequency or downsample from monthly to quarterly frequency), rolling or moving averages, and much more. Such pandas functions with suitable charts are discussed in the Appendix, Python Charts for Business Analysis . Now, it is time to practice what you have learned so far. Get your hands dirty with the practice exercise to test your learning from the chapter. Conclusion In this chapter, you have learned different methods to import a module or a package. You have also learned to use datetime and dateutil module to display or create date-time instances, perform arithmetic’s on such objects, formatting date-times as per your requirement and time zone manipulation. In the next chapter, you will learn to use other modules manage files conveniently. Learning file operations using Python will enable you to organize, back-up and automate your file handling tasks. Try it yourself The following practice exercises will help you reinforce the learning from this chapter: You are processing employee joining dates. One person joined on 12th August 2023 at 9:30 AM. Create a date-time object for this. Your boss wants the joining date from Exercise 1 formatted as 12-Aug-2023 09:30 AM for the report. Format the joining date accordingly. You have imported some data from Excel where the application date is stored as the string &quot;04/03/2025 14:45&quot; (DD/MM/YYYY HH:MM). Convert this string into a Python datetime object. A customer signed up on 1st March 2025. Calculate in Python to answer: When is their 30-day free trial ending? How many days have passed by 4th March 2025? Your company operates in India (Asia/Kolkata), but your manager in New York (America/New_York) wants to know what time a meeting scheduled at 4 PM IST on 4th March 2025 will be in their time zone. Convert the time accordingly. You need to schedule a report to be sent at 9 AM local time in Sydney, Australia on 10th March 2025. You also need to know what time that will be in UTC. Create a datetime for Sydney time and convert it to UTC. Concept check Answer the following questions to check your understanding: What is the difference between strftime() and strptime()? What is the difference between naive and aware datetime objects? What are the different ways to import a package or a module in Python? When would you use which method?   - Python-Powered Excel C HAPTER 1 Python for Excel Users Introduction In this chapter, we will discuss the need for automation in Excel workflows and explain why Python is a powerful tool for enhancing productivity. We will explore Python’s advantages over traditional Excel methods (VBA, Power Query) and set the foundation for the rest of the book. Also, Python analysts are often expected to deliver results within Excel; they will learn about dedicated tools to achieve this directly in Python. In this chapter, you will also run a ready-made Python project to see what Python-powered Excel automation can do, without writing or understanding any code. This will give you a preview of what you will learn later. We will guide readers on how to approach the book, whether you are new to Python or already have some experience with it. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: Excel users, analysts, and their workflow Excel’s limitations and the need for automation Benefits of learning Python for Excel Python and Excel versions used in this book Delivering value from this book Roadmap for different readers Excel users, analysts, and their workflow During training and consulting work, we meet many Excel or Python professionals who share their struggles. To streamline their process, we need to understand their current workflow. Let us look at the stories inspired by real-world scenarios commonly faced by a few representative professionals and analysts. Common roles and challenges Alex is a sales professional. He relies heavily on Excel to compile sales data such as products, prices, discounts, revenue, etc., to calculate key performance indicators ( KPIs ) for sales, and generate dynamic reports for his manager every month. The process is repetitive, time-consuming, and prone to manual errors. Maya , a content creator, runs a successful YouTube channel. To grow her audience strategically, she analyzes engagement metrics, publication time, duration, views, watch time, subscribers, impressions, and interactions. Every month, she downloads and organizes such datasets in Excel, manually applying formulas and creating charts to identify trends. Chris is a financial analyst responsible for evaluating financial performance across various sectors, including stock markets, investment portfolios, and corporate finance. His job involves handling extensive datasets, generating insights, and preparing reports, often repeating the same steps. Although they are working in totally different domains, they all use the same tool for their work, that is Excel, and share the same challenge of repeating manual tasks. Excel’s limitations will be examined in the next section. For now, let us meet Joy, who is highly proficient in Python. He is a data professional who transforms raw business data into clear insights with Python, but his company still wants reports to be delivered in Excel. Every week, he spends hours copy-pasting results, inserting charts and tables, and adding formats that match his brand template manually in Excel. All of them feel that as workloads grow, valuable time is consumed by repetitive and mundane tasks that steal focus from interpreting data for better decisions. Python transformed workflow efficiency Maya realized that automation could save her hours of tedious work each month. She decided to explore Python for Excel automation. She significantly streamlined the process by writing a bit of code to automate data fetching, analysis, and report generation to simplify her workflow. Let us take a glimpse at a slice of her workflow in action. Her Python script is available on GitHub. (Do not be concerned if it looks overwhelming; you will learn everything step by step in this book). Click on Run anyway as shown in the following figure: Figure 1.3 : Colab’s authorization warning In seconds, a useful report named YouTube_Report_upto_June is generated and downloaded that combines monthly data, calculates KPIs, and even includes charts in separate sheets. This was an oversimplified example for demo purposes; she can take this automation even further. For example, using Python, she can: Directly connect to YouTube’s application programming interface ( API ) for fetching fresh data automatically. Create and manage folders for raw data, charts, and reports. Create dynamic, interactive Excel charts and dashboards. Format and enhance reports programmatically, adjust row heights, apply styles, add formulas, even protect cells or sheets. Just like Maya, both Alex and Chris can benefit from automation. Instead of spending hours on repetitive tasks, they could focus on making data-driven decisions and improving their business strategies. Likewise, Joy can also rebuild the entire workflow in Python. Instead of exporting results to Excel and manually arranging everything, he can write Excel sheets, generate charts, place everything exactly where he needs it, and apply rich formatting as needed using Python. He can even deploy Excel workbooks for his managers, who can get the entire analysis with just one Excel click without needing any Python knowledge. In later chapters, you will learn how to: Create and manage folders for raw data, charts, and reports.
